summary
     Biomedical research has long sought to understand the mechanisms underlying cell-fate reprogramming, the process by which one cell type is converted into another. A recent study in the nematode worm Caenorhabditis elegans (C. elegans) has shed new light on this process, revealing an unexpected role for the Notch signaling pathway in promoting reprogramming of germ cells into somatic cells.

     In C. elegans, the Notch signaling pathway, mediated by the GLP-1 receptor, is known to maintain the undifferentiated state of germline stem cells and progenitors. However, this study found that increased GLP-1/Notch signaling actually facilitates the reprogramming of germ cells into somatic cells, a process that requires chromatin perturbation.

     Through a combination of genetic, tissue-specific transcriptome analysis, and functional studies of candidate genes, the researchers identified a possible explanation for this unexpected role of GLP-1/Notch. They found that GLP-1/Notch signaling activates specific genes that are silenced by the Polycomb repressive complex 2 (PRC2), a chromatin regulator that maintains gene silencing.

     The study also identified the conserved histone demethylase UTX-1 as a crucial GLP-1/Notch target that facilitates reprogramming. UTX-1 is a key regulator of gene expression, and its activation by GLP-1/Notch signaling is essential for the reprogramming process.

     Notably, the researchers found that GLP-1/Notch and PRC2 have an antagonistic effect on germ cell-fate decisions, with GLP-1/Notch promoting reprogramming and PRC2 maintaining the undifferentiated state of germline stem cells and progenitors. This antagonistic relationship is supported by the observation that many genes activated by GLP-1/Notch signaling are also repressed by PRC2.

     The findings of this study have significant implications for our understanding of development and disease. Abnormal Notch signaling has been implicated in various diseases, including cancer and neurodegenerative disorders. The discovery of a reprogramming-promoting role for GLP-1/Notch signaling in C. elegans provides new insights into the mechanisms underlying these diseases and may lead to the development of novel therapeutic strategies.
summary
     Biomedical research has identified the adaptor protein SKAP2 as a crucial component in the defense against Klebsiella pneumoniae (K. pneumoniae) infections. K. pneumoniae is a pathogen that affects the respiratory, blood, liver, and bladder systems and poses a significant clinical concern. Studies have shown that mice lacking SKAP2 (Skap2-/-) have a 100-fold higher bacterial burden compared to wild-type mice when infected with K. pneumoniae (ATCC 43816). SKAP2 expression in innate immune cells, such as neutrophils and monocytes, is essential for controlling the bacterial burden. Neutrophils from Skap2-/- mice are present in infected lungs and degranulate normally in response to K. pneumoniae infection. However, the production of reactive oxygen species (ROS) in response to K. pneumoniae is abolished in Skap2-/- neutrophils.

     Further investigation revealed that K. pneumoniae-induced ROS production in neutrophils requires the activity of several key proteins, including Src family kinases (SFKs), Syk, Btk, PLCÎ³2, and PKC. The loss of SKAP2 significantly hinders the phosphorylation of SFKs, Syk, and Pyk2, indicating that SKAP2 is proximal to their activation in pathogen-signaling pathways. This suggests that SKAP2-dependent signaling in neutrophils is essential for K. pneumoniae-activated ROS production and promoting bacterial clearance during infection.

     The sensitivity of K. pneumoniae to ROS is supported by increased bacterial burden in mice lacking the gp91phox component of NADPH oxidase (Cybb-/-) and observations in chronic granulomatous disease (CGD) patients. SKAP2 is required for full phosphorylation of SFKs, Syk, and Pyk2 in murine neutrophils, demonstrating a critical role in K. pneumoniae-induced signaling pathways required for ROS production. These findings indicate that SKAP2-dependent signaling in neutrophils is essential for host defense against K. pneumoniae infections.
Biomedical researchers have developed a mathematical model to understand the role of the hypnozoite reservoir in the transmission of Plasmodium vivax malaria. The hypnozoite reservoir is a group of latent liver-stage parasites that can reactivate weeks to months after the initial infection, causing new blood-stage infections and contributing to the spread of the disease.

The researchers created a within-host model of liver-stage hypnozoites, which was validated against data from tropical strains of P. vivax. This model demonstrated that the hypnozoite reservoir is over-dispersed, meaning that many individuals have few or no hypnozoites, while some have intensely infected livers. The model also showed that individuals with more hypnozoites are more likely to experience relapses and contribute to the transmission of P. vivax.

The researchers then embedded the within-host model into a P. vivax transmission model to demonstrate the build-up of the hypnozoite reservoir following new infections and its depletion through hypnozoite activation and death. The model predicted that incorporating hypnozoite-killing drugs, such as primaquine, into first-line treatment regimens would cause substantial reductions in P. vivax transmission. This is because individuals with the most hypnozoites are more likely to relapse and be targeted for treatment.

Relapse infections caused by the activation of hypnozoites in the liver have significant consequences for the transmission dynamics of P. vivax. In the absence of effective diagnostics for detecting liver-stage parasites, models like the one developed by the researchers will play a crucial role in estimating and predicting the effectiveness of interventions that target the hypnozoite reservoir.

Most existing models of malaria transmission do not account for the additional reservoir of parasites in the liver. However, the hypnozoite reservoir has been incorporated into some P. vivax transmission models as a state to denote hypnozoite infection or as up to two broods of hypnozoites. Mathematical models of malaria transmission that account for P. vivax relapses can provide valuable insights into the impact of malaria control interventions on the parasite's reservoirs in the vector, the blood, and the liver.

The researchers' within-host model of hypnozoites in liver hepatocytes demonstrated that many of the epidemiological patterns of relapse infections can be explained by making the assumption that hypnozoites activate and die at a constant rate. This model was integrated into the existing theory of Ross-Macdonald models to account for the relapse infections characteristic of P. vivax malaria.

     summary
Biomedical researchers have long used neurosphere formation as a marker for neural stem cell (NSC) function. However, the relationship between neurosphere-initiating cells (NICs) and NSCs has remained unclear. A recent study aimed to prospectively identify and isolate adult mouse lateral ventricle subventricular zone (SVZ) NICs. Using flow cytometry, the researchers identified a specific cell population, referred to as GlastmidEGFRhighPlexinB2highCD24â/lowO4/PSA-NCAMâ/lowTer119/CD45â (GEPCOT) cells. These cells were found to be highly mitotic and short-lived in vivo, based on fate-mapping with Ascl1CreERT2 and Dlx1CreERT2.

In contrast, pre-GEPCOT cells were quiescent, expressed higher levels of Glast, and lower levels of EGFR and PlexinB2. Pre-GEPCOT cells were unable to form neurospheres but expressed stem cell markers such as Slc1a3-CreERT, GFAP-CreERT2, Sox2CreERT2, and Gli1CreERT2, and were long-lived in the SVZ. The researchers found that GEPCOT NICs were highly enriched for multipotent NICs and were highly mitotically active in vivo, but persisted only transiently in the SVZ. In contrast, pre-GEPCOT cells were unable to form neurospheres and were long-lived in the SVZ.

Conditional deletion of the Bmi-1 polycomb protein depleted both pre-GEPCOT and GEPCOT cells, although pre-GEPCOT cells were more dependent on Bmi-1 for Cdkn2a (p16Ink4a) repression. The study's findings distinguish quiescent NSCs from NICs and make it possible to study their properties in vivo. The researchers identified two phenotypically and functionally distinct populations of neural stem/progenitor cells from the adult mouse SVZ. GEPCOT cells were highly enriched for NICs and were highly mitotically active in vivo, while pre-GEPCOT cells were unable to form neurospheres but expressed stem cell markers and were long-lived in the SVZ.
Summary
     Biomedical researchers have investigated the properties of Piezo1 ion channels, which are responsible for sensing forces in various cell types and tissues. Using cryo-electron microscopy, the structure of Piezo1 was found to be bowl-shaped and capable of inducing membrane curvature. This suggests that Piezo1 ion channels may interact functionally and influence each other's spatial distribution.

     To examine this further, the researchers used cell-attached patch-clamp electrophysiology and pressure-clamp stimulation to study Piezo1 ion channels in cells expressing the channels at low and high levels. The results showed that the density of Piezo1 ion channels had no effect on their pressure sensitivity or open probability, even at non-physiological densities that would cause significant overlap between channels.

     The researchers also used stochastic simulations to model the spatial distribution of Piezo1 ion channels. These simulations, combined with the electrophysiological data, revealed that Piezo1 ion channels behave as independent mechanotransducers at densities likely to be physiologically relevant. This means that Piezo1 channels do not have a strong tendency to spatially concentrate and functionally modulate each other's mechanical sensitivity.

     This property is essential for cells to transduce forces homogeneously across the entire cell membrane. Cells are constantly exposed to mechanical forces and have evolved diverse molecular mechanisms to detect these forces. Piezo1 is a mechanically gated cation channel that directly senses membrane tension with high sensitivity. The researchers propose that the independent behavior of Piezo1 ion channels enables cells to transduce mechanical forces with high spatial homogeneity.

     The study provides new insights into the properties of Piezo1 ion channels and their role in mechanotransduction. The findings have implications for our understanding of how cells sense and respond to mechanical forces, and may contribute to the development of new treatments for diseases related to mechanotransduction dysfunction.
Biomedical research has long suggested that decisions are made through the accumulation of noisy evidence to a threshold or bound. In perception, this mechanism explains how the strength of a stimulus, characterized by its signal-to-noise ratio, affects decision speed, accuracy, and confidence. A recent study aimed to test the predictions of this mechanism by introducing a novel sensory manipulation that mimics the addition of unbiased noise to motion-selective regions of the visual cortex.

The study used a well-studied motion task and verified the effect of the manipulation by recording from neurons in the visual cortex of macaque monkeys. The results showed that increasing the noise, or volatility, of the momentary evidence bearing on a decision led to faster decisions and greater confidence in both humans and monkeys. Notably, the accuracy of the decisions was minimally impaired. The magnitude of the effects was in agreement with predictions of a bounded evidence accumulation model.

To test the influence of volatility on decision-making, the researchers applied a manipulation that changed the statistical properties of a dynamic stimulus. They verified that the manipulation had the desired properties by recording from direction-selective neurons in the middle temporal (MT) and medial superior temporal (MST) areas of the macaque visual cortex. These areas are known to represent the momentary evidence in tasks similar to the one used in the study.

The study used a reaction-time version of the random-dot motion discrimination task to examine the relationship between volatility and decision speed. Three human participants and one macaque completed a large number of trials, and the results showed that increasing volatility led to faster decisions and greater confidence, with minimal impairment in accuracy. The findings support the predictions of the bounded evidence accumulation model and provide new insights into the neural mechanisms of decision-making under uncertainty. The study's results have implications for our understanding of how the brain processes sensory information and makes decisions in the presence of noise.
summary
     The insulin-like signaling pathway in Caenorhabditis elegans maintains a relatively short lifespan by phosphorylating and inactivating DAF-16, the ortholog of the FOXO transcription factors in mammalian cells. DAF-16 is phosphorylated by AKT kinases, preventing its nuclear translocation. Calcineurin, a PP2B phosphatase, also limits the lifespan of C. elegans, but its mechanism of action was previously unknown. This study shows that TAX-6â¢CNB-1, the C. elegans Calcineurin ortholog, and UNC-43, the Ca2+/calmodulin-dependent kinase type II (CAMKII) ortholog, regulate lifespan through DAF-16. UNC-43 phosphorylates and activates DAF-16, promoting its nuclear localization, while TAX-6â¢CNB-1 removes the phosphoryl group, leading to DAF-16 inactivation. The phosphorylation of DAF-16 at S286 by UNC-43 is removed by TAX-6â¢CNB-1, resulting in DAF-16 inactivation. Mammalian FOXO3 is also regulated by CAMKIIA and Calcineurin. The study found that UNC-43 and TAX-6â¢CNB-1 regulate C. elegans lifespan through the reversible phosphorylation of DAF-16. The data suggest that CAMKII (UNC-43) and Calcineurin (TAX-6â¢CNB-1) extend or shorten the lifespan of C. elegans by phosphorylating or de-phosphorylating serine 286 of DAF-16. The direct, antagonistic nature of these two enzymes raises the possibility that they would constitutively negate each other and have no control over DAF-16. However, the insulin signaling pathway appears to dominate in conditions where there is a conflict between IIS and CAMKII/Calcineurin signaling toward DAF-16. The study also found that multiple signaling pathways, including insulin/IGF-1 signaling, germline signaling, mitochondrial signaling, and dietary restriction, regulate aging in C. elegans. The insulin/IGF-1 signaling pathway, which includes the upstream insulin/IGF-1 receptor DAF-2 and the downstream FOXO transcription factor DAF-16, is the best characterized. The study provides new insights into the regulation of DAF-16 by insulin signaling, UNC-43 (CAMKII), and TAX-6â¢CNB-1 (Calcineurin) in C. elegans.
summary
     Biologists have long been interested in understanding how the nervous system organizes motor sequences, or the series of movements that occur in a specific order. A recent study in fruit flies, or Drosophila, has shed light on this process by examining how these insects groom their bodies with sequential movements. The researchers found that the motor programs responsible for cleaning individual body parts are organized into a suppression hierarchy, where the motor programs that occur first suppress those that occur later. This means that cleaning one body part reduces the sensory drive to its motor program, which in turn relieves suppression of the next movement, allowing the grooming sequence to progress down the hierarchy.

     To study this process, the researchers used genetically targeted activation of neural subsets to drive distinct motor programs that clean individual body parts. They found that dust on the body elicits different independently evoked cleaning motor programs for five body parts that progress in series. By controlling each cleaning motor program, the researchers demonstrated that they are organized into a suppression hierarchy.

     A computational model was also developed to demonstrate how a suppression hierarchy can mediate the sequential selection of the cleaning motor programs. This model suggests that the evolutionarily ancient ability to select among competing behaviors through hierarchical suppression may have provided the necessary infrastructure to perform motor actions in sequence. The study's findings provide the first example of an innate motor sequence implemented by the prevailing model for generating human action sequences.

     The researchers anticipate that identifying the neural circuitry for each cleaning module and determining how these circuitries interact will reveal how the serial organization of grooming behavior is encoded in the nervous system through a hierarchical suppression mechanism. This study contributes to our understanding of how the nervous system organizes motor sequences and has implications for understanding the neural basis of behavior in other animals. The findings also highlight the importance of hierarchical suppression in the emergence of sequential behavior.
summary
     Biologists have long sought to understand how cells ensure accurate chromosome segregation during meiosis I in oocytes. A key process is the coordination between cohesion resolution and kinetochore-microtubule interactions, which is regulated by the spindle assembly checkpoint (SAC). However, how these processes are coordinated remains unclear. A recent study has identified Shugoshin-like protein 2 (Sgol2) as a regulatory platform that coordinates essential cell cycle processes during meiosis I in oocytes.

     Sgol2 plays a crucial role in turning off the SAC, promoting the congression and bi-orientation of bivalents on meiosis I spindles, facilitating the formation of kinetochore-microtubule attachments (K-fibers), and limiting bivalent stretching. Sgol2's ability to protect cohesin and silence the SAC depends on its interaction with protein phosphatase 2A (PP2A). In contrast, its effect on bivalent stretching and K-fiber formation is independent of PP2A and mediated by the recruitment of the kinesin MCAK and the inhibition of Aurora C kinase activity, respectively.

     The study found that Sgol2 helps to silence the SAC by binding directly to PP2A and the mitotic checkpoint complex (MCC) protein Mad2. Sgol2 also moderates chromosome stretching by recruiting MCAK to kinetochores and likely promotes K-fiber formation by down-regulating Aurora B/C kinase activity at kinetochores. Depletion of Sgol2 in oocytes has pleiotropic consequences, including inefficient SAC silencing, increased bivalent stretching, and alterations in the dynamics of kinetochore-microtubule attachments.

     The study demonstrates that Sgol2's multiple interactions link many of the processes essential for faithful chromosome segregation. A single protein, Sgol2, regulates sister chromatid cohesion, chromosome traction, the spindle assembly checkpoint, and Aurora B/C kinases. The study also shows that different point mutations in Sgol2 alter the regulation of some but not other processes, indicating that the pleiotropy observed in Sgol2-deficient oocytes is not due to secondary or tertiary pathology.

     Furthermore, the study found that Sgol2's ability to protect centromeric cohesin depends on its interaction with PP2A. However, Sgol2's deficiency causes striking changes in chromosome and microtubule dynamics, suggesting that Sgol2 has additional functions beyond protecting cohesin. Overall, the study highlights the importance of Sgol2 as a regulatory platform that coordinates essential cell cycle processes during meiosis I in oocytes.
summary
     Biomedical research has long recognized the importance of RNA localization and protein synthesis in the persistence of experience-dependent changes in brain connectivity. Recent studies have shown that local translation plays a crucial role in altering the structure and function of synapses during synapse formation and experience-dependent synaptic plasticity. A new study investigated whether local stimulation also triggers the directed trafficking of RNAs from the nucleus to stimulated synapses. The study used imaging techniques to observe RNA localization and translation in cultured Aplysia sensory-motor neurons and found that RNAs were delivered throughout the arbor of the sensory neuron, but translation was enriched only at sites of synaptic contact and/or synaptic stimulation. This suggests that spatially restricting gene expression by regulating local translation rather than directing the delivery of mRNAs from the nucleus to stimulated synapses maximizes the readiness of the entire neuronal arbor to respond to local cues.

     The study also investigated the mechanisms that trigger local translation and found a role for calcium-dependent retrograde netrin-1/DCC receptor signaling. Netrin-1 is a protein that is released from synapses during synapse formation and synaptic plasticity, and the study found that its release triggers translation at sites of synaptic but not non-synaptic contacts. The researchers tested this idea by incubating Aplysia sensory-motor neuronal cultures with recombinant Fc-netrin-1 and found that it indeed regulates the synthesis of localized transcripts.

     The study's findings suggest that the spatial regulation of gene expression in Aplysia sensory-motor neurons is primarily mediated at the level of translation rather than at the level of RNA targeting from the nucleus. This is in contrast to previous studies that have reported that activity can regulate the localization of mRNAs within neurons. The study's results indicate that local translation is a key mechanism for regulating gene expression in response to local cues, and that netrin-1/DCC signaling plays a crucial role in this process. Further research is needed to validate the idea that mRNAs are delivered throughout the neuronal arbor, but the study's findings provide new insights into the complex mechanisms underlying experience-dependent changes in brain connectivity.
Biomedical researchers have developed a new method to study how cells interact and organize into complex patterns and structures during development. This process, called morphogenesis, is crucial for the formation of tissues and organs in the human body. However, current methods to model morphogenic events lack control over the emergence of different cell types and the ability to selectively perturb specific cell subpopulations.

     To address this limitation, scientists employed an inducible CRISPR interference (CRISPRi) system in human induced pluripotent stem cells (hiPSCs) to silence key proteins that regulate cell adhesion and cortical tension. By creating mixed populations of hiPSCs with and without the CRISPRi system, researchers induced mosaic knockdown (KD) of specific genes, such as ROCK1 and CDH1, which are involved in cell-cell adhesion and cortical tension.

     The results showed that mosaic KD of ROCK1 or CDH1 led to differential patterning within hiPSC colonies due to cellular self-organization, while retaining an epithelial pluripotent phenotype. The induction of mosaic KD stimulated a transient wave of differential gene expression within the mixed populations, which stabilized in coordination with observed self-organization. This approach enables genetic interrogation of emergent multicellular properties, facilitating a better understanding of the molecular pathways that regulate symmetry-breaking during morphogenesis.

     The study demonstrates a novel strategy to direct the emergence of heterotypic cell populations and control multicellular organization in pluripotent stem cells. This method has the potential to create robust models of morphogenesis necessary for the mechanistic study of human developmental tissue patterning and formation. By selectively perturbing specific cell subpopulations, researchers can gain insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying morphogenic events, ultimately contributing to a deeper understanding of human development and disease.
Biomedical researchers have long observed that the precision of visual working memory (VWM) decreases as the number of items to be remembered increases. A new study proposes a normative theory to explain this phenomenon, known as the set size effect. The theory suggests that the brain aims to minimize a weighted sum of two costs: an error-based behavioral cost and a neural encoding cost.

    The researchers developed a model based on this theory and found that it accurately predicts the set size effects observed in nine delayed-estimation experiments. Notably, the model shows that the effects of set size on encoding precision are mediated by probing probability, which aligns with previous empirical findings. The model also accounts for the non-monotonic relationship between set size and the total amount of resource invested in item encoding.

    The study's findings suggest that cognitive limitations in VWM may arise from rational cost minimization rather than from constraints. The theory may apply beyond working memory tasks, potentially explaining set size effects in attention tasks as well. The researchers propose that the selectivity of attention arises from a need to balance performance against the costs associated with neural activity.

    The model was tested on data from nine previous experiments and found to account well for the effects of both set size and probing probability on encoding precision. The study also predicts a non-monotonic relationship between population-level spiking activity and set size, which can be tested using neuroimaging experiments. Furthermore, the theory makes predictions about the effects of incentive manipulations on encoding precision, as such manipulations affect the expected behavioral cost function.

    The study's findings have implications for our understanding of cognitive limitations and the neural mechanisms underlying VWM. The theory provides a formal framework for understanding how the brain optimizes performance in the face of limited resources, and may lead to new insights into the neural basis of attention and working memory.
summary
     Biologists have long known that plants use a process called autophagy to protect themselves against infectious diseases. However, it was unclear how plant pathogens, such as the Irish potato famine pathogen Phytophthora infestans, interfere with autophagy-related processes. In a recent study, researchers discovered that PexRD54, an effector protein secreted by Phytophthora infestans, binds to a host autophagy protein called ATG8CL. This binding stimulates the formation of autophagosomes, which are membrane-bound structures that engulf and recycle cellular components.

     The researchers found that PexRD54 also depletes the autophagy cargo receptor Joka2 from ATG8CL complexes. Joka2 is a protein that plays a positive role in plant defense against pathogens. By removing Joka2 from ATG8CL complexes, PexRD54 interferes with Joka2's ability to help the plant defend against the pathogen. This suggests that PexRD54 has evolved to antagonize a host autophagy cargo receptor to counteract host defenses.

     Autophagy is a complex process that involves the selective degradation and recycling of cellular components. In plants, autophagy plays a crucial role in defense against pathogens. However, the molecular mechanisms that mediate defense-related autophagy and the selective nature of this process are not well understood. This study provides new insights into how plant pathogens manipulate autophagy-related processes to their advantage.

     The researchers used a combination of biochemical and genetic approaches to study the interaction between PexRD54 and ATG8CL. They found that PexRD54 has a high affinity for ATG8CL and stimulates the formation of ATG8CL-marked autophagosomes. This is distinct from the behavior of another pathogen effector, RavZ, which inhibits autophagy. Instead, PexRD54 activates selective autophagy, possibly to eliminate defense-related compounds or to reattribute cellular resources by promoting nutrient recycling.

     The discovery of PexRD54's ability to bind to ATG8CL and deplete Joka2 from ATG8CL complexes has important implications for our understanding of plant-pathogen interactions. It suggests that pathogens can activate autophagy for their own benefit, rather than simply inhibiting it. This study highlights the complex and dynamic nature of plant-pathogen interactions and the need for further research into the mechanisms of defense-related autophagy.
Summary of Human Cardiac Fibroblasts Adaptive Responses to Controlled Combined Mechanical Strain and Oxygen Changes in Vitro

     Cardiac fibroblasts (CFs) play a crucial role in cardiac tissue adaptation and inflammation during pathological conditions such as ischemia. However, the mechanisms underlying CF responses to environmental changes are not yet fully understood. To address this knowledge gap, researchers developed a novel microdevice that applies combined mechanical strain and controlled oxygen tension to human primary CFs.

     The study aimed to investigate how CFs respond to different combinations of mechanical strain and oxygen changes. The results showed that individual stimuli, such as mechanical strain or oxygen deprivation, triggered distinct responses in CFs, including proliferation and collagen production. However, only the combination of hypoxia (low oxygen levels) and simulated loss of contractility (2% strain) induced the release of inflammatory and pro-fibrotic cytokines and matrix metalloproteinases.

     This study provides new insights into the adaptive responses of human CFs to environmental changes, which are relevant to cardiac fibrotic disease. The findings suggest that the combination of hypoxia and reduced contractility is critical in eliciting inflammatory and fibrotic remodeling responses in CFs. In contrast, individual stimuli only regulated proliferative and collagen-related responses.

     The researchers propose an innovative in vitro model that recreates a pathological environment for understanding CF responses during cardiac injury. This model can be used to study the effects of environmental stimuli on CF behavior and to develop reparative strategies for cardiac disease. The study highlights the importance of considering the combined effects of mechanical strain and oxygen changes in the development of cardiac fibrosis.

     The results of this study have implications for future research on pathological myocardial remodeling and the development of in vitro models for cardiac disease. By understanding how CFs respond to environmental changes, researchers can design more effective therapeutic strategies to prevent or treat cardiac fibrosis. The novel microdevice used in this study provides a valuable tool for investigating the complex interactions between CFs and their environment.
summary
     Biologists have long known that histone acetylation and the deposition of a specific type of histone called H2A.Z are crucial for active transcription in cells. In fruit flies (Drosophila), a single complex called DOMINO is thought to perform both of these functions, but the exact mechanism was unclear. Researchers have now identified two distinct forms of the DOMINO complex, called DOM-A and DOM-B, which are generated by alternative splicing of a single gene. These two complexes have different functions: DOM-B incorporates H2A.Z into chromatin in an ATP-dependent manner, similar to the yeast SWR1 complex. In contrast, DOM-A functions as an ATP-independent histone acetyltransferase complex, similar to the yeast NuA4 complex, and targets lysine 12 of histone H4 for acetylation. Both complexes are necessary for transcriptional regulation, but through different mechanisms. This study provides an example of how different evolutionary strategies can lead to similar functional separation, as seen in yeast and humans, where nucleosome remodeling and histone acetyltransferase complexes originate from gene duplication and paralog specification. In Drosophila, the same diversity is generated by alternative splicing of a single gene. The researchers used RNA sequencing to explore the functional differences of the two DOM isoforms on transcription and found that knockdown of either DOM-A, DOM-B, TIP60, or H2A.V had significant effects on transcription. The study also suggests that the ATPase activity of DOM-A is dispensable for H4K12 acetylation by the DOM-A complex, a principle that might be conserved across metazoans. Overall, this work illustrates how alternative splicing generates functional diversity amongst chromatin regulators.
summary
     The formation of neuromuscular junctions (NMJs) between motoneurons and muscle cells is a complex process that requires precise communication between the two cell types. A key player in this process is Î²-catenin (Ctnnb1), a protein found in muscle cells that is critical for the differentiation of motoneurons. However, the mechanism by which muscle Ctnnb1 sends signals to motoneurons to facilitate NMJ formation is not well understood.

     Researchers used an in vivo transgenic approach to investigate the functions of muscle Ctnnb1 in NMJ formation. They found that a mutant form of Ctnnb1 that lacked the ability to regulate gene expression was unable to rescue the presynaptic deficits caused by a Ctnnb1 mutation. In contrast, the cell-adhesion function of Ctnnb1 was found to be dispensable. These results suggest that muscle Ctnnb1 regulates presynaptic differentiation by controlling the expression of muscle-derived proteins.

     The researchers then screened for proteins that may serve as a Ctnnb1-directed retrograde factor and identified Slit2, an environmental cue that repels or collapses neuronal axons. They found that overexpression of Slit2 in muscle cells was able to diminish the presynaptic deficits caused by a Ctnnb1 mutation in mice. Furthermore, Slit2 immobilized on beads was able to induce the formation of synaptophysin puncta in axons of spinal cord explants.

     These observations suggest that Slit2 serves as a factor utilized by muscle Ctnnb1 to direct presynaptic differentiation. The researchers also found that muscle Ctnnb1 associates with Tcf/Lef cis-elements in the promoter region of the Slit2 gene, and that Slit2 expression in muscle cells is promoted by elevating Ctnnb1. Transgenic mice expressing Slit2 specifically in the muscle were able to diminish presynaptic deficits caused by a Ctnnb1 mutation, including poor innervation, reduced vesicle density, and depressed miniature endplate potentials (mEPPs).

     Overall, these findings provide new insights into the mechanism of NMJ formation and suggest that muscle Ctnnb1 regulates presynaptic differentiation by controlling the expression of muscle-derived proteins, including Slit2. This research has important implications for our understanding of the complex interactions between motoneurons and muscle cells during NMJ formation.
Biomedical researchers have developed a new method to study the dynamics of force transmission at cell-cell junctions in epithelial clusters. These cell-cell junctions play a crucial role in regulating tissue development, maintenance, and adaptation. The researchers used a combination of computational and experimental approaches to quantify the forces transmitted between cells with both sub-cellular and multi-cellular resolution.

    The study found that basal force fluctuations at cell-cell junctions are coupled to the localization of E-cadherin, a key protein component of adherens junctions in epithelial cells. At the level of individual cell-cell junctions, the researchers observed that local mechanical stresses correlated with the localization of E-cadherin molecules. This suggests that E-cadherin plays a critical role in regulating force transmission at cell-cell junctions.

    The researchers also found that force transmission through a cell requires coordinated modulation of cell-matrix adhesion and actomyosin contractility in the cell and its neighbors. This means that cells must work together to regulate the forces transmitted between them. The study also showed that force transmission across a cell cluster is short-ranged, typically on the length scale of one cell diameter, due to the anchoring and scrambling of forces by cell-matrix adhesions and basal actomyosin contractility.

    The researchers applied their method to spontaneously-forming adherent epithelial cell clusters and observed that cell-cell force exchange depended on the cell position within a cluster. The study also found that force transmission was adaptive to reconfigurations due to cell divisions or positional rearrangements. These findings provide insights into the mechanisms that control mechanical stress homeostasis in dynamic epithelial tissues.

    The study highlights the importance of coordinated cell-cell and cell-matrix interactions in regulating force transmission and tissue mechanics. The researchers' method provides a powerful tool for studying mechanotransduction in cell-cell adhesions, which could lead to a better understanding of tissue development, maintenance, and adaptation. The findings of this study have implications for our understanding of various biological processes, including tissue morphogenesis, wound healing, and cancer progression.

     Biomedical researchers have made significant progress in understanding the structure and function of the huntingtin protein, which is associated with Huntington's disease. A polyglutamine expansion in the huntingtin protein is a hallmark of this neurodegenerative disorder. To investigate the structural and biochemical properties of huntingtin and the effect of the polyglutamine expansion, researchers used various biophysical experiments, including circular dichroism, single-particle electron microscopy, and cross-linking mass spectrometry.

     The study found that huntingtin is composed of five distinct domains and adopts a spherical Î±-helical solenoid structure. The amino-terminal and carboxyl-terminal regions of the protein fold to create a central cavity. Interestingly, the researchers discovered that the polyglutamine expansion increases the Î±-helical properties of huntingtin and affects the intramolecular interactions among the domains. This suggests that the polyglutamine expansion may modulate the structure and function of huntingtin.

     The researchers used a panel of highly purified human recombinant huntingtins with varied lengths of polyglutamine tracts to conduct systematic biophysical and biochemical analyses. They found that the impact of the polyglutamine tract on huntingtin's basic structural features suggests that the protein may undergo dramatic conformational changes upon interaction with its binding partners. Single-particle electron microscopy of recombinant human huntingtins with polyglutamine tract lengths of 23- and 78-residues revealed that the protein's shape may enable a structural impact of the amino-terminal polyglutamine tract.

     The study provides a satisfying solution to the mystery of how the amino-terminal polyglutamine tract may modulate huntingtin structure and function. The researchers found that the Î±-helical properties of huntingtin increase with the length of the polyglutamine tract, suggesting that the protein's structure and function may be modulated by the size of the polyglutamine tract. This finding has important implications for understanding the pathogenesis of Huntington's disease and may lead to the development of new therapeutic strategies.

     Biomedical research has long recognized the importance of ferritinophagy, the autophagic turnover of ferritin, in regulating intracellular iron bioavailability. However, the mechanisms controlling ferritinophagy flux and the role of NCOA4, a selective cargo receptor for ferritin, in iron-dependent processes have remained poorly understood. A recent study has shed light on the molecular nature of the NCOA4-ferritin complex and the regulation of NCOA4-mediated ferritinophagy.

     The study found that NCOA4 interacts directly with ferritin heavy chain 1 (FTH1) via a conserved NCOA4 C-terminal domain and a key conserved residue on FTH1. This interaction is essential for the delivery of ferritin to lysosomes via autophagosomes. Furthermore, the study revealed that NCOA4 abundance is under dual control via autophagy and the ubiquitin-proteasome system. The ubiquitin-dependent turnover of NCOA4 is promoted by excess iron and involves an iron-dependent interaction between NCOA4 and the HERC2 ubiquitin ligase.

     The study also demonstrated that NCOA4 plays an essential role in erythroid differentiation in zebrafish and cultured cells. When iron levels are high, NCOA4 abundance is low, promoting ferritin accumulation and iron capture. Conversely, when iron is low, NCOA4 levels increase to promote ferritinophagy. The study showed that the HERC2 ligase uses its CUL7-homology domain to recognize NCOA4 under high iron conditions, mediating NCOA4 turnover via the ubiquitin-proteasome system and reducing steady-state NCOA4 levels.

     The findings of this study provide new insights into the regulation of ferritinophagy and the role of NCOA4 in iron-dependent processes. The study highlights the importance of NCOA4 in maintaining cellular iron homeostasis and suggests that aberrant degradation of ferritin in lysosomes could be deleterious to the cell when intracellular iron is high. Overall, this research has significant implications for our understanding of iron metabolism and the regulation of ferritinophagy.
The lateral prefrontal cortex (LPFC) plays a crucial role in higher-level cognition, but its functional organization has remained unclear. A long-standing theory proposes that the LPFC is organized hierarchically, with progressively rostral areas processing increasingly abstract information to facilitate efficient and flexible cognition. However, previous support for this theory has been limited.

     Recent human functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) data revealed a rostral-caudal gradient of abstraction in the LPFC, indicating that progressively rostral areas of the LPFC process more abstract information. Dynamic causal modeling showed asymmetrical interactions among LPFC sub-regions, suggesting hierarchical processing. Contrary to expectations, the relative strength of efferent (output) versus afferent (input) connections positioned the mid-LPFC as the apex of the hierarchy.

     The study also found that cognitive demands induced connectivity modulations towards the mid-LPFC, consistent with a role in integrating information for control operations. Furthermore, the strengths of these dynamics were related to trait-measured higher-level cognitive ability. This suggests that the LPFC is hierarchically organized, with the mid-LPFC positioned to synthesize abstract and concrete information to control behavior.

     The researchers confirmed the rostral-caudal axis of the LPFC operates according to abstraction principles by examining gradients of stimulus domain-generality/specificity and temporal control over action. They also modeled interactions among LPFC sub-regions to examine how cognitive control demands elicit asymmetrical influences indicative of hierarchical processing.

     The study found that both the hierarchical orientation of the mid-LPFC in fixed connectivity and the top-down influences from rostral LPFC to mid-LPFC during the engagement of cognitive control were positively related to higher-level cognitive ability. This indicates that the functional organization of the LPFC is closely tied to cognitive capacity.

     The researchers examined interactions within the LPFC by varying demands on stimulus domain and cognitive control. They found that progressively rostral areas processed progressively abstract levels of cognitive control, with demands on feature control, contextual control, and temporal control eliciting increasingly rostral activations. Dynamic causal modeling revealed that hierarchical asymmetries in connectivity were strongest in the mid-LPFC.

     Cognitive demands elicited both top-down influences from rostral to mid-LPFC and bottom-up influences from caudal to mid-LPFC. Analyses of effective connectivity and its modulations suggest that the mid-LPFC forms a nexus where information converges to influence action. These findings collectively suggest that the LPFC is hierarchically organized, with the mid-LPFC positioned to synthesize abstract and concrete information to control behavior.

     A recent study has identified a gene called TBC1D3 that plays a crucial role in the development of the brain's outer layer, known as the cortex. This gene is specific to hominoids, a group that includes humans, chimpanzees, and gorillas. Researchers found that when TBC1D3 is expressed in the brains of mice, it promotes the generation of a type of cell called outer radial glia (oRG). These cells are important for brain development and are more abundant in primates than in other animals.

     The study used a technique called in utero electroporation to introduce the TBC1D3 gene into the brains of mice. This allowed the researchers to observe the effects of the gene on brain development. They found that the expression of TBC1D3 caused the delamination of ventricular radial glia cells and promoted the generation of oRG cells.      The researchers also found that the expression of TBC1D3 led to the formation of cortical folding in the brains of mice. Cortical folding is a characteristic of the human brain and is thought to be linked to higher intelligence. The study suggests that TBC1D3 may play a key role in the evolution of the human brain.

     The researchers propose that TBC1D3 acts as a candidate gene that controls many other genes involved in cortex expansion and folding. This study provides evidence that brain development can be regulated by duplicated genes during hominoid evolution. The findings have implications for our understanding of the evolution of the human brain and the development of neurological disorders.

     The study also found that the expression of TBC1D3 reduced the expression of a gene called Trnp1, which is involved in stemness signaling pathways. This suggests that TBC1D3 may play a role in regulating the intrinsic stemness of neural progenitor cells.

     Overall, the study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying cortical folding and the evolution of the human brain. The findings highlight the importance of TBC1D3 in regulating brain development and suggest that it may be a key player in the evolution of higher intelligence.
summary
     The plant immune coactivator NPR1 plays a crucial role in activating systemic acquired resistance in plants by reprogramming the transcriptome. Previous research suggested that immune-induced ubiquitination and proteasomal degradation of NPR1 facilitate continuous delivery of active NPR1 to target promoters, maximizing gene expression. However, a new study reveals that ubiquitination of NPR1 has transcriptional roles prior to its proteasomal turnover.

     The study found that ubiquitination of NPR1 is a progressive event initiated by a Cullin-RING E3 ligase, which promotes chromatin association and expression of target genes. The E4 ligase UBE4 then enhances polyubiquitination of NPR1, leading to its targeting for proteasomal degradation. Conversely, two proteasome-associated deubiquitinases, UBP6/7, oppose ubiquitin ligase activities and enhance NPR1 longevity.

     The study showed that loss of UBE4 uncouples NPR1 target gene expression from proteasome activity, highlighting the importance of progressive ubiquitination for NPR1 activity. Initial CRL3-mediated ubiquitination is required for NPR1 to attain its full transcriptional activity, while the stepwise formation of long ubiquitin chains mediated by UBE4 inactivates NPR1 and promotes its degradation by the proteasome.

     The research indicates that initial ubiquitination boosts NPR1 transcriptional coactivator activity by enhancing target promoter occupancy in the short term and potentially promoting genomic mobility of NPR1 in the longer term. The study suggests that ubiquitin chain extension and trimming activities can fine-tune transcriptional outputs of unstable eukaryotic activators without strict requirement for sacrificial turnover.

     The findings provide new insights into the complex regulation of NPR1 and its role in plant immune responses. The study reveals that ubiquitination of NPR1 is a stepwise event requiring the actions of CRL3 and the E4 ligase UBE4, and that deubiquitinases UBP6/7 oppose ubiquitin ligase activities to fine-tune NPR1 activity. The research has implications for understanding the mechanisms of plant immune responses and the regulation of unstable eukaryotic activators.
Biologists have identified the precursors of small interfering RNAs (siRNAs) that guide DNA methylation in Arabidopsis thaliana. These siRNAs are 24 nucleotides long and play a crucial role in silencing transposons and a subset of genes. The biogenesis of 24 nt siRNAs requires nuclear RNA polymerase IV (Pol IV), RNA-dependent RNA polymerase 2 (RDR2), and DICER-like 3 (DCL3). However, the precursors of these siRNAs were previously undefined.

     Researchers have now identified Pol IV and RDR2-dependent RNAs (P4R2 RNAs) that accumulate in DCL3 mutants. These P4R2 RNAs are mostly 26-45 nucleotides long and initiate with a purine adjacent to a pyrimidine, characteristics shared by Pol IV transcripts generated in vitro. RDR2 terminal transferase activity may account for occasional non-templated nucleotides at the 3' termini of P4R2 RNAs.

     The 24 nt siRNAs primarily correspond to the 5' or 3' ends of P4R2 RNAs, suggesting a model in which siRNAs are generated from either end of P4R2 duplexes by single dicing events. This model is supported by the observation that dozens of P4R2 RNAs can overlap at a given locus, but individual P4R2 RNAs represent individual Pol IV-RDR2 transcription units.

     The researchers also found that P4R2 RNAs are dependent on both Pol IV and RDR2, as they do not accumulate in nrpd1 (Pol IV) or rdr2 mutants. Furthermore, P4R2 RNAs that accumulate in DCL2/DCL3/DCL4 triple mutants are substrates for DCL3, which can cut them into 24 nt RNAs in vitro. These findings provide new insights into the biogenesis of 24 nt siRNAs and their role in guiding DNA methylation in Arabidopsis.
Biomedical research has long sought to understand how immature neural networks develop into their mature form, enabling complex computations underlying cognition. A recent study investigated the progression of large-scale synaptic and cellular activity patterns across development in immature mice using high spatiotemporal resolution in vivo electrophysiology. The findings reveal that mature cortical processes emerge rapidly and simultaneously after a discrete but volatile transition period at the beginning of the second postnatal week of rodent development.

This transition is characterized by a relative neural quiescence, after which spatially distributed, temporally precise, and internally organized activity occurs. The study demonstrates a similar developmental trajectory in humans, suggesting an evolutionarily conserved mechanism that could facilitate a transition in network operation. The researchers hypothesize that this transient quiescent period is a requisite for the subsequent emergence of coordinated cortical networks.

The study found that the transition between immature and mature network dynamics was marked by unexpectedly decreased coordinated cellular and synaptic activity at the beginning of the second postnatal week. After this timepoint, precise neuronal synchronization and oscillatory coupling in space and time robustly emerged. The researchers observed that neural spiking was more efficiently recruited to oscillatory activity with consistent, strong phase-locking, and higher temporal precision, features characteristic of adult neural networks engaged in information processing.

The study suggests that a transient period of developmental perturbation may be an evolutionarily conserved indicator of a substantial shift in cortical network operations. The findings provide new insights into the postnatal time course for neural networks to perform complex computations and highlight the importance of a transient quiescent period in the development of mature cortical dynamics. The study's results have implications for our understanding of brain development and the emergence of cognitive functions in both rodents and humans.
Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand how multiple strains of a pathogen, such as Plasmodium falciparum, the parasite responsible for malaria, coexist and circulate within a population. This is a crucial question in epidemiology, as it has significant implications for disease control. A new agent-based model has been developed to address this issue, focusing on the var gene family of P. falciparum, which encodes the parasite's major surface antigen, PfEMP1.

The model considers the extensive diversity of multi-copy genes that undergo antigenic variation through sequential, mutually exclusive expression. It tracks the dynamics of all unique var repertoires in a population of hosts and shows that even under high levels of sexual recombination, strain competition mediated through cross-immunity structures the parasite population into a subset of coexisting dominant repertoires of var genes. The degree of antigenic overlap between these repertoires depends on transmission intensity.

Previous mathematical models have demonstrated that immune selection can lead to the maintenance of discrete, non-overlapping, antigenic repertoires, even when antigenic variability is driven by several unlinked genes that undergo recombination. The "var strain" model suggests that vector control strategies that reduce transmission will not always drastically reduce var gene diversity, as a reservoir of parasite genomes with diverse, highly non-overlapping var repertoires will remain.

The new model extends earlier strain theory by considering the effects of immune selection in populations of pathogens whose antigen-encoding loci are multi-copy and recombining. It explores whether structured parasite populations resulting from the selection pressures exerted by competition among distinct var repertoires for hosts, mediated by cross-immunity, can emerge. Empirical comparison of patterns of genetic variation at antigenic and neutral sites supports the role of immune selection in structuring parasite diversity.

This research provides an opportunity to extend existing strain theory in a way that can be tested empirically. The findings have significant implications for malaria research and control, as they suggest that vector control strategies may not always lead to a drastic reduction in var gene diversity. Instead, a reservoir of parasite genomes with diverse, highly non-overlapping var repertoires may remain, complicating efforts to control the disease.

     The article reports on the mechanism by which the deamidase OspI from the enteric bacteria Shigella flexneri disrupts the interaction between the ubiquitin-conjugating enzyme UBC13 and the RING-finger ubiquitin ligase TRAF6. OspI deamidates a glutamine residue in UBC13, converting it to glutamate (Q100E), which silences the polyubiquitination activity of the UBC13/TRAF6 complex and inhibits the downstream NF-ÎºB inflammatory response. The precise role of deamidation in silencing the UBC13/TRAF6 complex was unknown, but the study reveals that deamidation inhibits the interaction between UBC13 and TRAF6 RING-domain (TRAF6RING) by perturbing both native and transient interactions.

     Deamidation creates a new intramolecular salt-bridge in UBC13 that competes with a critical intermolecular salt-bridge at the native UBC13/TRAF6RING interface. This competition prevents transient interactions necessary to form a typical UBC13/RING complex. Additionally, repulsion between the negatively charged E100 and the negatively charged surface of TRAF6RING prevents transient interactions in the UBC13/RING complex.

     Further studies showed that deamidation disrupts the interaction between UBC13 and TRAF6RING by three mechanisms: i) a new intramolecular R14/E100 salt-bridge appears in dUBC13, which competes with a critical intermolecular salt-bridge in the native complex; ii) the salt-bridge competition also perturbs the UBC13/TRAF6RING transient complexes to inhibit association; and iii) transient repulsion between the negatively charged E100 and the negatively charged interface of TRAF6RING reduces the interaction.

     The study highlights a mechanism by which a post-translational modification, deamidation, perturbs the conformation and stability of transient complexes to inhibit protein-protein association. The findings have implications for understanding how Shigella flexneri suppresses the inflammatory response (NF-ÎºB pathway) via deamidation of UBC13 at Q100. Deamidation of Q100 to E100 inhibits the synthesis of K63-linked polyubiquitin chains by the UBC13/TRAF6 complex, preventing the downstream activation of NF-ÎºB pathway. These chains serve as a scaffold to bring together TAK1/2 and IKK kinases, eventually leading to phosphorylation and activation of the IKK kinases, IÎºB degradation, and nuclear translocation of transcription factor Nf-ÎºB.
Biomedical researchers have long used a technique called optogenetics to activate or inactivate specific types of neurons in the brain to understand their functions. However, a new study suggests that this approach may not always provide a complete picture of how these neurons work. The study, conducted in the auditory cortex of awake mice, found that activating and inactivating two types of interneurons, called somatostatin-positive (Sst+) and parvalbumin-positive (Pvalb+) interneurons, had different effects on neural responses.

When Sst+ interneurons were inactivated, the responses of other neurons in the auditory cortex increased, indicating that these interneurons play a role in regulating the strength of neural signals. In contrast, inactivating Pvalb+ interneurons weakened the tuning of neural responses to specific sounds and reduced the amount of information transferred between neurons. However, when Sst+ and Pvalb+ interneurons were activated, no such differences were observed. This suggests that these two types of interneurons support distinct computational functions in the auditory cortex.

The researchers used a simple network model to understand why activating and inactivating interneurons produced different results. They found that small changes in the parameters of the model, such as the level of spontaneous activity or the strength of the light manipulation, could determine whether activation and inactivation would produce consistent or paradoxical conclusions about interneuron function.

The study's findings have implications for the interpretation of recent causal data on interneuron function. The researchers suggest that the abundance of recent data may not be as straightforward as previously thought, and that the effects of activating and inactivating interneurons may depend on the specific context and parameters of the experiment. The study highlights the need for a more nuanced understanding of how to use causal strategies to gain insight into interneuron function.

The researchers used optogenetics to manipulate Sst+ and Pvalb+ interneurons in the auditory cortex of awake mice, while recording neural responses to tones. They found that inactivating Sst+ interneurons increased response gain, while inactivating Pvalb+ interneurons weakened tuning and decreased information transfer. The study's findings suggest that Sst+ and Pvalb+ interneurons support specific, separable functions in the auditory cortex, and that the effects of activating and inactivating these neurons depend on the specific context and parameters of the experiment.

     Biomedical research has shown that working memory (WM) is an emergent property of large-scale network interactions in the brain. However, the causal relationship between oscillatory activity and WM performance is not fully understood. To investigate this relationship, researchers used transcranial alternating current stimulation (tACS) to modulate oscillatory activity in a right frontoparietal network that supports WM. The study aimed to provide causal evidence for the role of oscillatory synchronization during demanding WM performance.

     The researchers conducted two experiments using tACS to selectively entrain endogenous brain rhythms. They tested four hypotheses: (1) behavioral WM performance is enhanced by entraining synchronous activity within a right frontoparietal network activated by WM demands; (2) regional brain activity in this frontoparietal network is modulated in a phase-dependent manner; (3) changes in blood oxygenation level-dependent (BOLD) activity induced by phase-dependent network stimulation correlate with behavioral performance; and (4) functional connectivity across distant brain regions is modulated in a phase-dependent manner.

     The results showed that externally induced synchronization improved performance when cognitive demands were high. Simultaneously collected functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) data revealed that tACS effects depended on the relative phase of the stimulation and the internal cognitive processing state. Specifically, synchronous tACS during the verbal WM task increased parietal activity, which correlated with behavioral performance. Furthermore, functional connectivity results indicated that the relative phase of frontoparietal stimulation influences information flow within the WM network.

     The study demonstrated a link between behavioral performance in a demanding WM task and large-scale brain synchronization across a right frontoparietal network activated by the task. The researchers found that manipulations of tACS phase modulate the underlying brain activity and that tACS can influence long-range connectivity in a phase- and brain state-dependent manner. The results suggest that external manipulations of large-scale brain synchronization can be used to shape activity and connectivity within brain networks, and those modulations are detectable using fMRI. Overall, the study provides a direct demonstration of the role of phase synchronization in cognitive demanding WM processes and shows the neural correlates associated with this process.

     Biomedical research has shown that fruit flies (Drosophila melanogaster) exhibit robust odor-guided behaviors in response to odor gradients. To investigate how early olfactory circuits process temporal variations of olfactory stimuli, researchers subjected flies to precisely defined odor concentration waveforms and examined the spike patterns of olfactory sensory neurons (OSNs) and projection neurons (PNs). The study found a significant temporal transformation between OSN and PN spike patterns, with PNs strongly signaling the OSN spike rate and its rate of change.

A simple two-dimensional model was developed to predict the PN output based on the OSN spike rate and its rate of change. The model closely predicted the PN output, indicating that PNs primarily signal the acceleration and rate of change of dynamic odor stimuli to higher brain centers. This allows animals to reliably respond to the onsets of odor concentrations.

PNs relay olfactory information to higher brain centers such as the mushroom body and the lateral horn. Recent studies have shown that Drosophila OSNs encode not only the odor concentration but also its rate of change as a function of time. Building on this advance, the researchers asked how PNs further contribute to creating internal representations of dynamic olfactory environments.

The study tested first- and second-order neurons in the Drosophila olfactory system with a wide range of dynamically varying odor concentrations. A simple 2D LN model was constructed for the temporal processing of olfactory information. The shape of the 2D nonlinearity and the relatively low prediction error of the model support the hypothesis that the PN spike rate is most strongly dependent on the rate of change of its feedforward OSN input.

The researchers found that Drosophila's early olfactory system signals the acceleration of odor concentrations to higher brain centers. This encoding mechanism allows an animal to detect the odor onset at a very early phase. The study suggests that acceleration encoding in the Drosophila antennal lobe enables higher olfactory centers to rapidly respond to onsets of slowly rising odor stimuli. This is crucial for flying insects to detect and respond to an encountered odor plume before the fly's position drifts relative to adjacent plumes.
summary
     In flowering plants, seed development begins with the fusion of the maternal egg and central cells with two paternal sperm cells, resulting in the formation of the embryo and endosperm, respectively. The fertilization products are surrounded by the maternally derived seed coat, whose development is blocked by epigenetic regulators belonging to the Polycomb Group (PcG) protein family prior to fertilization. However, a new study reveals that fertilization of the central cell leads to the production of auxin, a plant hormone, which is likely exported to the maternal tissues, driving seed coat development by removing the PcG block.

     The researchers found that mutants for the MADS-box transcription factor AGL62 have impaired transport of auxin from the endosperm to the integuments, resulting in seed abortion. They propose that AGL62 regulates auxin transport from the endosperm to the integuments, leading to the removal of the PcG block on seed coat development. The study suggests that auxin production in the developing endosperm is the trigger for seed coat development.

     The researchers investigated plants mutant for AGL62 and found that they abort early after fertilization, correlating with early endosperm cellularization and failure to develop a seed coat. They also found that the agl62 mutant has defects in both seed coat development in fertilized ovules and endosperm development in autonomous seeds, suggesting that AGL62 is involved in mediating both endosperm and seed coat developmental pathways.

     The study provides multiple lines of evidence supporting the hypothesis that auxin production in the fertilized central cell/endosperm drives seed coat development. This includes the rapid accumulation of auxin in the integuments after fertilization, impaired auxin biosynthesis but not auxin signaling in the endosperm causing defects in seed coat development, ovules of transgenic lines producing auxin ectopically in the central cell initiating seed coat development without fertilization, and failure of seed coat development in the agl62 mutant correlating with failure to export auxin from the endosperm.

     The study shows that seed coat development requires production of auxin in the fertilized central cell/endosperm and most likely the transport of auxin to the ovule integuments in an AGL62-dependent manner. The fertilization of the central cell results in the production of auxin that is exported to the integuments, lifting the PRC2 block on seed coat development and allowing for cell elongation and differentiation. However, this process is highly dependent on the activity of the transcription factor AGL62, since agl62 seeds fail to export auxin from the endosperm, leading to auxin accumulation and consequently, failure to develop a seed coat.

     The interaction between a bacteriophage and its host is mediated by the phage's receptor binding protein (RBP), which plays a crucial role in governing phage activity and host range. Despite its importance, the molecular rules of RBP function remain poorly understood. To address this knowledge gap, researchers developed a high-throughput method called ORACLE (Optimized Recombination, Accumulation, and Library Expression) to systematically dissect the functional role of every residue in the tip domain of T7 phage RBP.

     Using ORACLE, the researchers created a large, unbiased library of phage variants at a targeted gene locus, making all single amino acid substitutions at every site (1660 variants) and quantifying the functional role of all variants on multiple bacterial hosts. This approach generated high-resolution functional maps delineating regions concentrated with function-enhancing substitutions and host-specific substitutional patterns, many of which were previously unknown.

     The study revealed that single amino acid substitutions alone can generate broad functional diversity, highlighting the evolutionary adaptability of the RBP. The researchers also discovered T7 variants with far greater virulence than wild-type T7, demonstrating that even naturally adapted phages can be engineered for higher efficacy. Furthermore, the study identified gain-of-function variants against resistant hosts and host-constricting variants that eliminated certain hosts.

     To demonstrate the therapeutic value of ORACLE, the researchers engineered T7 variants that avert the emergence of spontaneous resistance in pathogenic Escherichia coli causing urinary tract infections. The study explains the molecular drivers of adaptability of the tip domain and identifies key functional regions determining activity and host range. The results indicate that ORACLE can generate phage variants superior to wild-type phage, which could serve as starting points for further engineering therapeutic phages. Further experiments will be required to assess the in vivo efficacy of the T7 variants. The study presents a generalized framework for characterizing sequence-function relationships in many phage-bacterial systems.
Individuals with congenital amusia, a lifelong condition characterized by unreliable pitch processing, have been found to rely less on pitch cues during speech perception and more on other dimensions such as duration. A recent study used functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) to investigate the neural basis for this strategy. Fifteen individuals with amusia and 15 controls participated in the study, which involved reading sentences with a comma indicating a grammatical phrase boundary and then selecting the best match for the written sentence from two spoken sentences that differed only in pitch and/or duration cues.

The results showed that individuals with amusia exhibited reduced functional connectivity between left prefrontal language-related regions and right hemisphere pitch-related regions. This difference in connectivity was not present during a control task. The study suggests that the reliability of perceptual dimensions is linked with functional connectivity between frontal and perceptual regions, and that individuals with amusia may have developed a compensatory mechanism to down-weight pitch cues due to its unreliability.

The study's findings are consistent with previous research showing that individuals with amusia rely less on pitch than controls to process spoken language. The results also support the idea that right hemisphere auditory areas are not only abnormally connected to right frontal areas during tonal tasks, but are also less integrated with frontal left hemisphere regions when processing speech and language. The study's null results for group differences in activation during speech processing are consistent with prior reports that amusics and controls do not differ in pitch representations within sensory regions.

Brainstem encoding of pitch in speech and musical stimuli has been shown to be unimpaired in individuals with amusia. The study's findings suggest that amusics neglect pitch because they have implicitly learned that their memory for pitch is unreliable, and that this down-weighting of pitch is reflected in decreased functional connectivity between right auditory areas and downstream task-relevant areas that integrate information from perceptual regions. Overall, the study provides new insights into the neural basis of congenital amusia and the compensatory mechanisms that individuals with this condition may use to process speech and language.
summary
     A newly discovered contagious cancer, Devil Facial Tumour 2 (DFT2), has been found in the Tasmanian devil, a species that already harbors a widespread contagious cancer, Devil Facial Tumour 1 (DFT1). In contrast to DFT1, DFT2 cells express major histocompatibility complex (MHC) class I molecules, which play a crucial role in the immune system. However, the MHC class I alleles expressed by DFT2 cells are common among host devils or non-polymorphic, reducing their immunogenicity in a population sharing these alleles. This suggests that contagious cancers may exploit partial histocompatibility between the tumor and host, but loss of allogeneic antigens could facilitate the widespread transmission of DFT2.

     The researchers found that DFT2 cells express both classical and non-classical MHC class I molecules in vitro and in vivo, in contrast to DFT1, which has lost expression of MHC class I. The non-classical MHC class I molecule expressed by DFT2 cells is non-polymorphic, likely reducing the immunogenicity of these tumor cells. Additionally, the classical MHC class I allele with the highest expression in DFT2 cells is shared with host devils. The researchers also observed that MHC class I expression varies among DFT2 tumors in vivo, suggesting that MHC class I-negative subclones could emerge as DFT2 transmits more widely.

     The study's findings indicate that DFT2 cells express MHC class I molecules, unlike DFT1 and the contagious cancer circulating among dogs, CTVT. Further analysis is needed to define the host immune response to DFT2 and its role in shaping tumor evolution. The similarity of MHC class I alleles between DFT2 and host devils may reflect a shared geographical range and genotype pool to the cancer founder. The frequency of this allele among the wider population may impact the ability of DFT2 to spread further. The heterogenic expression of MHC class I molecules in DFT2 biopsies suggests that MHC class I expression is not fixed and may be gradually lost in DFT2.

     Biomedical research has shed light on the neural mechanisms underlying spatial and non-spatial behaviors in the granular retrosplenial cortex (RSG). A recent study used optogenetic circuit mapping in mice to investigate the neural codes underlying RSG function. The results reveal a double dissociation between parallel circuits in superficial RSG, which process disparate inputs.

     The anterior thalamus and dorsal subiculum, sources of spatial information, selectively recruit small low-rheobase (LR) pyramidal cells in RSG. In contrast, neighboring regular-spiking (RS) cells are preferentially controlled by claustral and anterior cingulate inputs, sources of mostly non-spatial information. This parallel processing is enabled by the precise sublaminar organization of distinct principal neuronal subtypes and axonal afferents within RSG layer 1.

     The study found that LR neurons receive directional and spatial inputs from the anterior thalamus and dorsal subiculum. The synaptic dynamics of thalamic inputs to LR cells allow for the robust encoding of angular speed, even though these inputs only explicitly contain information about head direction, not speed. This suggests that LR neurons are ideally suited to integrate head direction input from the thalamus and spatial inputs from the hippocampal formation via the dorsal subiculum.

     The integration of various types of directional and distance information is a key computational function of the retrosplenial cortex as a whole. The distinct morphology and intrinsic properties of LR neurons make them well-positioned to support spatial orientation computations in the RSG. Further research is needed to determine how these parallel streams of information are integrated in downstream neurons within both the ipsilateral and contralateral granular and dysgranular retrosplenial cortices.
