     Traumatic brain injury (TBI) is a significant risk factor for dementias, including chronic traumatic encephalopathy (CTE), a type of tauopathy. However, the mechanisms that promote the spread of tau aggregates after TBI are not fully understood. Researchers used a novel approach to investigate the role of seizures in promoting the spread of tauopathy after TBI. They developed a genetically encoded fluorescent Tau biosensor in zebrafish larvae that reports the accumulation of human Tau species when seeded via intraventricular brain injections.

     The researchers subjected zebrafish larvae to a novel TBI paradigm, which produced various TBI features, including cell death, post-traumatic seizures, and Tau inclusions. They found that bath application of dynamin inhibitors or anticonvulsant drugs rescued TBI-induced tauopathy and cell death. These results suggest a role for seizure activity in the prion-like seeding and spreading of tauopathy following TBI.

     The study used zebrafish larvae as a model system, which offered a potent complement to rodent models of TBI and cellular models of tauopathy. The researchers engineered a novel in vivo Tau biosensor model in zebrafish that can visualize pathological Tau spreading and accumulation within the intact and vibrant CNS. They found that post-traumatic seizures correlate strongly with spreading tau pathology following TBI and that manipulating this seizure activity mitigated Tau aggregation.

     The results of the study implicate seizures and dynamin-dependent endocytosis in the spread of Tau seeds, offering potential therapeutic targets. The findings suggest that anti-convulsants that dampen post-traumatic seizures may be a route to moderating subsequent tauopathy. Further work is warranted to explore this potential therapeutic approach. The study provides new insights into the mechanisms underlying the progression of tauopathy after TBI and highlights the potential of zebrafish as a valuable model system for studying neurotrauma.
Biomedical researchers have long studied the connections between different brain regions and how they relate to behavior and lifestyle. Typically, these studies focus on the strength of communication between distinct brain areas, known as functional connectivity. However, a new study suggests that the spatial arrangement of these brain regions, or their topography, plays a significant role in determining functional connectivity.

The researchers used resting-state functional magnetic resonance imaging (rfMRI) to investigate the relationship between the spatial configuration of brain regions and measures of brain connectivity. They found that the shape and exact location of brain regions interact strongly with the modeling of brain connectivity. Furthermore, the spatial arrangement of functional regions was strongly predictive of non-imaging measures of behavior and lifestyle.

The study's findings have important implications for interpreting the relationship between functional imaging data and cognitive traits. The researchers suggest that cross-subject variations in the spatial configuration of functional brain regions may be misinterpreted as changes in functional connectivity. This could lead to inaccurate conclusions about the relationship between brain activity and behavior.

To address this issue, the researchers propose that it is essential to disambiguate the influence of spatial topography when estimating functional connectivity. They note that recent advances have improved the alignment of brain regions across individuals, but more work is needed to tackle this challenge.

The study's results also highlight the importance of considering the organization of the brain into large-scale functional networks. These networks are composed of distinct brain regions that show temporal correlations with each other, revealing complex patterns of functional connectivity. The researchers used partial canonical correlation analysis (CCA) to investigate the relationship between network matrices and spatial topography.

Their results showed that network matrices contain little unique trait-level cross-subject information that is not also reflected in the spatial topographical organization of functional regions. This suggests that the cross-subject information represented in commonly adopted "connectivity fingerprints" largely reflects spatial variability in the location of functional regions across individuals, rather than variability in coupling strength.

Overall, the study's findings emphasize the need for a better understanding of the relationship between spatial configuration and functional connectivity in the brain. This knowledge is crucial for accurately interpreting the relationship between functional imaging data and cognitive traits.

     The research also explored the integrity of the regeneration potential of skeletal muscles in TBP2 null mice during adult life. The study found that MuSCs isolated from TBP2-deficient muscles differentiated into myotubes with the same efficiency as wild-type MuSCs, indicating an intact capacity of adult MuSCs to proliferate, self-renew, and differentiate during ongoing muscle regeneration in the absence of TBP2.

     Furthermore, the study detected comparable numbers of MuSCs in muscles of wild-type and TBP2 null mice 12 days after injury, indicating an intact capacity of adult MuSCs to proliferate, self-renew, and differentiate during ongoing muscle regeneration in the absence of TBP2. Summary
     Researchers used a technique called optogenetics to study how brain stimulation affects the connections between different areas of the brain in non-human primates. They used a large-scale optogenetic interface to stimulate excitatory neurons in the primary somatosensory ( S1 ) and motor ( M1 ) cortices of two macaques. At the same time, they recorded electrical activity across these areas using electrocorticography ( ECoG ).

     The researchers found that stimulation in either S1 or M1 strengthened the connections between these areas within minutes. They also observed heterogeneous changes in connectivity across the network, which reflected the correlations introduced by stimulation-evoked activity. These findings are consistent with Hebbian plasticity models, which suggest that "neurons that fire together, wire together".

     To test the Hebbian model, the researchers compared the correlations directly induced by stimulation with the changes in network correlations observed after conditioning. They found a robust relationship between stimulus-evoked correlations and changes in baseline connectivity across the network. This suggests that large-scale stimulation-induced changes are consistent with Hebbian plasticity.

     The researchers used two measures of functional connectivity to provide complementary views of the mechanisms of plasticity. They found that both measures supported a key role for Hebbian learning in sensorimotor cortical plasticity. However, they note that a deeper understanding of the relationship between these bodies of work would require further study of intermediate time-frames and detailed cortical mapping.

     The large-scale optogenetic interface used in this study enabled robust estimation of functional connectivity and stimulation-evoked correlations. This work has significant implications for stimulation-based neurorehabilitation strategies and extends Hebbian plasticity models to large-scale circuits.

     A rare case of a Rhesus macaque monkey, born without primary visual cortex (V1), was studied to understand the neural consequences of such damage. The monkey, named S, showed unremarkable in-group behavior, but was impaired in visual task training. Magnetic resonance imaging revealed that visual structures outside of the lesion were normal, and stimulation under anesthesia activated the lateral geniculate nucleus and pulvinar. Visual cortical activation was sparse, but included face patches.

     Functional connectivity analysis showed an intact network of bilateral dorsal visual areas temporally correlated with V5/MT activation, even without V1. However, there was little evidence for strengthened subcortical input to V5/MT supporting residual visual function or blindsight-like phenomena. The monkey's visual structures, both cortical and subcortical, outside of the large lesion remained intact, suggesting a role in residual visual function.

     The structural connectivity between subcortical regions and area V5/MT was weak, but the microstructure was intact. A connectivity analysis was performed to understand the nature of the response in area V5/MT, which showed no significant activation to either checkerboard or moving dots stimuli. However, foci of activation were found in the region of V5/MT in dorsal STS of both hemispheres, suggesting sub-threshold activation.

     A functional network of cortical areas showing a similar BOLD response over time to V5/MT was mapped out, even without strong visually-evoked V5/MT responses. Retinal information may reach the dorsal visual areas via weak pathways with LGN and pulvinar, but the lack of consistent visual cortex activation makes this question difficult to answer. The lack of significant differences between monkey S and controls in the pathways between LGN-V5/MT and pulvinar-V5/MT suggests either or both could support residual visual abilities.
Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand how the human innate immune system distinguishes between foreign and host DNA in the nucleus. A key player in this process is the innate immune sensor IFI16, which recognizes foreign double-stranded (ds) DNA and assembles into supramolecular signaling platforms to initiate an immune response. However, the mechanism by which IFI16 distinguishes between foreign and host DNA has remained unclear.

     A recent study used biochemical assays and single-molecule techniques to investigate how IFI16 recognizes foreign dsDNA in the nucleus. The researchers found that IFI16 scans along exposed foreign dsDNA in a one-dimensional manner, tracking long stretches of DNA to assemble into supramolecular signaling platforms. This scanning mechanism allows IFI16 to efficiently and selectively assemble into oligomers on foreign DNA.

     The study also showed that nucleosomes, which are the basic units of chromatin, act as barriers that prevent IFI16 from targeting host DNA. Nucleosomes directly interfere with the one-dimensional movements of IFI16, preventing it from binding to host DNA. This unique scanning-assisted assembly mechanism enables IFI16 to distinguish between foreign and host DNA in the nucleus.

     The researchers used time-resolved bulk and single-molecule fluorescence assays to demonstrate the scanning mechanism of IFI16. They found that IFI16 scans along exposed dsDNA to assemble into distinct clusters, and that chromatinization is sufficient to inhibit IFI16 from targeting host dsDNA for assembly. In vivo, this one-dimensional scanning mechanism allows a limited number of IFI16 molecules to allocate to large genomes of invading pathogens.

     The findings of this study provide new insights into the molecular mechanism of IFI16-mediated immune responses. The researchers propose that the one-dimensional diffusion-mediated assembly plays a key role in regulating the overall IFI16-mediated immune responses. The study highlights the importance of chromatinization in allowing IFI16 to distinguish between foreign and host DNA in the nucleus, and provides a unifying molecular mechanism that explains how IFI16 resolves central issues in initiating its foreign-dsDNA sensing pathways.
Summary
     A new technique called spatially targeted optical microproteomics (STOMP) has been developed to analyze the composition of small protein deposits in tissues. This method uses a combination of two-photon laser scanning microscopy, photochemical affinity labeling, and mass spectrometry to identify the proteins present in specific regions of interest (ROIs) within tissues. The technique does not require genetic manipulation and can be applied to various biological samples, including cell lines, primary cell cultures, ex vivo specimens, biopsy samples, and fixed post-mortem tissue.

     To perform STOMP, specimens are stained with fluorescent dyes or antibodies to visualize the ROIs, which are then soaked in a solution containing a photo-tag. The photo-tag is a molecule that forms covalent bonds with proteins when exposed to laser light. Confocal imaging and two-photon excitation are used to couple the photo-tag to all proteins within the ROI, allowing for the identification of proteins at a resolution of 0.67 Âµm in the xy-plane and 1.48 Âµm axially. After tissue solubilization, the photo-tagged proteins are isolated and identified by mass spectrometry.

     In a test case, STOMP was used to examine amyloid plaques in an Alzheimer's disease (AD) mouse model and a post-mortem AD case. The results confirmed known plaque constituents and discovered new ones, demonstrating the potential of STOMP to provide insights into the etiology of neurodegenerative diseases. The technique also has the potential to advance research in cell biology by allowing for the analysis of small features that are not amenable to biochemical purification.

     The photo-tagging procedure used in STOMP offers single-micron-scale three-dimensional resolution, which is an order of magnitude better than the current state of the art. The predominant protein photo-tagged in the STOMP sample was AÎ², which is not visible in the non-plaque experiment due to its low overall abundance. The total volume of tissue required for photo-tagging to obtain sufficient material for mass spectrometry analysis is a subjective matter and depends on various factors, including instrument sensitivity, fixation method, and sample complexity.

     The Frizzled3 (Fz3) gene plays a crucial role in the development of axons in cranial and spinal motor neurons. Research has shown that disruption of the Fz3 gene leads to defects in axonal growth in specific motor nerves, including the VIIth and XIIth cranial motor nerves, the phrenic nerve, and the dorsal motor nerve in fore- and hindlimbs. In Fz3-deficient limbs, dorsal axons stall at a precise location in the nerve plexus and do not reroute to other trajectories, unlike other axon path-finding mutants.

     The study found that affected motor neurons undergo cell death two days prior to the normal wave of developmental cell death, which coincides with innervation of muscle targets. This suggests that developing neurons with long-range axons are programmed to die unless their axons arrive at intermediate targets on schedule. The research also implicates planar cell polarity (PCP) signaling in motor axon growth and highlights the question of how PCP proteins, which form cell-cell complexes in epithelia, function in the dynamic context of axonal growth.

     In the limb, the absence of Fz3 leads to a specific stalling defect among lateral motor column (LMCL) axons that is distinct from the phenotypes produced by defects in other signaling systems active in limb axon guidance. Affected cranial and spinal motor neurons die shortly after their axons stall, providing in vivo evidence for the idea that as growing axons arrive at intermediate targets, essential survival signals are relayed to their cell bodies.

     The study investigated whether the primary cause of motor axon deficiency in Fz3-deficient mice is defective motor neuron differentiation, excessive motor neuron death, or defective axon growth and/or guidance. Since the majority of Fz3-deficient motor axons do not follow erroneous trajectories, aberrant path-finding is unlikely to be the primary cause. Instead, the research suggests that controlling axonal growth may be the only function of Fz3 in motor neurons.

     The study found that a discrete subset of motor neurons exhibits both axon growth defects and apoptotic cell death at E11.5-E12.5 in Fz3-deficient embryos, but not in Fz3-heterozygous controls. This suggests that Fz3 plays a critical role in regulating axonal growth and survival in motor neurons, and that its absence leads to premature cell death. The findings have implications for our understanding of PCP signaling in motor axon growth and development.

     Biologists have long believed that transposable elements (TEs) play a significant role in establishing gene regulatory networks in the early mouse embryo. TEs are repetitive DNA sequences that can jump from one location to another in the genome, and they have been co-opted by the embryonic and extraembryonic lineages of the early mouse embryo as enhancers. Enhancers are regions of DNA that increase the expression of nearby genes. However, there is limited evidence that TEs actually contribute to gene regulation.

     To investigate the role of TEs in gene regulation, researchers tested a set of long terminal repeat (LTR) TE families in mouse embryonic and trophoblast stem cells. They used a combination of epigenomic and transcriptomic data to identify TEs that were associated with tissue-specific gene expression programs. The results suggested that a large number of TEs were involved in establishing these gene expression programs.

     To confirm the regulatory relationships between TEs and genes, the researchers used genetic editing to remove individual TEs from the genome. They found that a subset of TEs was indeed important for gene regulation in early mouse development. However, a wider survey of TEs using CRISPR interference revealed that only a minority of TEs played significant roles in gene regulation.

     The study highlights the importance of functional experiments in evaluating the gene regulatory roles of TEs. It also suggests that TEs may be used to support lineage-specific expression of a subset of genes in early development. However, the results also indicate that many TEs play only minor, redundant, or no role in gene regulation, despite strong correlative evidence at the level of chromatin composition and conformation.

     The researchers used CRISPR interference to test the regulatory roles of multiple TE+ enhancers in embryonic stem cells. They found that TE+ enhancers may be used to support lineage-specific expression of a subset of genes in early development. However, the correlations observed may emerge in the absence of causal links between TEs and gene expression.

     To directly test for a functional role of TE+ enhancers in gene regulation, the researchers performed CRISPR-mediated genetic excision of selected TEs. They validated the relatively small effect of some TE+ enhancers on gene expression by performing additional genetic editing experiments. The study identifies a number of TEs that are important for driving expression of lineage-specific genes, and highlights the need for extensive genetic work to evaluate the proportion of TE+ enhancers that act in a redundant fashion with other regulatory elements.

     Biologists have long been fascinated by the evolution of C4 photosynthesis, a complex trait that has independently emerged in at least 60 plant lineages. C4 photosynthesis is a more efficient way for plants to convert sunlight into energy, but its evolution is not well understood. A new study has used a combination of data analysis and mathematical modeling to uncover the evolutionary paths that led to the development of C4 photosynthesis.

     The researchers conducted a meta-analysis of 18 lineages containing species that use C3, C4, or intermediate C3-C4 forms of photosynthesis. They then developed a novel Bayesian approach based on a hidden Markov model to predict how the C4 phenotype evolved. This approach allowed them to infer the likely evolutionary trajectories connecting phenotypes from sparsely sampled, highly stochastic data.

     The study found that the emergence of C4 photosynthesis is associated with diverse and flexible evolutionary paths that group into four major trajectories. The researchers also discovered that the order of C4 trait acquisition is flexible and driven by non-photosynthetic drivers. This means that the evolution of C4 photosynthesis was influenced by factors other than photosynthesis itself.

     The study suggests that early evolutionary events determined the downstream phenotypes of C4 sub-types by restricting lineages to independent pathways across phenotype space. The researchers propose that mechanistic diversity underlies the multiple evolutionary pathways generating C4 photosynthesis, which may be a key factor in facilitating the convergent evolution of complex traits.

     The findings of this study have implications for efforts to recapitulate the acquisition of C4 photosynthesis through genetic engineering of C3 species. The researchers suggest that including additional early traits in the analysis did not alter the predicted order of the original 16 traits, indicating that their conclusions are robust. Overall, this study provides new insights into the evolution of C4 photosynthesis and highlights the importance of considering non-photosynthetic drivers in the evolution of complex traits.

     Biomedical researchers have reengineered the ATPase active site of a bacterial protein called SpoIVA to mimic its ancestral GTPase activity. SpoIVA is a cytoskeletal protein that hydrolyzes ATP to form static polymers during the sporulation process in the bacterium Bacillus subtilis. The protein evolved from a TRAFAC class of P-loop GTPases, but the reason for the switch from GTP to ATP hydrolysis is unclear. By modifying the nucleotide-binding pocket of SpoIVA, the researchers created a functional GTPase, SpoIVAGTPase, which could hydrolyze both ATP and GTP. However, SpoIVAGTPase failed to polymerize in the presence of GTP, suggesting that the nucleotide base, not just the energy released from hydrolysis, is crucial for its function.

Further experiments showed that SpoIVAGTPase could only polymerize in the presence of ATP, indicating a specific requirement for the nucleotide base. The researchers also found that physiological levels of intracellular ATP, but not GTP, facilitate the necessary conformational changes in SpoIVA and SpoIVAGTPase. This suggests that the switch from GTP to ATP hydrolysis in SpoIVA may have been driven by the high levels of ATP present during the sporulation process.

The study reveals that the nucleotide base can play a critical role in specific biological functions, in addition to the energy released from hydrolysis. The researchers propose that the increased levels of ATP relative to GTP at the end of sporulation may have driven the change in nucleotide preference in SpoIVA. This finding has implications for our understanding of the evolution of protein function and the importance of nucleotide specificity in biological processes. The study provides new insights into the mechanisms underlying the formation of static polymers in bacteria and highlights the complex interplay between nucleotide hydrolysis and protein function.

     Biologists have long studied the Arabidopsis circadian clock, a complex system that regulates gene expression across the day-night cycle. While previous research has identified a multiple feedback loop circuit that generates the 24-hour rhythm, it remained unclear how robust the clock is in individual cells and how clock timing is coordinated across the plant. To address this question, researchers examined clock activity at the single-cell level in Arabidopsis seedlings over several days under constant environmental conditions.

     The study revealed robust single-cell oscillations, but these oscillations were desynchronized, meaning they occurred at different times in different cells. The researchers observed two waves of clock activity: one traveling down the root and the other up the root. They also found evidence of cell-to-cell coupling, particularly in the root tip, suggesting that cells communicate with each other to coordinate their clock activity.

     By analyzing the data, the researchers estimated the coupling strength between cells and found that it was strongest in the root tip. A simple model suggested that the observed period differences between cells, combined with cell-to-cell coupling, could generate the observed waves in clock gene expression. This finding reveals the spatial structure of the plant clock and suggests that, unlike the centralized mammalian clock, the Arabidopsis clock has multiple coordination points.

     The study's results also support a more decentralized model of clock coordination in plants, contradicting earlier studies that suggested cellular oscillations were uncoupled. The researchers used a reporter for the core clock gene CIRCADIAN CLOCK ASSOCIATED 1 (CCA1) to examine the dynamics of the Arabidopsis clock across the whole plant at the single-cell level over several days. They found that the coherent waves of gene expression suggested that the plant clock signal could be coupled, and estimated the coupling strength based on a technique developed for mammalian circadian cells.

     The study's findings reveal that the damping of rhythms at the whole-plant level is mainly due to desynchronization of oscillating single cells with different periods, rather than noise in gene expression or damping of individual oscillations. Overall, the research provides new insights into the structure and robustness of the plant circadian clock system, highlighting the importance of cell-to-cell communication in coordinating clock activity across the plant.

     Biomedical researchers investigated how the brain processes speech in noisy environments, specifically in adults with clinically normal hearing. In everyday life, speech from multiple speakers can overlap, making it difficult to focus on a single conversation. Normally, the brain uses a combination of fast processing and active listening to separate the attended speech from background noise. However, some adults with normal hearing struggle to follow conversations in noisy settings, despite having no apparent hearing impairments.

     The study analyzed data from over 100,000 patient records and found that about 10% of adults with clinically normal hearing visited a clinic due to reduced hearing, only to be told that their hearing was not the cause of their communication difficulties. To better understand this phenomenon, the researchers used a combination of behavioral and physiological tests to identify neural biomarkers associated with poor multi-talker speech perception.

     The study found that multi-talker speech intelligibility thresholds varied widely among adults with normal hearing, but could be predicted from neural phase-locking to frequency modulation (FM) cues measured using ear canal EEG recordings. By combining neural temporal fine structure processing, pupil-indexed listening effort, and behavioral FM thresholds, the researchers were able to account for 78% of the variability in multi-talker speech intelligibility.

     The study also identified disordered bottom-up and top-down markers of poor multi-talker speech perception, which could inform the design of next-generation clinical tests for hidden hearing disorders. The researchers used parallel psychophysical and neurophysiological tests to converge on a set of neural biomarkers that identify poor multi-talker speech intelligibility in adults with clinically normal hearing.

     The study's findings have implications for the development of new diagnostic measures and interventions for individuals who struggle to follow conversations in noisy social environments. The researchers present a simple battery of behavioral and physiological tests that can account for nearly 80% of the variability in a test of multi-talker speech intelligibility that does not involve linguistic cues. The study's results suggest that the strong association between psychophysical tests for FM detection and speech-in-noise intelligibility can be attributed, at least in part, to encoding of FM cues at early stages of auditory processing.

     Biomedical researchers have long faced a challenge in visualizing the microvascular network deep inside the living mammal due to tissue light scatter. However, the transparency of the mammalian eye provides a unique opportunity to study the microvessels of the retina, a part of the central nervous system, noninvasively. Despite the clarity of the eye, imperfections in its optics blur microscopic retinal capillaries and single blood cells flowing within, limiting early evaluation of microvascular diseases that originate in capillaries. To overcome this limitation, a team of researchers used 15 kHz adaptive optics imaging to noninvasively measure single-cell blood flow in the C57BL/6J mouse, a widely used research animal.

     The researchers employed ultrafast imaging and an automated analysis pipeline to measure the flow of millions of blood cells across the full spectrum of retinal vessel diameters, ranging from 3.2 to 45.8 micrometers. This was achieved without the need for surgery or contrast dye, enabling the measurement of flow rates that spanned four orders of magnitude, from 0.0002 to 1.55 microliters per minute. The study demonstrated the capability to image single blood cells noninvasively in the smallest to largest vessels of the mouse retinal circulation, using near-infrared backscatter without the need for foreign contrast agents.

     The findings of this study have significant implications for the understanding of microcirculation dynamics in the central nervous system and systemic microvascular health. The eye may serve as a window to supplement the understanding of microcirculation dynamics in the CNS, and the imaging capabilities developed in this study may be directly applicable to clinical approaches using the same instrumentation. The researchers also demonstrated the ability to image single blood cells in both tight and sparsely packed formations inside microvessels, enabling sub-micrometer precision when measuring the lumen diameters of retinal vessels. In total, 123 vessels in 19 mice were studied, providing a comprehensive understanding of single-cell blood flow in the smallest to largest vessels of the living mouse retina.

     Biomedical research has identified a new mechanism by which the body's immune cells respond to infection or environmental stress. When immune cells, such as macrophages, are challenged by pathogens or stress, they activate a complex called an inflammasome, which triggers an inflammatory response and a form of cell death called pyroptosis. A recent study has found that the disruption of glycolytic flux, the process by which cells break down glucose for energy, is a key trigger for inflammasome signaling and pyroptotic cell death.

     The researchers discovered that when glycolytic flux is disrupted, it leads to a decrease in NADH levels and an increase in mitochondrial reactive oxygen species (ROS) production. This signal is essential for the activation of the NLRP3 inflammasome, which is responsible for producing the pro-inflammatory cytokine IL-1Î². The study found that this signal can be rescued by adding products that restore NADH production.

     The researchers also found that the intracellular pathogen Salmonella typhimurium can disrupt glycolytic flux by taking up host-cell glucose, leading to the activation of the NLRP3 inflammasome and pyroptosis. This suggests that the body's immune cells can sense metabolic dysfunction or infection by intracellular pathogens through the disruption of glycolytic flux.

     The study's findings reveal an important inflammatory signaling network used by immune cells to respond to metabolic dysfunction or infection. The researchers suggest that innate immune cells sense perturbed metabolite production and flux through the glycolytic pathway, leading to the activation of the NLRP3 inflammasome and the initiation of inflammatory responses.

     The study's results also indicate that glycolytic perturbation is a mechanism by which innate immune cells sense altered homeostasis during S. typhimurium infection and induce a pro-inflammatory response via NLRP3 inflammasome formation and pyroptotic cell death. The researchers found that blocking glycolytic flux serves as a trigger for inflammation and cell death in macrophages, and that supplementation with the glycolytic product pyruvate can prevent inflammasome formation and reduce cell death.

     Overall, the study provides new insights into the mechanisms by which the body's immune cells respond to infection and environmental stress, and highlights the importance of glycolytic flux in the activation of the NLRP3 inflammasome and the initiation of inflammatory responses.

     Biomedical researchers have made a significant discovery about the structure and function of MreB, a protein found in bacteria. MreB is an actin-like protein that plays a crucial role in maintaining the shape of bacterial cells. Unlike other actin-like proteins, which form filaments with parallel protofilaments, MreB forms filaments with antiparallel protofilaments. This unique arrangement allows MreB to interact with the bacterial cell membrane and other membrane-bound components.

     The researchers used a combination of techniques, including crystallography and electron microscopy, to study the structure of MreB filaments. They found that the antiparallel arrangement of MreB protofilaments is essential for the protein's function in cell shape maintenance. In fact, deleting the N-terminal amphipathic helix of MreB or mutating the residues in the hydrophobic loop made the protein less prone to aggregation and reduced its ability to maintain cell shape.

     The researchers also used crystallography to study the structure of MreB in different nucleotide and polymerization states. They found that the protein undergoes conserved conformational changes as it forms antiparallel filaments. Additionally, they discovered that the antimicrobial agents A22/MP265 bind close to the bound nucleotide of MreB, preventing nucleotide hydrolysis and destabilizing the double protofilaments.

     The findings of this study provide new insights into the structure and function of MreB and its role in bacterial cell shape maintenance. The antiparallel arrangement of MreB protofilaments is unprecedented among actin-like proteins and ensures that each subunit can interact with the membrane and membrane-bound components of the elongasome. The study also sheds light on the mechanism of protofilament formation and the inhibitory mechanism of the antimicrobial agents A22/MP265. Overall, this research has significant implications for our understanding of bacterial cell biology and the development of new antimicrobial therapies.

     Biologists have long recognized the importance of invariant floral forms in ensuring reproductive success and robustness to natural perturbations. In Arabidopsis thaliana, a small flowering plant, petal number is consistently four. However, in the closely related species Cardamine hirsuta, petal number varies. To understand the genetic basis for this difference, researchers investigated the role of APETALA1 ( AP1 ), a pleiotropic floral regulator. They found that divergence in AP1 can account for the species-specific difference in petal number robustness between Arabidopsis and Cardamine hirsuta. This large effect of AP1 is explained by epistatic interactions, where Arabidopsis AP1 masks the phenotypic expression of quantitative trait loci ( QTL ) controlling petal number in Cardamine hirsuta, thereby conferring robustness. In contrast, Cardamine hirsuta AP1 fails to complement this function of Arabidopsis AP1, resulting in variable petal number. The researchers also discovered that upstream regulatory regions of AP1 contribute to this divergence. Furthermore, they found that variable petal number is maintained in Cardamine hirsuta despite sufficient standing genetic variation in natural accessions to produce plants with four-petalled flowers. This suggests that AP1 divergence likely exposed cryptic genetic variation in Cardamine hirsuta that contributes directly to maintaining variable petal number within natural accessions of the species. The study highlights the importance of AP1 as a gene of major effect in the evolutionary transition from a robust phenotype of four petals to a variable petal number in Cardamine hirsuta. The findings also suggest that there may be variability in the gene regulatory network controlling petal number in Arabidopsis that is hidden beneath the uniformity of wild-type development.
summary
     Biomedical research has identified an early event in the progression to type 2 diabetes: the misfolding of proinsulin in the pancreas. Proinsulin is a precursor to insulin, a hormone critical for metabolic homeostasis. Normally, proinsulin is folded correctly in the endoplasmic reticulum (ER) of pancreatic islet cells, forming three internal disulfide bonds. However, a subset of proinsulin molecules in healthy islets contains unpaired cysteine residues, which can lead to the formation of aberrant disulfide-linked complexes with other proinsulin molecules.

     Studies in human and rodent islets have shown that a perturbed ER folding environment can lead to the formation of non-native proinsulin complexes. In genetically obese mice with otherwise normal islets, disulfide-linked complexes of proinsulin are more abundant, and this increase tracks with the onset of islet insulin deficiency and diabetes. In leptin receptor-deficient mice, which are prone to obesity and diabetes, the abundance of disulfide-linked proinsulin complexes is further increased.

     Research has also identified specific cysteine residues, Cys(B19) and Cys(A20), as necessary and sufficient for the formation of proinsulin disulfide-linked complexes. Proinsulin Cys(B19)-Cys(B19) covalent homodimers are particularly stable and resistant to reductive dissociation, providing a structural basis for aberrant proinsulin complex formation.

     The study found that proinsulin misfolding is an early event associated with prediabetes, which worsens with Î²-cell dysfunction in type 2 diabetes. In islets from prediabetic mice, a large proportion of proinsulin molecules are entangled in disulfide-linked dimers and higher-order complexes. These findings suggest that proinsulin misfolding via disulfide-linked complexes is a key event in the progression to type 2 diabetes. Further research is needed to understand the mechanisms underlying proinsulin misfolding and its role in the development of type 2 diabetes.

Summary of the article: Entorhinal cortex receptive fields are modulated by spatial attention, even without movement

     Researchers have long been interested in the role of the entorhinal cortex, a region of the brain involved in spatial navigation and memory. One type of cell in this region, called grid cells, is known for its ability to precisely decode an animal's position in space. Recent studies have suggested that grid cells may play a broader role in mental operations, such as attention and perception. However, for this hypothesis to be true, grid cell activity must not be dependent on physical movement.

     To test this idea, scientists recorded the activity of individual cells in the entorhinal cortex of two rhesus macaque monkeys. The monkeys were trained to perform a task that required them to maintain central fixation while covertly attending to a small dot moving around a computer monitor. The researchers found that a significant population of entorhinal cells (14% of 141 neurons) exhibited spatially structured receptive fields, similar to those seen in grid cells. These cells were activated by the movement of the dot, even though the monkeys were not physically moving.

     This finding is significant because it suggests that grid cells in the entorhinal cortex do not rely on physical movement to function. Instead, their activity can be modulated by spatial attention, even in the absence of movement. This challenges the idea that grid cells are solely involved in navigation and supports the hypothesis that they play a more general role in mental operations.

     The researchers also compared their findings to previous studies on the hippocampus, a region of the brain involved in memory and spatial navigation. In contrast to the entorhinal cortex, grid-like representations were not observed in the hippocampus. This difference highlights the unique properties of grid cells in the entorhinal cortex and their potential role in attention and perception.

     Overall, the study provides strong evidence that the activation of cells in the entorhinal cortex with spatially structured firing fields does not require physical movement. This finding has implications for our understanding of the neural basis of attention and perception, and suggests that grid cells may play a more general role in mental operations than previously thought.

The task, designed for head-fixed mice, assesses perceptual and value-based decision-making. summary
     Biologists have long been interested in understanding how organisms adapt to their environments through modifications to their sensory systems. A recent study has shed light on this process by investigating how different species of weakly electric fish perceive and respond to electric communication signals. The researchers found that two distinct types of sensory receptors are responsible for detecting these signals: spiking receptors and oscillating receptors.

     Spiking receptors, found in species that can detect subtle variations in electric communication signals, encode signal waveform into spike-timing differences between sensory receptors. In contrast, oscillating receptors, found in species that are insensitive to waveform variation, produce spontaneously oscillating potentials and respond to electric pulses by resetting their phase. This results in transient synchrony among receptors that encodes signal timing and location, but not waveform.

     The researchers discovered that oscillating receptors are most sensitive to frequencies found only in the collective signals of groups of conspecifics, and this sensitivity is correlated with increased behavioral responses to these frequencies. This suggests that different perceptual capabilities correspond to different receptor physiologies. The study's findings provide the first evidence for sensory coding through oscillatory synchrony.

     The researchers also found that oscillating receptors are tuned to much lower frequencies than the peak power frequencies of conspecific electric organ discharges (EODs). Furthermore, these oscillation frequencies confer enhanced sensitivity to interpulse intervals (IPIs) that can only be produced by groups of fish. This suggests that oscillating receptors are specialized for detecting signals from a large group, whereas spiking receptors are specialized for detecting signals from individuals.

     To test this hypothesis, the researchers performed behavioral playback experiments in which they presented a single fish with a train of 10 conspecific EODs at constant IPIs. The results showed that species with oscillating receptors responded strongly to submillisecond IPIs within the range of enhanced receptor sensitivity, while species with spiking receptors did not. These findings suggest that oscillatory electrical activity in sensory receptors may represent an adaptation for detecting communication signals from large groups of fish.

     A new structure of the bacteriophage BPP-1 has been determined using cryo-electron microscopy at a resolution of 3.5 Ã. BPP-1 is a virus that infects and kills Bordetella species, which cause whooping cough in humans and respiratory diseases in other mammals. The virus has a T = 7l icosahedral capsid, approximately 670 Ã in diameter. The structure of the BPP-1 head reveals two distinct protein folds: a jellyroll fold for the cement protein (CP) and an HK97-like fold for the major capsid protein (MCP). Notably, the fold topology of MCP is non-circularly permuted from the previously known Johnson fold topology, which has been observed in other viral and cellular proteins. This new topology is likely the only feasible alternative to the old topology. The structure also shows that Î²-sheet augmentation and electrostatic interactions contribute to the formation of non-covalent chainmail in BPP-1, unlike the covalent inter-protein linkages of the HK97 chainmail. The termini of both CP and MCP are ideally positioned for DGR-based phage-display engineering. The atomic description of the BPP-1 head will enhance bioengineering efforts, reveal non-covalent molecular interactions conducive to stable bacteriophage capsid formation, and clarify evolutionary relationships of BPP-1 MCP and CP with proteins in other viruses. The structure of the BPP-1 head provides new insights into the diversity-generating retroelement (DGR) system, which is a naturally occurring phage-display system. The BPP-1 MCP and CP adopt two of the three characteristic folds of major virus lineages, jellyroll and HK-97/Johnson. The Johnson fold in BPP-1 MCP is non-circularly permuted compared to the previously known topology of the Johnson fold, which has been observed in the Siphoviridae and Myoviridae families of the three tailed-bacteriophage families. The BPP-1 structure is the first atomic model of a Podoviridae family member and provides a basis for understanding the evolutionary relationships of BPP-1 MCP and CP with proteins in other viruses.
Summary of the article "Principles of dengue virus evolvability derived from genotype-fitness maps in human and mosquito cells"

     Dengue virus (DENV) is a mosquito-borne pathogen that cycles between mosquito and mammalian hosts. To understand how DENV populations adapt to these different host environments, researchers used a combination of serial passage in human and mosquito cell lines and ultra-deep sequencing to estimate the fitness effects of all single-nucleotide variants in these populations. This approach allowed the researchers to determine the contributions of beneficial and deleterious mutations to the collective fitness of the population.

     The analysis revealed that the continuous influx of deleterious mutations counterbalances the effect of rare, host-specific beneficial mutations, shaping the path of adaptation. Beneficial mutations were found to preferentially map to intrinsically disordered domains in the viral proteome and cluster to defined regions in the genome. These phenotypically redundant adaptive alleles may facilitate host-specific DENV adaptation.

     The researchers used a technique called CirSeq to characterize the fitness landscapes of DENV populations adapting to human and mosquito cells by tracing individual allele trajectories for almost all possible single nucleotide variants across the DENV genome. This analysis revealed the influence of both positive and negative selection in shaping the evolutionary paths of DENV in these distinct cellular environments.

     Notably, many beneficial mutations were clustered at a few specific locations in the genome, suggesting adaptation relies on hotspots of host-specific selection. To further analyze these hotspots, the researchers mapped the allele fitness values on the three-dimensional structure of dengue protein E, a well-studied transmembrane protein that forms the outermost layer of the viral envelope.

     The findings of this study quantify the cost of deleterious mutation across the dengue genome and highlight how negative selection shapes diversity in viral populations. The results also suggest that adaptation relies on host-specific beneficial mutations that are clustered in specific regions of the DENV genome and enriched in regions of the proteome that exhibit structural flexibility.

     Importantly, the evolutionary constraints described in this study mirror trends observed across DENV and Zika strains, indicating that this simple system recapitulates key biophysical and biological constraints shaping long-term viral evolution. The study provides new insights into the principles of DENV evolvability and highlights the importance of understanding the interplay between beneficial and deleterious mutations in shaping the evolutionary paths of viral populations.

     Biomedical researchers used a technique called single-particle tracking with photoactivated localization microscopy (spt-PALM) to study the behavior of KRasG12D, an active mutant of the KRas protein, on cell membranes. They found that KRasG12D exists in three distinct states on the membrane: an immobile state, an intermediate mobility state, and a fast diffusion state. The immobile state occurs in domains approximately 70 nanometers in size, while the intermediate mobility state occurs in domains approximately 200 nanometers in size. The rest of the membrane supports fast diffusion of KRasG12D.

     The researchers observed that KRasG12D is continuously removed from the membrane via the immobile state and replenished to the fast state, similar to the process of Ras internalization and recycling. Importantly, the partitioning of KRasG12D into these three states remains invariant over a wide range of protein expression levels. This suggests that the diffusion and trafficking properties of KRasG12D are maintained in a homeostatic condition.

     Further analysis revealed that the intermediate and immobile states of KRasG12D are each associated with distinct membrane domains. The measured sizes of these domains are consistent with previous beliefs that nanoscopic membrane domains regulate Ras organization on the membrane. The researchers propose a new model to describe the membrane dynamics of KRasG12D, where nested membrane nanodomains dictate the diffusion and trafficking of the protein.

     The study provides new insights into how complex nanodomains organize on the membrane to dictate Ras diffusion and trafficking. The findings have implications for understanding the mechanisms of Ras multimer formation and signaling. Future experiments can build on these results to determine the molecular and structural identities of the Ras-associated membrane nanodomains and define the mechanisms of Ras multimer formation and signaling.

     Biologists have long sought to understand how Bone Morphogenetic Protein (BMP) signaling patterns the dorsoventral (DV) axis of vertebrates and invertebrates during embryonic development. A key aspect of this process is the formation of a BMP signaling gradient, which is crucial for proper patterning of the embryo. Previous research has proposed several mechanisms for BMP gradient formation, including a counter-gradient of BMP antagonists and ligand shuttling. However, a new study in zebrafish has shed light on the underlying mechanism.

     The researchers used a combination of quantitative imaging and mathematical modeling to investigate BMP gradient formation in zebrafish embryos. They developed a robust method to directly measure the BMP signaling gradient and integrated the results with a computational model to test hypotheses for gradient formation. The study found that the BMP signaling gradient in zebrafish is not established by a counter-gradient of BMP antagonists or by ligand shuttling, as previously thought.

     Instead, the researchers discovered a new mechanism, known as the source-sink mechanism, which relies on a restricted distribution of BMP antagonists acting as a sink that drives BMP flux dorsally and gradient formation. This mechanism was supported by measurements of Bmp2 diffusion, which showed that it is consistent with the source-sink model. The study suggests that the BMP signaling gradient in zebrafish is shaped by the interplay between BMP ligands and antagonists, with the antagonists acting as a sink to drive BMP flux and gradient formation.

     The findings of this study provide new insights into the mechanisms of BMP signaling and gradient formation during embryonic development. Biomedical researchers have identified a crucial component of the lysosome, a cellular organelle responsible for breaking down and recycling cellular waste. The lysosomal transporter MFSD1, a member of the major facilitator superfamily, plays a vital role in maintaining liver homeostasis. MFSD1 is a polytopic transmembrane protein that facilitates the export of low-molecular-weight catabolic end-products from lysosomes. However, the function and regulation of MFSD1 have remained poorly understood until now.

They found that GLMP is drastically reduced in lysosomes from Mfsd1 knockout mice, and vice versa. This suggests that MFSD1 and GLMP form a tightly linked complex that is essential for lysosomal function. Glmp knockout mice exhibited a similar phenotype to Mfsd1 knockout mice, further supporting the critical role of the MFSD1/GLMP complex in maintaining liver homeostasis.

summary
     Biologists have made significant progress in understanding the mechanism of kinesin, a motor protein essential for various cellular processes in eukaryotes. Kinesin's ability to transport cargo along microtubules, powered by ATP hydrolysis, is crucial for cell division, intracellular transport, and other vital functions. To gain insight into this process, researchers used cryo-electron microscopy (cryo-EM) to obtain high-resolution structures of the kinesin motor domain attached to microtubules in two different states: without nucleotides (ATP or ADP) and with ATP.

     The cryo-EM reconstructions revealed atomic models of the kinesin-microtubule complex at a resolution of 5-6 angstroms. By comparing these models with existing X-ray structures of ADP-bound kinesin, the researchers inferred a mechanistic scheme for kinesin's nucleotide-gated force-generation. This scheme proposes that microtubule attachment triggers a "clamshell" opening of the nucleotide cleft and the release of ADP. The binding of ATP then re-closes the cleft, tightly coupling to the translocation of cargo via kinesin's "neck linker" element.

     The researchers also identified a universally conserved "linchpin" residue in kinesin (N255) that mediates microtubule attachment and triggers the clamshell opening. This mechanism is reminiscent of the nucleotide-exchange steps in myosin and F1-ATPase motors. Furthermore, the study found that microtubules can regulate kinesin's affinities for ADP and ATP by modulating the conformation of the switch loops, providing a structural basis for kinesin's nucleotide-binding behavior on and off microtubules.

     The researchers also solved high-resolution cryo-EM reconstructions of the human monomeric kinesin motor domain attached to microtubules in two different chemical states, representing the motor before and after ATP binding. The no-nucleotide and ATP-bound models of the kinesin-microtubule complex revealed a clamshell-like closure of the nucleotide cleft triggered by ATP binding. Additionally, cryo-EM analysis of the ADP and ATP analog-bound states of a kinesin point mutant supported this hypothesis, revealing two previously unidentified binding modes of kinesin in which the motor domain attaches tightly to the microtubule but is rotationally mobile.
Biologists have long known that mitochondria, the energy-producing structures within cells, require mechanisms to remove damaged or misfolded proteins to maintain their essential functions. However, the quality control (QC) pathways for misfolded mitochondrial proteins have remained poorly understood. A recent study in the yeast Saccharomyces cerevisiae has shed light on a novel QC pathway at the mitochondrial outer membrane (MOM).

    Researchers identified two temperature-sensitive (ts-) proteins, sen2-1HAts and sam35-2HAts, which are degraded from the MOM by the ubiquitin-proteasome system. This process involves the attachment of ubiquitin molecules to the proteins, marking them for destruction. The ubiquitination of sen2-1HAts is mediated by the ubiquitin ligase Ubr1, while sam35-2HAts is primarily ubiquitinated by San1.

    The study found that the degradation of these proteins requires the involvement of chaperone proteins, specifically the SSA family of Hsp70s and the Hsp40 Sis1. This is the first evidence of chaperone involvement in mitochondrial-associated degradation (MAD). Additionally, the Cdc48-Npl4-Ufd1 AAA-ATPase complex, Doa1, and a mitochondrial pool of the transmembrane Cdc48 adaptor Ubx2 are also implicated in the degradation process.

    The researchers further investigated the role of ubiquitination and proteasomal degradation in the turnover of sam35-2HAts and sen2-1HAts. They found that ubiquitinated forms of these proteins accumulate in the mitochondrial pellet fraction under conditions of proteasome impairment. Deletion of the proteasome shuttling factor Ddi1 did not affect the degradation of these proteins, distinguishing MAD from ER-associated degradation (ERAD).

    Overall, this study reveals a unique QC pathway at the mitochondrial outer membrane that involves a combination of cytosolic and mitochondrial factors. This pathway is distinct from other cellular QC pathways and provides new insights into the mechanisms of protein quality control in mitochondria. The findings have implications for our understanding of mitochondrial function and dysfunction in various diseases.

     Biomedical researchers have long understood the importance of extracellular signal-regulated kinase (ERK) in regulating cell growth and tissue homeostasis. However, the mechanisms by which ERK activity is generated and propagated throughout tissues have remained unclear. To address this knowledge gap, a team of scientists used advanced imaging techniques to visualize ERK activity in the skin of living mice.

     The researchers created a special biosensor that allowed them to track ERK activity at the single-cell level in the epidermis, the outermost layer of the skin. Under normal conditions, the epidermis occasionally exhibited bursts of ERK activity that spread radially from cell to cell. This phenomenon, dubbed "Spatial Propagation of Radial ERK Activity Distribution" (SPREAD), was found to occur at a frequency that correlated with the rate of cell division in the epidermis.

     Further investigation revealed that SPREADs were stimulated by a compound called 12-O-tetradecanoylphorbol 13-acetate (TPA), which activates epidermal growth factor receptors (EGFRs) and their cognate ligands. The researchers also found that SPREADs were associated with the G2/M phase of the cell cycle, suggesting a link between ERK activity and cell proliferation.

     When the skin was wounded, ERK activation propagated as a "trigger wave" parallel to the wound edge, indicating that ERK activation can be superimposed on existing patterns of activity. These findings provide new insights into how cell proliferation and transient ERK activity are coordinated in a living tissue.

     The study's results have significant implications for our understanding of tissue homeostasis and the regulation of cell growth. By visualizing ERK activity in real-time, the researchers have shed light on the complex dynamics of growth signal activation and propagation in the epidermis. This knowledge may ultimately contribute to the development of new treatments for skin disorders and other conditions characterized by abnormal cell growth.

     Biologists have long sought to understand how living organisms synchronize their internal biological clocks with the 24-hour day-night cycle. A team of researchers has made significant progress in this area by studying the circadian clock of cyanobacteria, a type of photosynthetic bacteria. The study found that the cyanobacterial circadian clock can be driven both in living cells and in a laboratory dish using purified proteins.

     The researchers discovered that the phase of the circadian rhythm follows a simple scaling law in response to light-dark cycles, tracking midday across conditions with variable day length. This means that the clock can adapt to changes in the day-night cycle, such as those that occur with the seasons. The study also found that the core biochemical oscillator, comprised of the Kai proteins, behaves similarly when driven by metabolic pulses in a laboratory dish, indicating that the dynamics of the clock are intrinsic to these proteins.

     To understand how the circadian clock aligns with external cycles, the researchers developed a mathematical framework based on the instantaneous transformation of the clock cycle by external cues. This framework successfully predicts the behavior of the clock under many different cycling environments. The study suggests that the entrained behavior of the circadian clock can be captured quantitatively by a mathematical framework with a small number of parameters.

     The findings of this study have implications for understanding the molecular origins of key mathematical features of the circadian clock. The precisely defined nature of the cyanobacterial clock facilitates experiments that probe the molecular mechanisms underlying the clock's behavior. The researchers expect that the mathematical framework developed in this study will be useful for understanding the circadian clocks of other organisms as well. Overall, the study provides new insights into the mechanisms of the circadian clock and its ability to adapt to changing environmental conditions.
Biomedical research has long sought to understand the causes of sudden cardiac death (SCD), a leading cause of premature death in the general population. Genome-wide association studies have identified the transcription factor Meis1 as a risk factor for SCD. A recent study investigated the role of Meis1 in the development and function of sympathetic neurons, which play a crucial role in regulating heart rhythm.

The study found that inactivating Meis1 in the neural crest of mice led to altered sympatho-vagal regulation of cardiac rhythmicity in adults. This resulted in chronotropic incompetence and cardiac conduction defects, increasing the susceptibility to SCD. Further investigation revealed that Meis1 is a major regulator of sympathetic target-field innervation and that Meis1-deficient sympathetic neurons die by apoptosis from early embryonic stages to perinatal stages.

Meis1 was found to regulate the transcription of key molecules necessary for the endosomal machinery, and the traffic of Rab5+ endosomes was severely altered in Meis1-inactivated sympathetic neurons. This suggests that Meis1 interacts with various trophic factors signaling pathways during postmitotic neurons differentiation. The study also demonstrated that Meis1 acts independently of the well-studied network of transcription factors governing early specification of sympathetic neurons to orchestrate later phases of neuronal differentiation.

The loss of Meis1 function in early sympathetic neurons leads to an imbalanced sympatho-vagal regulation of cardiac functions in adult mice, resulting in increased susceptibility to SCD. Following specific inactivation of Meis1 in peripheral neurons, the traffic of Rab5+ early endosomes is impaired, and sympathetic target-field innervation is compromised. These findings complement previous research demonstrating Meis1's role in regulating neonatal cardiomyocytes mitosis, particularly following myocardial infarction.

The study highlights the importance of Meis1 in the development and function of sympathetic neurons and its potential role in mediating susceptibility to unexplained SCD. The results suggest that Meis1 is a novel actor in sympathetic neurons differentiation and that its dysfunction may contribute to the development of cardiac conduction defects and SCD. Further research is needed to fully understand the mechanisms underlying Meis1's role in cardiac regulation and its potential as a therapeutic target for preventing SCD.

     Biologists have long been fascinated by the diverse shapes and structures of biofilms, complex communities of bacteria that adhere to surfaces. Despite knowing the essential components required for biofilm formation, the mechanisms driving their surface morphology remained unclear. A recent study used a combination of experimental and theoretical approaches to investigate biofilm morphogenesis in Vibrio cholerae, a type of bacteria that causes cholera. The researchers found that mechanical instabilities, such as wrinkling and delamination, play a crucial role in shaping the surface morphology of growing biofilms. They also identified interfacial energy as a key driving force behind these mechanical instabilities, which dictate the creation and annihilation of new and existing interfaces within the biofilm.

     The study used a range of techniques, including mechanical measurements, theory and simulation, quantitative image analysis, and mutagenesis, to demonstrate the importance of mechanical instabilities in biofilm morphogenesis. The researchers showed that as biofilms grow, they can wrinkle and delaminate due to compressive stress, leading to changes in their surface topography. By manipulating the spatial distribution of mechanical stress, the researchers were able to alter the biofilm's surface morphology.

     The findings of this study have implications for our understanding of morphogenesis, the process by which tissues and organs take shape during development. The researchers suggest that the principles underlying biofilm morphogenesis, which rely on mechanical instabilities and interfacial energies, may be generally applicable to morphogenesis processes in higher organisms. This idea is supported by the fact that similar mechanical instabilities have been observed in other types of biofilms, such as those formed by Pseudomonas aeruginosa.

     The study also highlights the potential of biofilms as a model system for investigating the role of mechanical forces in morphogenesis. By manipulating the mechanical properties of biofilms, researchers may be able to discover new genes and compounds that can alter biofilm morphology and address biofilm-related problems. Overall, the findings of this study provide new insights into the complex processes underlying biofilm morphogenesis and suggest that mechanical forces play a critical role in shaping the surface morphology of these complex bacterial communities.
Biomedical research has long sought to understand the mechanisms underlying the development of epileptic neuronal networks following severe seizures. A recent study has shed light on the role of the transcription factor NRSF (neuron-restrictive silencer factor) in this process. NRSF is a crucial regulator of neuronal phenotype, and previous research has shown that interfering with its function can attenuate the development of epilepsy.

The study found that epilepsy-provoking seizures led to a significant increase in NRSF levels in the mature hippocampus, a region of the brain involved in seizure activity. However, surprisingly, this increase in NRSF levels resulted in the repression of only a small subset of potential NRSF target genes, approximately 10%. These repressed genes were rescued when NRSF binding to chromatin was blocked, indicating that NRSF is directly responsible for their repression.

Further investigation revealed that the subset of genes repressed by NRSF had mid-range binding frequencies to the repressor, making them sensitive to moderate fluctuations in NRSF levels. This dynamic and selective regulation of NRSF target genes may play a critical role in influencing neuronal properties in both pathological and physiological contexts.

The study used large-scale transcriptome arrays, qPCR validation, chromatin immunoprecipitation (ChIP), and gene set enrichment analysis (GSEA) to examine the basis of NRSF-dependent gene expression changes during epileptogenesis. The results showed that NRSF-regulated hippocampal genes included ion channels, receptors, calcium-related molecules, and other transcription factors that govern neuronal function and plasticity. These findings suggest that the repression of these genes by NRSF may contribute to the pathogenesis of epilepsy.

The key findings of this study are that NRSF contributes to epileptogenesis by regulating a group of genes that critically influence neuronal function, and that only a small subset of potential NRSF target genes are actually repressed by the transcription factor in the context of seizure-induced epilepsy. The study also highlights the importance of dynamic and selective regulation of NRSF target genes in influencing neuronal properties in both pathological and physiological contexts.

     The mammalian olfactory system processes sensory information through parallel pathways formed by mitral and tufted cells in the olfactory bulb. To understand how these parallel pathways maintain or enhance the encoding of specific stimulus features, researchers investigated the differences in activity-dependent lateral inhibition between mitral and tufted cells in mice. The study found that mitral and tufted cells have distinct lateral inhibitory circuits that reflect differences in the activation of deep and superficial granule cells. These circuit-level differences allow mitral and tufted cells to best discriminate odors in separate concentration ranges, indicating that segregating information about different ranges of stimulus intensity may be an important function of these parallel sensory pathways.

     The olfactory system is similar to other sensory systems, such as the visual system, in that it uses parallel pathways to process sensory information. In the retina, feature selectivity in each ganglion cell type emerges due to its connectivity to specific bipolar and amacrine cell types. Similarly, in the olfactory system, differences in odor-evoked responses in mitral and tufted cells emerge due to differences in connectivity to olfactory sensory neurons and external tufted cells. The combination of activity-dependent lateral inhibition at both low and intermediate rates enables tufted and mitral cells to collectively encode odors better than either population alone and supports novel computations that are unlikely to occur with a single neuron type.

     The proposed mechanism of activity-dependent lateral inhibition in mitral and tufted cells involves the activation of deep and superficial granule cells. Simulations show that these circuit-level differences allow mitral and tufted cells to best discriminate odors in separate concentration ranges. This suggests that segregating information about different ranges of stimulus intensity may be an important function of these parallel sensory pathways. The study provides new insights into how local circuits support feature selectivity in parallel streams and highlights the importance of understanding the mechanisms behind parallel processing in sensory systems.
Summary
     The M2 muscarinic receptor is a prototypic model of allostery in G protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs). However, the molecular and supramolecular determinants of allostery in this receptor are not well understood. To investigate this, researchers compared monomers and oligomers of the M2 muscarinic receptor to identify the allosteric properties gained in oligomers. Allosteric interactions were monitored using a FRET-based sensor and pharmacological assays involving mutants engineered to prevent intramolecular effects.

     The study found that allosteric effects in monomers were exclusively negative and primarily derived from intramolecular electrostatic repulsion between the allosteric and orthosteric ligands. In contrast, allosteric effects in oligomers could be positive or negative, depending on the allosteric-orthosteric pair, and arose from interactions within and between the constituent protomers. The complex behavior of oligomers is characteristic of muscarinic receptors in myocardial preparations.

     The researchers used molecular dynamics simulations to examine electrostatic, steric, and conformational determinants of allostery at the atomic level. The simulations started from the crystal structure of the monomeric M2 receptor occupied by the agonist iperoxo (4MQS). The ligand and accessory proteins were removed, and conformations of the receptor were sampled from a canonical ensemble accessible within a period of 30 ns.

     The study provides new insights into the molecular and supramolecular determinants of allostery in the M2 muscarinic receptor. The findings suggest that the complex behavior of oligomers is a key feature of muscarinic receptors in myocardial preparations. The study also highlights the importance of considering the oligomeric nature of GPCRs in understanding their allostery. The results have implications for the development of new therapeutic strategies targeting GPCRs.

     Biomedical researchers have identified a crucial role for the enzyme SETD2 in the repair of DNA double-strand breaks (DSBs) and the activation of the p53-mediated checkpoint. SETD2 is a histone-modifying enzyme that trimethylates histone H3 lysine 36 (H3K36me3), a chromatin state that coordinates the DNA damage response. The study found that SETD2 is necessary for the activation of the ATM protein, a key regulator of the DNA damage response, following DNA damage. Additionally, SETD2 is required for the formation of RAD51 presynaptic filaments, which are essential for homologous recombination repair of DSBs.

The researchers used reporter assays to demonstrate that SETD2 is necessary for the DNA damage response (DDR) and that depletion of SETD2 impairs ATM activation and delays the recruitment of 53BP1 to sites of DSBs. They also found that SETD2 is required for the recruitment of RAD51 to resected DNA 5' ends and that SETD2-depleted cells show reduced homology-directed repair of DSBs.

SETD2 mutations have been found in several cancers, including clear cell renal cell carcinoma (ccRCC). The study found that SETD2-mutant ccRCC cells displayed impaired DNA damage signaling and failed to activate p53, a master guardian of the genome that is rarely mutated in ccRCC. Despite the persistence of DNA lesions, SETD2-deficient cells showed decreased cell survival after DNA damage.

The researchers propose that SETD2 provides a chromatin bookmarking instrument that facilitates signaling and repair of DSBs. They suggest that the loss of SETD2 may afford an alternative mechanism for the inactivation of the p53-mediated checkpoint without the need for additional genetic mutations in TP53. This study provides new insights into the role of SETD2 in the DDR and highlights the importance of chromatin states in maintaining genome integrity. Further studies are needed to fully elucidate the mechanistic basis of the SETD2 impact on the DDR.
