PDAC is a common and aggressive form of pancreatic cancer, often driven by mutations in the Kras gene. The study used a mouse model to suppress Arid1a in adult acinar cells with oncogenic Kras mutations and found that this led to rapid and irreversible reprogramming of acinar cells into mucinous PDAC precursor lesions. In contrast, silencing Arid1a during embryogenesis, concurrent with KrasG12D activation, resulted in retention of acinar cell fate. These findings suggest that Arid1a acts as a critical modulator of Kras-dependent changes in acinar cell identity.

Suppression of Arid1a in adult acinar cells with oncogenic Kras mutations accelerated acinar-to-ductal reprogramming and specification of mucinous pancreatic cancer precursor lesions, which could not be reversed by restoring endogenous Arid1a expression. This indicates that Arid1a can provide a potent barrier to Kras-driven acinar-to-ductal metaplasia and early-stage pancreatic neoplasia. The study also found that Arid1a loss enables a non-reversible reprogramming of cell fate that is dependent on cellular and genetic context.

The researchers sought to understand the mechanism by which Arid1a regulates pancreas cell identity. They found that perturbation of the SWI/SNF chromatin-remodeling complex impacts the expression of SWI/SNF target genes. This implies that genomic areas rendered accessible by Arid1a harbor elements involved in preventing acinar-to-ductal trans-differentiation in the context of mutant Kras, a critical rate-limiting step in Kras-driven pancreatic neoplasia. The study's transcriptional and chromatin profiling data suggest that Arid1a disruption, in the presence of oncogenic Kras, reduces the chromatin accessibility of key transcription factors to the enhancers of genes needed to sustain acinar cell fate.

These findings collectively suggest that acute loss of Arid1a in the setting of a pre-existing Kras mutation rapidly perturbs the identity and function of pancreatic acinar cells, enhancing their sensitivity to metaplastic and PanIN-inducing signals stemming from oncogenic Kras. The study demonstrates that Arid1a depletion can modulate the early stages of pancreatic neoplasia in a context-specific manner that depends on the timing of Arid1a inactivation and the presence of oncogenic Kras.
Biomedical research has shown that social networks play a crucial role in buffering the effects of stress in many species. However, the absence of a social network can also be stressful. A recent study examined the effects of social isolation on neuroendocrine responses in mice and found that it alters the intrinsic properties of neurons in the paraventricular nucleus of the hypothalamus (PVN) in a sexually dimorphic fashion.

The study found that isolating preadolescent female mice from their littermates for less than 24 hours increased the first spike latency (FSL) and decreased the excitability of corticotropin-releasing hormone (CRH) neurons. In contrast, age-matched male mice did not exhibit these changes. Furthermore, subjecting either male or group-housed female mice to acute physical stress, such as swimming, increased FSL in both groups.

However, the increase in FSL following social isolation or acute physical stress was blocked by the glucocorticoid synthesis inhibitor, metyrapone, and mimicked by exogenous corticosterone. This suggests that the increase in FSL results in a decrease in the excitability of CRH neurons. The study's findings demonstrate that social isolation, but not acute physical stress, has sex-specific effects on PVN CRH neurons.

The researchers used acute brain slices to examine the synaptic and intrinsic properties of identified CRH neurons in the PVN. They found that social isolation altered the biophysical properties of these neurons in female mice, but not in male mice. Specifically, social isolation increased FSL and decreased neuronal excitability in female mice, while acute physical stress increased FSL in both male and female mice.

The study also found that social isolation occluded the effects of acute physical stress on female mice, but had no effect on FSL in male mice. This suggests that female mice may interpret social isolation as a stress, while male mice do not. The study's findings have important implications for our understanding of the sex-specific effects of social isolation on neuroendocrine responses and highlight the need for further research in this area.
Biologists have long studied the protein hemoglobin (Hb) to understand how it adapts to different environments and body sizes in mammals. Hb plays a crucial role in delivering oxygen to tissues, and its ability to bind and release oxygen is influenced by two key factors: affinity and cooperativity. Affinity refers to how strongly Hb binds to oxygen, while cooperativity describes how the binding of one oxygen molecule affects the binding of subsequent molecules.

    Researchers have previously thought that only Hb affinity determined molecular adaptations in vertebrates. However, a new study suggests that both affinity and cooperativity are important for optimizing tissue oxygen delivery. The study used a mathematical model to analyze the relationship between cooperativity and affinity in Hb, creating a "biophysical Hill landscape." This landscape revealed that mammalian Hb cooperativity values all fall on a ridge of maximum cooperativity, allowing for fine-tuning of oxygen delivery to meet the distinct needs of different tissues.

    The study found that the maximum cooperativity point of Hb has been tuned during evolution to satisfy the particular physiological requirements of different tissues for oxygen supply. For example, Hb in small animals with high metabolic rates has lower affinity for oxygen than in large animals, reflecting the adaptive adjustment of tissue oxygen unloading by Hb. The study also found that the greater an animal's metabolic rate, the higher the observed maximum Hill cooperativity value of oxygen binding by Hb.

    Furthermore, an inverse linear correlation was found between the metabolic rate and Hb affinity. This means that as an animal's metabolic rate increases, its Hb affinity decreases, allowing for more efficient oxygen delivery to tissues. The study's findings provide new insights into the mechanism underlying body-size-related adaptation of mammalian Hb and highlight the importance of both affinity and cooperativity in optimizing tissue oxygen delivery. The results have significant implications for our understanding of how Hb adapts to different environments and body sizes in mammals.

     Malaria infection begins when Plasmodium sporozoites are injected into the skin of a mammalian host by an Anopheles mosquito. To understand how sporozoites locate and enter a blood vessel, researchers used intravital microscopy in mice to study sporozoite motility and their interaction with dermal blood vessels. The study found that sporozoites exhibit two types of motility: 'avascular motility' and 'perivascular motility'. Avascular motility is characterized by high speed and less confinement, allowing sporozoites to move freely in areas far from blood vessels. In contrast, perivascular motility is more constrained and occurs when sporozoites are near blood vessels.

     The researchers observed that the curvature of sporozoite tracks engaging with vasculature optimizes contact with dermal capillaries. Imaging of sporozoites with mutations in key adhesive proteins highlighted the importance of the sporozoite's gliding speed and its ability to modulate adhesive properties for successful exit from the inoculation site. The study found that mutant sporozoites with modest changes in motility pattern or speed had dramatic decreases in blood vessel invasion and infectivity. This suggests that exit from the inoculation site presents an important barrier for the sporozoite and is therefore an opportune time to intervene.

     The researchers used intravital microscopy to visualize sporozoites over the first 120 minutes after inoculation and found significant changes in their motility during this time frame. They also rendered sporozoites fluorescent to study the function of surface proteins in dermal parasite motility and blood vessel recognition. The study found that CSÎN sporozoites spent more time engaging with blood vessels but were unable to enter the blood circulation. In contrast, TRAP-VAL sporozoites had a slow gliding speed that significantly decreased the volume of tissue explored, resulting in a reduced ability to encounter blood vessels.

     The gliding motility of Plasmodium sporozoites is essential for their infectivity, and previous studies have observed sporozoite motility in the dermis. However, this study provides the first in-depth analysis of sporozoite motility over time. The findings suggest that sporozoites in the dermis exhibit two types of motility and that their motility can change in response to environmental signals encountered by the sporozoite. This study refines our understanding of how the sporozoite navigates through and out of the dermis and highlights the importance of modulating adhesive properties and gliding speed for successful exit from the inoculation site.

     Biologists have long known that cells are organized into functional modules, which are groups of components that work together to perform specific tasks. These modules are crucial for cell function, but they are not always conserved across different species, suggesting that cells can evolve to perform the same functions with different proteins. A recent study investigated how cells adapt to the loss of a key component of the polarity module in budding yeast, a type of fungus. The researchers deleted the BEM1 gene, which is essential for cell polarization, and allowed the cells to evolve over 1,000 generations.

     Initially, the cells without BEM1 showed a rapid increase in fitness, followed by a slow recovery to nearly 90% of the fitness of their BEM1-containing ancestors. Genome sequencing and monitoring of cell polarization revealed a common evolutionary trajectory, with a fixed sequence of adaptive mutations that improved cell polarization by inactivating proteins. This suggests that cells can be robust to physiologically destructive perturbations and that recovery by gene inactivation can lead to rapid divergence in the parts list for cell biologically important functions.

     The study found that the cells adapted to the loss of BEM1 by following a reproducible evolutionary trajectory, which could be largely explained by the interactions among the mutated genes. This led to a substantial recovery in the speed and accuracy of cell polarization. The researchers also observed that bem1â cells often contained more than one polarity site, suggesting that the positive feedback that ensures a single site of polarization is weaker.

     The deletion of BEM1 allowed the cells to reveal alternative, more evolutionarily ancient polarization modules. The study's results demonstrate that the multiple layers of regulation that allow cells to polarize rapidly and accurately also make it possible for them to adapt to strong perturbations. Under strong selective pressure, the larger target size for mutations that inactivate proteins favors evolutionary trajectories that remove components from modules over those that quantitatively alter their properties. This suggests that cells can evolve to perform the same functions with different proteins, and that recovery by gene inactivation can lead to rapid divergence in the parts list for cell biologically important functions.
Biomedical research has long sought to understand the neural mechanisms underlying economic decision-making. A recent study investigated the neural basis of economic commitment in humans, focusing on the role of the medial prefrontal cortex (mPFC) in integrating value signals over time. In the study, participants made successive choices between goods or gambles with lasting consequences, such as investing in a house or choosing a partner. The researchers found that the rostral medial prefrontal cortex (rmPFC) encoded stimulus value only when acceptance or rejection was deferred into the future, suggesting a role in integrating value signals over time.

In contrast, the dorsal anterior cingulate cortex (dACC) encoded stimulus value only when participants rejected or deferred accepting a prospect. Notably, dACC BOLD signals reflected two decision biases: a tendency to defer commitments to later and a tendency to weight potential losses more heavily than gains. These biases paradoxically maximized reward in the task. The findings offer new insights into the pressures that shape economic decisions and the computation of value in the mPFC.

Previous studies have examined the neural mechanisms of economic behavior by offering participants a succession of independent gambles or unrelated consumer choices. However, these studies did not account for the lasting consequences of economic decisions. The current study addressed this limitation by devising a task in which decisions involved commitment to an asset with enduring financial consequences. The researchers aimed to understand the computational mechanisms by which economic commitments are made in humans and to investigate their neural implementation in the reward circuitry of the mPFC.

The study used a multi-alternative choice task in which decisions had financial ramifications that persisted over prolonged episodes and could not be reversed. In half of the blocks, participants had to choose between accepting and deferring acceptance of a prospect. The results showed that the dACC encoded value when a prospect was excluded, while the rmPFC encoded value only during deferral. Joint consideration of the behavioral data and dACC activity allowed the researchers to pinpoint two pressures that shaped decisions in the task: a bias to defer until a later date and a bias to weight unfavorable options more heavily. These findings provide fresh insights into the neural mechanisms of economic decision-making and the computation of value in the mPFC.

This effect was lost when paraspeckles were disrupted. In contrast, the insertion of a single antisense Alu had only a weak effect. Using real-time video-microscopy, the researchers showed that IRAlu drove a circadian expression of EGFP protein.

     The study suggests that paraspeckles control circadian gene expression at a post-transcriptional level due to their circadian expression. The researchers propose that paraspeckle bodies can rhythmically retain RNAs in the nucleus, leading to a rhythmic expression of the corresponding gene. This mechanism provides an additional layer of gene regulation by sequestering mature mRNAs within the nucleus.

     Previous studies have shown that two protein components of paraspeckles, NONO and SFPQ, display a circadian expression pattern in primary cultures of pituitary cells and a rat pituitary cell line. Furthermore, the disruption of paraspeckles led to a loss of rhythmic pattern in a few circadian genes, suggesting that paraspeckle retention plays a relevant role in circadian gene expression. The study highlights the importance of post-transcriptional circadian regulation in determining oscillations at the mRNA level.

They found that paraspeckles were rhythmically expressed in the cell line and that the insertion of IRAlu into the 3'-UTR of the EGFP gene led to a rhythmic circadian nuclear retention of the EGFP mRNA. The study provides new insights into the mechanisms of circadian gene expression and highlights the role of paraspeckles in controlling gene expression at a post-transcriptional level.

     Biomedical research has long recognized the importance of presynaptic voltage-gated calcium channels (VGCCs) in converting electrical signals into chemical communication. However, the molecules in the active zone that regulate VGCC function have remained poorly understood. A recent study has shed light on the role of Munc13, an active-zone protein essential for exocytosis, in controlling VGCC function.

     The study found that Munc13 interacts with VGCCs via a pair of basic residues in its C2B domain. This interaction is crucial for VGCC function, particularly during high-frequency action potential bursts and single action potential stimuli. The researchers demonstrated that eliminating this interaction by removing Munc13 or replacing it with a Munc13 C2B mutant altered VGCC's response to and recovery from high-frequency action potential bursts and altered calcium influx from single action potential stimuli.

     The study's findings have significant implications for understanding how synaptic transmission is controlled. Munc13's ability to modulate VGCC function on millisecond to second time scales suggests that synaptic throughput can be actively controlled by VGCC-active-zone protein interactions. The researchers pinpointed a critical interaction site to two basic residues within the C2B domain of Munc13, which is orthogonal to the potential membrane-interacting loops of this domain.

     To test the significance of the Munc13-VGCC interaction, the researchers examined the impact of removing Munc13 on action potential-driven calcium influx in nerve terminals. They found that expression of an shRNA targeting the major cortical isoforms Munc13-1 and Munc13-2 led to a 94% or greater depletion of this protein at nerve terminals, resulting in complete block of exocytosis.

     The study's results suggest that Munc13's interaction with VGCCs is essential for VGCC channel reactivation in nerve terminals. The researchers conclude that this interaction site in Munc13's C2B domain mediates VGCC channel reactivation, highlighting a novel form of synaptic modulation. The findings have broad implications for understanding how synaptic transmission is controlled and how Munc13 impacts information transfer at nerve terminals.
Biologists have long been fascinated by the intricate shapes of plant cells, particularly the puzzle-shaped cells found in the epidermis of many plant species. These cells, characterized by their lobes and indentations, have been observed in various plant organs and species, but their functional benefit has remained unclear. A new study proposes that these complex cell shapes serve as an effective strategy to reduce mechanical stress in the cell wall of the epidermis.

The researchers suggest that in tissues that grow isotropically, or in all directions, puzzle-shaped cells emerge to prevent the formation of large, isodiametric cells that would bulge under the stress produced by turgor pressure. Turgor pressure is the force exerted by water within plant cells, which can reach up to 10 bar in Arabidopsis leaf cells and up to 50 bar in specialized cells such as stomata.

To test this hypothesis, the researchers analyzed data from various plant organs and species, including Arabidopsis thaliana, and used simulation models to show that a mechanism actively regulating cellular stress plausibly reproduces the development of epidermal cell shape. The results suggest that mechanical stress is a key driver of cell-shape morphogenesis.

The study proposes that the puzzle-shaped cells seen in the epidermis of many plant species emerge from a mechanism that evolved to limit mechanical stress in tissues that grow isotropically, such as the epidermis of leaves and cotyledons. Finite Element Method (FEM) analysis of 3D pressurized cells shows that cell shape influences the direction and magnitude of mechanical stress exerted on the cell wall. When an epidermal cell becomes large in two directions, stress is greatly increased.

In contrast, puzzle-shaped cells with lobes and indentations provide a solution to this problem by avoiding large open areas in the cell and the high stresses they induce. The researchers found a strict correspondence between fruit shape and puzzle-shaped epidermal cells, which fits the prediction of their model that puzzle shapes are required to allow cells to enlarge in isotropically growing tissues, but are not required in elongated organs.

The study's findings suggest that plant cells regulate their shape to prevent their LEC, a proxy for stress, from becoming too large. The researchers propose a different function for the puzzle shape, that it is an adaptation to a developmental constraint related to the mechanical forces that act on turgid plant cells that reside in the epidermis.
Summary
     Biologists have long sought to understand how human cells accurately assemble the complex molecular machinery needed to remove non-coding regions (introns) from pre-mRNA molecules and connect the remaining coding regions (exons). A crucial step in this process is the assembly of pre-spliceosomes, which are composed of various subcomplexes that work together to facilitate splicing. Recent research has shed light on how these subcomplexes interact with each other and with the pre-mRNA molecule to promote accurate and efficient splicing.

     The study found that the U1 subcomplex, which binds to the 5' splice site of an intron, plays a key role in recruiting the U2 subcomplex to the branch site/3' splice site of the upstream intron. Interestingly, the presence of U1 bound to the 5' splice site of the next intron significantly increases the rate and efficiency of U2 recruitment, suggesting a synergistic effect between cross-intron interactions. This synergy is thought to be important for promoting correct and efficient splicing of multi-intron pre-mRNAs.

     The researchers used a technique called CoSMoS to observe the dynamics of spliceosomal subcomplexes on individual pre-mRNA molecules in human cell extracts. They found that the binding of U1 to the 5' splice site is largely unaffected by the presence of U2 binding sites positioned either downstream or upstream of the 5' splice site. This suggests that pre-spliceosomes can form on multi-intron pre-mRNAs through at least two different pathways: direct cross-intron interactions or cross-exon interactions, where the exons flanking an intron are first defined, followed by cross-intron interactions between adjacent cross-exon complexes.

     The study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying accurate and efficient splicing. The findings suggest that the collaboration between flanking 5' splice sites is a major contributor to exon inclusion and that this collaboration dramatically increases the rate of stable U2 recruitment during pre-spliceosome formation. This research has important implications for our understanding of the complex process of splicing and may ultimately contribute to the development of new therapeutic strategies for genetic disorders related to splicing defects.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have discovered a novel folding assistance system for membrane proteins that operates on the cytosolic side of the endoplasmic reticulum (ER). This system relies on ubiquitination, a process where a molecule called ubiquitin is attached to a specific lysine on the protein. In the case of the Wnt signaling coreceptor LRP6, ubiquitination on a specific lysine, Lys-1403, helps the protein fold correctly. If folding is successful, the ubiquitin is removed by a deubiquitinating enzyme called USP19, allowing the protein to exit the ER. This process is similar to the glucosylation-deglucosylation cycle that occurs in the ER lumen during the calnexin/calreticulin folding cycle.

     The researchers found that if LRP6 fails to fold correctly, it is polyubiquitinated on other lysines and targeted for degradation. However, if the protein is successfully folded, it undergoes palmitoylation, a process that helps it exit the ER. This ubiquitin-dependent folding system is not unique to LRP6 and appears to operate on other membrane proteins, including the cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator (CFTR) and the anthrax toxin receptor 2. These proteins are involved in severe human diseases, such as cystic fibrosis and hyaline fibromatosis syndrome.

     The researchers propose that the ubiquitination of LRP6 on Lys-1403 interacts with a ubiquitin-binding protein that acts as a chaperone, providing the protein with time to fold correctly. If folding is successful, the ubiquitin is removed by USP19, allowing the protein to exit the ER. This discovery highlights the importance of ubiquitination in the folding of membrane proteins and provides new insights into the regulation of protein quality control in the ER. The ubiquitin-dependent folding system may have implications for the treatment of diseases caused by misfolded membrane proteins.
Biomedical research has shown that sex differences in cognitive processes can contribute to individual differences in vulnerability to neuropsychiatric disorders. A key area of interest is value-based decision making, where sex differences have been proposed but often result in minimal differences in overall performance between sexes. However, computational tools can uncover latent variables that define different decision-making approaches, even in animals with similar correct performance.

     In a study using mice, researchers examined sex differences in the latent variables underlying behavior in a classic value-based decision-making task, the restless two-armed bandit. While male and female mice had similar accuracy, they achieved this performance via different patterns of exploration. Male mice tended to make more exploratory choices overall, often getting "stuck" in exploration once they started. In contrast, female mice explored less but learned more quickly during exploration.

     The results suggest that sex exerts a stronger influence on decision making during periods of learning and exploration than during stable choices. This is significant because exploration during decision making is altered in people diagnosed with addictions, depression, and neurodevelopmental disabilities. The neural mechanisms of exploration may be a highly translational avenue for conferring sex-modulated vulnerability to neuropsychiatric diagnoses.

     The study used a combination of computational modeling approaches to characterize sex differences in a canonical explore/exploit task. While males and females had similar performance, they used different latent explore-exploit strategies to learn about the dynamic environment. Post-hoc win-stay lose-shift analyses revealed that female mice displayed more win-stay behaviors, indicating that they were more likely than males to repeat behaviors that produced reward on the previous trial.

     The findings highlight the importance of considering sex differences in cognitive processes, particularly during periods of learning and exploration. The results have implications for understanding the neural mechanisms of exploration and how they contribute to sex-modulated vulnerability to neuropsychiatric disorders. Further research is needed to elucidate the complex interactions between sex, explore-exploit states, and reward outcomes that influence animal choices.
Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand the process of endoplasmic reticulum-associated protein degradation (ERAD), where misfolded or damaged membrane proteins are extracted from the endoplasmic reticulum (ER) and degraded by the proteasome. A crucial step in this process is the movement of hydrophobic transmembrane (TM) segments from the phospholipid bilayer into the cytosol, often accompanied by hydrophilic and folded ER luminal protein parts. However, the mechanism by which this occurs has remained unclear.

    A recent study used a reconstituted system with purified proteins from the yeast Saccharomyces cerevisiae to investigate the role of the ubiquitin ligase Doa10 in ERAD. The results show that Doa10 acts as a retrotranslocase, facilitating the extraction of membrane proteins from the ER. The study found that the TM segment of a substrate protein interacts with the membrane-embedded domain of Doa10, allowing it to passively move into the aqueous phase. In contrast, luminal substrate segments cross the membrane in an unfolded state, with their unfolding occurring on the luminal side of the membrane due to the action of the cytoplasmic Cdc48 ATPase.

    This study provides new insights into the ERAD process, revealing how a membrane-bound retrotranslocase cooperates with the Cdc48 ATPase in the extraction of proteins with folded luminal domains. The findings have implications for our understanding of protein quality control pathways in the ER and other organelles, where membrane proteins are removed from the organelle for proteasomal degradation in the cytosol. The study also sheds light on the long-standing question of whether luminal domains are moved across the membrane in a folded state or if they are unfolded prior to retrotranslocation.

    The researchers used a reconstituted system to investigate ERAD mediated by the ubiquitin ligase Doa10 from S. cerevisiae. Doa10 is a 150 kDa protein with 14 transmembrane segments, and the study shows that it is a retrotranslocase that facilitates the extraction of membrane proteins from the ER. The results demonstrate that Doa10 cooperates with the Cdc48 ATPase in the extraction of proteins with folded luminal domains, providing new insights into the ERAD process. The study's findings have implications for our understanding of protein quality control pathways in the ER and other organelles.

     Biologists have long understood that Hedgehog (Hh) proteins play a crucial role in guiding development and maintaining adult tissue homeostasis in both invertebrates and vertebrates. However, the exact mechanisms by which Hh proteins induce transcriptional changes in target cells have been unclear. A recent study in Drosophila (fruit flies) has shed new light on this process.

     In Drosophila wing imaginal discs, Hh proteins are expressed in posterior compartment cells and signal to a band of anterior cells at the anterior-posterior (AP) border. Normally, Hh proteins inhibit the proteolytic processing of full-length Cubitus interruptus (Ci) protein, a transcriptional repressor, and activate the full-length Ci protein to induce transcriptional changes. However, a new study has found that Hh can signal normally in the absence of regulated Ci processing.

     The researchers created Ci variants that cannot be processed and expressed them at physiological levels in Drosophila wing imaginal discs. They found that these Ci variants supported a normal pattern of graded target gene activation and the development of adults with normal wing morphology, provided a constitutive source of Ci repressor was present to suppress ectopic dpp expression in anterior cells. This suggests that Ci can mediate normal Hh morphogen action in wing discs in the complete absence of regulated processing.

     The study also found that processing-resistant Ci variants were significantly activated in the absence of Hh by elimination of Cos2 or PKA, revealing separate inhibitory roles of these two components in addition to their well-established roles in promoting Ci processing. Furthermore, the researchers found that wing patterning by Hh does not require regulation of the level of either repressor or full-length Ci protein by processing.

     These findings have significant implications for our understanding of Hh signaling and its role in development and disease. Aberrant Hh protein production, distribution, and responses are common causes of developmental birth defects and cancer, including holoprosencephaly, limb and digit abnormalities, medulloblastoma, and basal cell carcinoma. A deeper understanding of the mechanisms underlying Hh signaling may lead to the development of new therapeutic strategies for these diseases.

     Biomedical research has long sought to understand the complex neuronal circuits in the spinal cord dorsal horn that process sensory information and contribute to pain perception. A recent study used an optogenetic approach to selectively activate spinal interneurons that express the calcium-binding protein calretinin (CR). The results show that these interneurons form an interconnected network that can initiate and sustain enhanced excitatory signaling, directly relaying signals to lamina I projection neurons.

     The study found that photoactivation of CR interneurons in vivo resulted in significant nocifensive behavior, which was sensitive to morphine and caused a conditioned place aversion. This behavior was also enhanced by spared nerve injury. In contrast, halorhodopsin-mediated inhibition of these interneurons elevated sensory thresholds. These findings suggest that dorsal horn circuits involving excitatory CR neurons play a crucial role in the generation and amplification of pain signals.

     The research confirms that CR neurons form an excitatory dorsal horn network that contributes to spinal pain signaling and can amplify pain signals in the absence of peripheral input. The strong interconnectivity of this neurochemically-defined subpopulation in the dorsal horn positions them to alter incoming sensory information prior to its relay to higher brain centers. This capacity is demonstrated in the nocifensive responses elicited by spinal photostimulation.

     The study's findings are consistent with current models of dorsal horn microcircuitry, which place CR neurons in a polysynaptic circuit that signals through SOM neurons to activate lamina I projection neurons and initiate pain signaling. The research extends our understanding of excitatory interneurons in spinal sensory processing, which has traditionally been seen as playing a limited role in linking low-threshold modality (innocuous) tactile input to excite more dorsal nociceptive circuitry.

     The study's optogenetic approach allowed for the selective activation of CR interneurons, providing new insights into their functional roles in sensory experience. Future work will focus on determining how these circuits respond during peripherally evoked sensory processing, as well as exploring the potential of CR interneurons as a future analgesic target.

     Biomedical research has shed light on the role of Ras-associated binding protein 23 (RAB23) in regulating early osteogenesis, the process of bone formation. Mutations in the RAB23 gene cause Carpenter Syndrome, a condition characterized by developmental abnormalities, including polysyndactyly and skull morphogenesis defects. To understand how RAB23 regulates skull development, researchers generated mice deficient in RAB23 and studied their skeletal development.

     The RAB23-deficient mice exhibited premature fusion of multiple sutures, resulting from aberrant osteoprogenitor proliferation and elevated osteogenesis in the suture. Further investigation revealed that FGF10-driven FGFR1 signaling was elevated in RAB23-deficient mice, leading to an imbalance in MAPK, Hedgehog signaling, and RUNX2 expression. Inhibition of elevated pERK1/2 signaling normalized osteoprogenitor proliferation, reduced osteogenic gene expression, and prevented craniosynostosis.

     The study found that RAB23 regulates the sutural stem cell niche marker GLI1, represses Hedgehog signaling, and inhibits osteoprogenitor expansion. RAB23 also represses FGF10-driven pERK1/2 signaling, which directly regulates osteoprogenitor development, RUNX2-mediated differentiation, and represses GLI1. These findings suggest that RAB23 coordinates suture biogenesis by regulating FGF-ERK1/2-RUNX2 activity and the activation of GLI1.

     RAB23 controls suture morphogenesis and patency through three mechanisms: FGF10-pERK1/2 signaling, the repression of Hedgehog targets through a canonical pathway, and the repression of GLI1 by pERK1/2. GLI1 contributes to the regulation of the mesenchymal stem cell niche, while FGF10-pERK1/2 and Hedgehog-GLI1 signaling regulate osteoprogenitor cell proliferation. The study's findings provide new insights into the role of RAB23 in regulating early osteogenesis and suggest potential therapeutic targets for conditions characterized by abnormal bone formation.
Biologists have long sought to understand how cells "remember" past events, such as environmental or developmental cues, to influence gene expression. A key player in this process is the Polycomb Repressive Complex 2 (PRC2), which maintains epigenetic repression of many genes in animals and plants. PRC2 modifies chromatin at its targets, and these modifications can be inherited through cell division. However, it remains unclear whether this inheritance directs long-term memory of individual gene expression states (cis memory) or if local chromatin states are dictated by diffusible factors (trans memory).

    To address this question, researchers studied the Arabidopsis gene FLOWERING LOCUS C (FLC), a Polycomb target gene involved in vernalization, the acceleration of flowering following prolonged cold exposure. Vernalization is mediated by cold-induced epigenetic repression of FLC. By monitoring the expression of two copies of FLC in the same plants, the researchers found that one copy can be repressed while the other is active, and this "mixed" expression state is inherited through many cell divisions as plants develop. These data demonstrate that epigenetic memory of FLC expression is stored not in trans but in cis, meaning that the local chromatin environment of the gene itself instructs its own epigenetic inheritance.

    The researchers used an assay to visualize mitotic heritability of FLC expression states at the single-cell level. They crossed FLC-mCherry to FLC-Venus plants to generate F1 hybrids carrying a single copy of each transgene. In a trans-based memory system, the only possible heritable expression states of FLC-Venus/FLC-mCherry are ON/ON and OFF/OFF, because epigenetic information is stored as a diffusible signal. However, the researchers found that FLC repression requires PRC2 but is independent of DNA methylation, making it a useful system for studying the cis-memory storage capability of Polycomb at a single gene. In vernalized plants, FLC expression is bistable, with the number of cells in which FLC is stably repressed increasing quantitatively with the duration of prior cold exposure.

    These findings provide evidence that Polycomb-repressed chromatin at a single gene can instruct its own inheritance and constitute a cis memory system. This challenges the idea that epigenetic memory is solely stored in trans, through the concentrations of diffusible regulatory factors. Instead, the local chromatin environment of individual genes, such as FLC, can store epigenetic memory, allowing cells to "remember" past events and influence gene expression accordingly.
BiP, a heat shock protein 70 (Hsp70) chaperone located in the endoplasmic reticulum (ER), plays a crucial role in maintaining protein homeostasis. Recent research has shed light on the physiological modulation of BiP activity through its oligomerization. Using site-specific proteolysis and crosslinking, scientists have found that BiP oligomers consist of adjacent protomers engaging the interdomain linker of one molecule in the substrate binding site of another, which attenuates the chaperone function of oligomeric BiP.

Native gel electrophoresis revealed a rapid and reciprocal relationship between the burden of unfolded proteins and BiP oligomers. This suggests that the oligomeric form of BiP is a relatively inactive storage form of the chaperone that is accessible on a short timescale. The findings are consistent with a model where competition between oligomerization and substrate binding buffers the ER against rapid fluctuations in unfolded protein load.

Calcium depletion in the lumen of the ER causes rapid oligomerization of mammalian BiP and a coincidental diminution in substrate binding. This indicates that the oligomers are relatively inert. The equilibration between inactive oligomers and active monomeric BiP is poised to buffer fluctuations in ER unfolded protein load on a rapid timescale, which is not attainable by inter-conversion of active and covalently-modified BiP or the conventional unfolded protein response.

The research suggests that BiP oligomerization is rapidly tuned to the physiological state of the cell. Given that oligomerization inactivates participating BiP protomers, the oligomeric form is a relatively inactive storage form of the chaperone. The findings are consistent with a scenario where BiP favors itself as a client (oligomerizing) over other clients when calcium levels in the ER decline. This shift in equilibrium between unfolded proteins and active chaperones would favor the former.

The inverse correlation between unfolded protein burden and BiP oligomerization appears to break down in the context of ER calcium depletion, which is both a strong inducer of the unfolded protein response and promotes rapid oligomerization of BiP. The study provides a coherent picture of how enhanced production of unfolded proteins rapidly draws on the pool of inactive, oligomeric BiP to service its growing clientele.

     Biomedical researchers have determined the near-atomic structure of a flexible filamentous plant virus called Pepino mosaic virus (PepMV) using a technique called electron cryomicroscopy. PepMV is a representative of the genus Potexvirus, which includes economically important plant pathogens. The virus particle is composed of several hundred copies of a helically arrayed coat protein (CP) that protects a single-stranded RNA molecule.

     The researchers found that the structure of PepMV CP is similar to that of nucleoproteins (NPs) from the genus Phlebovirus, a group of enveloped viruses with a segmented single-stranded RNA genome. The NPs from phleboviruses are associated with the viral genome in loose ribonucleoproteins (RNPs) protected inside an envelope.

     The main difference between the CP of PepMV and the NP of phleboviruses is in their C-terminal extensions, which determine the characteristics of the distinct multimeric assemblies. The CP of PepMV forms a flexuous, helical rod, while the NP of phleboviruses forms a loose ribonucleoprotein.

     The homology between the CP of PepMV and the NP of phleboviruses suggests that there may have been gene transfer between eukaryotic positive-sense single-stranded RNA viruses and negative-sense single-stranded RNA viruses. Flexible filamentous viruses are ubiquitous plant pathogens that have a significant impact on agriculture. Their infective particles are non-enveloped and flexible rod-shaped virions that contain several hundred copies of a CP arranged in a helical fashion protecting a positive-sense single-stranded RNA molecule.

     The researchers were able to model the CP of PepMV and its interactions with viral RNA. The overall fold of PepMV CP resembles that of NPs from the genus Phlebovirus. The study provides new insights into the structure and function of flexible filamentous viruses and highlights the potential for gene transfer between different types of viruses.
Biomedical researchers have developed a new method for precisely locating the binding sites of antibodies on cell surfaces. This technique, called SiteLoc, uses topological image averaging to achieve nanometer-scale resolution and can be applied to various molecular distance estimations. The method involves calculating the average distance between a bound ligand and a reference surface, allowing for accurate localization of binding sites on native cell surfaces.

Traditional methods for epitope identification are often costly, time-consuming, and unsuitable for high-throughput analysis. These limitations hinder the development of therapeutic monoclonal antibodies and vaccines. The new SiteLoc method addresses these challenges by providing a fast and efficient way to screen epitope locations on cell surfaces. This is particularly important for understanding the immunological outcome of host-target interactions, as the location of an epitope can significantly impact the effectiveness of an antibody or vaccine.

The SiteLoc method uses high-resolution images to determine the location of binding sites on cell surfaces. By averaging the fluorescent signal along a cell contour, the method can accurately estimate molecular heights on both spherical and non-spherical cellular surfaces. This is a significant improvement over existing techniques, which are often designed for identical or spherical particles and are not suited for unaltered cells with varying surface morphology.

The researchers demonstrated the effectiveness of SiteLoc by localizing the binding site of a non-specific monoclonal IgG antibody (Xolair) on the M protein of bacteria. The results agreed well with previously reported data, indicating that the antibody Fc binding site is located at the S region of the M protein. The SiteLoc method has the potential to revolutionize the development of therapeutic monoclonal antibodies and vaccines by providing a fast and efficient way to screen epitope locations on cell surfaces. This could lead to improved understanding of the immunological outcome of host-target interactions and the development of more effective treatments.

     Biomedical research has long recognized the importance of systemic vascular pressure regulation in vertebrates, with peripheral resistance in tissue capillary beds playing a key role. However, the relationship between central control of vascular flow and peripheral resistance remains unclear, particularly in the context of hypoxia, or oxygen deprivation. A recent study has shed light on this relationship by investigating the role of peripheral resistance and oxygen sensing in the skin, a large vascular bed, in response to hypoxia in mice.

     The study found that the skin's response to hypoxia, mediated by the HIF (Hypoxia-Inducible Factor) transcription factors, has a significant impact on systemic cardiovascular parameters, including heart rate, arterial pressures, and body temperature. Specifically, the study demonstrated that the loss of HIF-1Î± and HIF-2Î± expression in keratinocytes, the primary cell type in the skin, resulted in altered cardiovascular responses to hypoxia, including changes in systemic blood pressures and cutaneous temperatures.

     The researchers used a 10% oxygen challenge to induce hypoxia in mice and observed the effects on cardiovascular parameters. They found that mice lacking HIF-1Î±, HIF-2Î±, NOS2, and Arg-1 expression in the epidermis showed significant changes in systemic blood pressures and cutaneous temperatures compared to littermate control animals. The study also proposed a model for hypoxic responses involving HIF isoform activity and nitric oxide homeostasis in the skin, which suggests that hypoxia results in an initial HIF-1Î±/NOS2/NO-mediated increase in vascular diameter and resulting increases in perfusion, followed by a HIF-2Î±/Arginase-induced reduction of intracellular L-arginine.

     These findings represent the first demonstration of a dynamic role for oxygen sensing in a peripheral tissue directly modifying cardiovascular response to the challenge of hypoxia. The study highlights the importance of peripheral tissues, such as the skin, in cardiovascular adaptation to hypoxia and provides new insights into the complex relationship between central control of vascular flow and peripheral resistance. The results have significant implications for our understanding of how the body responds to oxygen deprivation and may lead to the development of new therapeutic strategies for treating hypoxia-related disorders.
Biomedical researchers have developed a new method for mapping the connections between individual neurons in the brain with high precision. This technique, called cellular resolution circuit mapping, uses a light-sensitive protein called channelrhodopsin (ChR2) to activate specific neurons and study their connections. By combining ChR2 with a technique called temporal focusing, which expands a beam of light in the imaging plane, researchers can selectively target individual neurons and their proximal dendrites. This approach allows for the reliable generation of action potentials in single neurons, which can be verified using a calcium indicator called GCaMP6s. Additionally, patch-clamp electrophysiological recordings can be used to determine the presence or absence of synaptic connections between neurons.

The previous methods for optogenetic mapping, which use ChR2 and other light-sensitive proteins, have limitations. These methods can activate not only the targeted neuron but also nearby fibers of passage or local axonal boutons, making it difficult to obtain accurate circuit maps. Furthermore, the expression of ChR2 throughout the dendritic and axon terminal fields can lead to unintended light-induced electrical responses.

The new method described in this study overcomes these limitations by restricting ChR2 expression to the soma and proximal dendrites of neurons. This allows researchers to selectively target individual neurons and their immediate connections, providing a reliable method for evaluating synaptic connectivity with single-neuron resolution. The synaptic organization of individual neurons into circuits is essential for interpreting sensory input and producing behavioral responses. Understanding the precise patterns of connectivity among different types of neurons is critical for elucidating circuit function, and this new method provides a valuable tool for achieving this goal. By enabling the mapping of functional connectivity with single-cell resolution, this technique has the potential to significantly advance our understanding of neural circuits and their role in various physiological and pathological processes.

     Biomedical research has long sought to understand how transcription factors (TFs) interact with DNA in eukaryotic genomes. Two TFs, Sox2 and Oct4, have been studied extensively, with previous research suggesting an ordered assembly of these proteins on DNA. However, a new analysis of single-molecule data from living cells reveals that the interaction between Sox2 and Oct4 is not ordered assembly, but rather negative reciprocity.

     Negative reciprocity means that the expression of Sox2 increases the genomic binding of Oct4, but the expression of Oct4 decreases the binding of Sox2. This finding was unexpected and suggests that TFs like Sox2 and Oct4 exhibit different cooperativities at different genomic loci. The analysis also implies that single-molecule methods can provide novel detection capabilities for understanding the complex interactions between TFs and DNA.

     The genomic-diversity model, which accounts for the variation in sign of cooperativity across the genome, suggests that Sox2 and Oct4 sometimes help and sometimes hinder each other across the genome. This finding has significant ramifications, indicating that single-molecule data can provide more information than previously expected. In fact, a single number, Î=-0.22, can suggest that Sox2 and Oct4 exhibit both positive and negative cooperativity across genomic loci in vivo.

     The implications of this finding are that there may be energy expenditure behind the scenes that maintains loci at which Sox2 and Oct4 bind away from thermodynamic equilibrium, or there may be mixed cooperativity across the genome, with Sox2 and Oct4 helping each other through positive cooperativity at some loci and hindering each other through negative cooperativity at other loci. These possibilities suggest unexpected complexity in how TFs interact on DNA, and future studies will be needed to address the implications of this finding. The single-molecule methods used in this research offer exciting capabilities for unraveling such mechanisms and understanding the complex interactions between TFs and DNA.
Biomedical researchers have long understood the importance of detecting pathogens and mounting an immune response to prevent infection. However, this response comes at a high metabolic cost, and avoiding pathogens before infection may be advantageous. A team of scientists investigated whether animals possess sensory mechanisms to detect bacterial endotoxins, specifically lipopolysaccharides (LPS), before infection. They focused on Drosophila melanogaster, the common fruit fly, to study this phenomenon.

The researchers found that Drosophila display strong aversive responses to LPS and that gustatory neurons expressing Gr66a bitter receptors mediate avoidance of LPS in feeding and egg-laying assays. They discovered that the expression of the chemosensory cation channel dTRPA1 in these cells is necessary and sufficient for LPS avoidance. Furthermore, LPS stimulates Drosophila neurons in a TRPA1-dependent manner and activates exogenous dTRPA1 channels in human cells. These findings demonstrate that flies detect bacterial endotoxins via a gustatory pathway through TRPA1 activation as a conserved molecular mechanism.

The study showed that LPS is avoided during feeding and oviposition, and that dTrpA1 expression in bitter-sensing gustatory neurons is necessary and sufficient for LPS avoidance. This suggests that a TRPA1-dependent mechanism of avoidance of LPS may serve flies to prevent infection with Gram-negative bacteria. The researchers also found that control animals, but not dTrpA1-/- mutants, preferred laying eggs on control food rather than on food contaminated with LPS.

The team tested whether TRPA1 mediates gustatory avoidance of LPS in flies and found that blocking neurotransmission in gustatory neurons by expressing the light chain of tetanus toxin abolished avoidance of LPS. This indicates that flies can detect LPS through a gustatory mechanism. The study demonstrates that fruit flies possess a gustatory mechanism underlying the detection and avoidance of LPS. The avoidance of LPS-contaminated food during feeding and oviposition may serve to prevent Gram-negative bacterial infections, potentially compensating for the lack of adaptive immunity in these animals. The findings suggest a broadly conserved principle whereby dTRPA1 channels play a crucial role in LPS detection by sensory neurons in flies and mammals, regardless of the particular sensory modality involved.

Summary
     Biologists have long assumed that the amount of messenger RNA (mRNA) in a cell directly correlates with the amount of protein produced. However, research has shown that this is not always the case. A team of scientists has identified a new factor, called mRNA:ncRNA avoidance, which plays a significant role in controlling protein expression levels in bacteria and archaea.

     mRNA:ncRNA avoidance refers to the tendency of mRNAs to avoid interacting with non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs). The researchers found that this avoidance increases translational efficiency, or the rate at which mRNAs are translated into proteins. They also discovered that the avoidance of stochastic mRNA:ncRNA interactions has a greater impact on protein abundance than other factors, such as mRNA structure and codon usage.

     The team generated synonymously variant green fluorescent protein (GFP) mRNAs with different potential for mRNA:ncRNA interactions and found that GFP levels correlated well with interaction avoidance. This suggests that taking stochastic mRNA:ncRNA interactions into account can enable precise modulation of protein abundance.

     The researchers analyzed data from 97% of species and found a reduced capacity for interaction between native RNAs relative to controls. They also generated synonymously variant GFP mRNAs that differ in their potential to interact with core ncRNAs and demonstrated that GFP expression levels can be both predicted and controlled.

     The study found that there is strong selection for the avoidance of stochastic mRNA:ncRNA interactions across prokaryotes. However, the complete avoidance of stochastic interactions is combinatorially unlikely, suggesting a tradeoff between avoidance and optimal expression. The researchers predict that taking crosstalk between mRNAs and ncRNAs into account will enable the design of constructs where protein expression levels can be precisely controlled.

     A meta-analysis of proteomics and transcriptomics data revealed a strong correlation between mRNA:ncRNA avoidance and protein abundance. The study suggests that mRNAs with low avoidance values will produce fewer proteins for each mRNA transcript than those with high avoidance. Overall, the research provides new insights into the regulation of protein expression in bacteria and archaea and highlights the importance of considering mRNA:ncRNA interactions in the design of genetic constructs.
summary
     Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS) is a fatal neurodegenerative disease characterized by the loss of both upper and lower motor neurons. A key pathological event in ALS is the denervation of neuromuscular junctions (NMJs), which is observed in both patients and animal models of the disease. However, the underlying mechanisms of NMJ denervation in ALS are not well understood.

     Researchers used in vivo imaging to study the dynamics of NMJ denervation in a mouse model of ALS over a three-month period. They found that single motor units (MUs) are dismantled asynchronously, meaning that the loss of NMJs occurs at different times within a single MU. This suggests that NMJ denervation is a complex and dynamic process, rather than a simple manifestation of global motor neuron degeneration.

     The study revealed that weeks before complete axonal degeneration, the dismantling of axonal branches is accompanied by new axonal sprouting, resulting in the formation of new synapses onto nearby NMJs. This indicates that NMJ denervation is not a one-way process, but rather a continuous process of denervation and new innervation.

     The researchers also found that denervation events tend to propagate from the first lost NMJ, suggesting that factors extrinsic to motor neurons, such as neuromuscular factors, contribute to NMJ denervation. Additionally, distal branches of axons are more susceptible to denervation than proximal branches.

     These findings have important implications for the development of therapeutic strategies for ALS. The study highlights a long temporal window between early branch-specific dismantling and global motor axon degeneration, which could be exploited to prevent or slow disease progression. However, the rescue of neuronal loss in SOD1 mice does not necessarily improve NMJ denervation, suggesting that local branch-specific events may play a key role in NMJ denervation.

     Overall, this study provides new insights into the complex and dynamic process of NMJ denervation in ALS, and highlights the need for further research into the underlying mechanisms of this disease.
Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand the mechanisms behind the side effects of tamoxifen, a hormone therapy used to treat breast cancer. While tamoxifen improves survival rates, long-term treatment can lead to unpleasant symptoms such as hot flashes, bone density changes, and fatigue. However, the tissues and cells responsible for these side effects have remained unclear due to a lack of suitable animal models.

     A recent study used mice to investigate the effects of tamoxifen on physiology. The researchers found that mice treated with tamoxifen exhibited changes in body temperature, bone density, and movement. To understand the underlying mechanisms, the researchers used single-cell RNA sequencing to analyze gene expression in the hypothalamus and preoptic area (hypothalamus-POA) of the mice. This analysis revealed widespread changes in gene expression in response to tamoxifen treatment.

     The researchers discovered that estrogen receptor alpha (ERÎ±) plays a crucial role in mediating the effects of tamoxifen on the hypothalamus-POA. Conditional knockout of ERÎ± in the hypothalamus-POA abolished or reversed the gene expression changes induced by tamoxifen. Furthermore, ERÎ±-deficient mice did not exhibit tamoxifen-induced changes in body temperature, bone density, or movement. These findings suggest that ERÎ± signaling in the central nervous system is necessary for the physiological effects of tamoxifen treatment.

     The study also found that ERÎ± expression in the hypothalamus-POA is necessary for the effects of tamoxifen on thermoregulation, bone density, and movement. The researchers propose that tamoxifen treatment modulates ERÎ± signaling in the central nervous system to alter fundamental aspects of physiology and health. Given the known roles of ERÎ± in mediating various physiological processes, the study's findings provide new insights into the mechanisms behind the side effects of tamoxifen treatment.

     The study's results have significant implications for understanding the effects of tamoxifen on the hypothalamus-POA and the role of ERÎ± in mediating these effects. The findings suggest that ERÎ± signaling in the central nervous system is a key mediator of the physiological effects of tamoxifen treatment. Further research is needed to fully understand the mechanisms behind the side effects of tamoxifen and to develop strategies to mitigate these effects in patients undergoing hormone therapy.
summary
     ISG15, a protein produced in response to interferon, has been found to play a crucial role in defending against Listeria monocytogenes infection. Research has shown that ISG15 expression is dramatically induced in non-phagocytic cells upon Listeria infection, and this induction can occur independently of type I interferon. The induction of ISG15 is mediated by the cytosolic surveillance pathway, which senses bacterial DNA and triggers a signaling cascade involving STING, TBK1, IRF3, and IRF7.

     The study found that ISG15 expression restricts Listeria infection both in vitro and in vivo. To understand the mechanism behind this restriction, the researchers used stable isotope labeling in tissue culture (SILAC) to identify proteins that are modified by ISG15. They discovered that ISG15 modification of ER and Golgi proteins leads to increased secretion of cytokines that counteract infection.

     Specifically, the researchers found that ISG15 modification of ER and Golgi proteins results in the increased secretion of cytokines such as IL-6 and TNF-Î±, which are known to counteract Listeria infection. This suggests that ISG15 plays a key role in the innate immune response to Listeria infection.

     The study also found that ISG15 expression is induced in an interferon-independent manner in human non-phagocytic cells. This is significant because it suggests that ISG15 can be induced in response to Listeria infection without the need for interferon signaling. The researchers used a viral protein, B18R, to block interferon signaling and found that ISG15 expression was still induced in response to Listeria infection.

     Overall, the study provides new insights into the role of ISG15 in defending against Listeria infection and highlights the importance of ISG15 in the innate immune response. The findings suggest that ISG15 is a key component of the host defense against Listeria and that its modification of ER and Golgi proteins plays a critical role in restricting infection.
Summary
     Biomedical researchers use a technique called single-molecule FÃ¶rster Resonance energy transfer (smFRET) to study the structure and dynamics of biomolecules. However, analyzing the large amounts of data produced by this method has been a challenge. To address this issue, a new software called DeepFRET has been developed. DeepFRET uses deep learning, a type of artificial intelligence, to rapidly and accurately classify smFRET data.

     DeepFRET is an open-source software that can analyze raw microscope images and output histograms of biomolecule behavior. The only human intervention required is setting a quality threshold for the data. The software integrates standard features of smFRET analysis and outputs common kinetic information metrics. In tests, DeepFRET achieved an accuracy of over 95% in classifying ground truth data, outperforming human operators and other commonly used threshold methods. It also required significantly less time to analyze the data, taking only about 1% of the time needed by human operators.

     DeepFRET's neural network was trained using simulated or high-quality annotated experimental data. The software can be used for various applications, including biomolecular recognition, protein folding and dynamics, and super-resolution imaging. Its development is expected to enable new fully automated analysis methodologies in structural biology. DeepFRET is available as a free-to-use standalone executable for both Windows and Mac users, making it accessible to researchers with limited programming skills.

     Deep learning-based analysis has several advantages over other approaches. It can recognize abstract patterns and learn useful features directly from raw input data, reducing the need for extensive data preprocessing or empirically defined rules. This leads to reproducible and less opinionated evaluation of single-molecule data. DeepFRET's improved classification was achieved without any preprocessing or post-inspection of data, demonstrating its potential to benchmark the reproducibility of smFRET data acquisition methods for multiple biomolecular systems across laboratories. The software's performance was quantified using a confusion matrix, which showed a high level of agreement between the ground truth data and the data selected and classified by DeepFRET.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have identified a key protein called SCML2 that plays a crucial role in the regulation of gene expression. SCML2 is part of a complex called Polycomb repressive complex-1 (PRC1), which helps determine which genes are turned on or off in cells. The study found that SCML2 binds to PRC1 through a specific region called the SPM domain and also interacts with non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs) through a novel region called the RNA-binding region (RBR). This interaction with ncRNAs is essential for SCML2's ability to target specific genes and repress their expression.

     The researchers discovered that SCML2's ability to bind to chromatin, the complex of DNA and proteins that make up chromosomes, relies on the coordinated action of its MBT domains, RNA-binding activity, and interaction with PRC1 through the SPM domain. Deletion of the RBR reduced the occupancy of SCML2 at target genes, and overexpression of a mutant SCML2 lacking the RBR caused defects in PRC1 recruitment. These findings suggest that ncRNAs play a role in regulating SCML2 function and that SCML2 participates in the epigenetic control of transcription directly and in cooperation with PRC1.

     The study also found that SCML2 can enforce repression directly, independent of PRC1 recruitment, at a subset of genes. This suggests that SCML2 has multiple mechanisms for repressing gene expression. The identification of SCML2 as a Polycomb group protein that binds RNA and contributes to repression of Polycomb targets sheds new light on the interplay between ncRNAs and epigenetic regulation.

     The researchers propose a model in which the RBR contributes to the localization of SCML2 to chromatin, where it interacts with PRC1 and creates a chromatin environment that is refractory to transcription. This model suggests that SCML2 is recruited to target genes through a process that requires its RNA-binding activity and that the presence of mutant SCML2 lacking the RBR causes a partial release of PRC1 from chromatin. Overall, the study provides new insights into the mechanisms of epigenetic regulation and the role of ncRNAs in controlling gene expression.
summary
     Biologists have long understood that ribosomes play a crucial role in regulating gene expression by controlling the translation of messenger RNA (mRNA) into proteins. In Escherichia coli, a type of bacteria, the SecM protein regulates the SecA-dependent secretion pathway by causing ribosomes to stall during translation. However, the exact mechanism of this stalling process was not well understood until now.

     Researchers used a technique called cryo-electron microscopy (cryo-EM) to visualize the structure of ribosomes stalled by SecM at a resolution of 3.3-3.7 angstroms. This allowed them to identify two distinct mechanisms of stalling that occur at different stages of the translation cycle. The first mechanism involves the inactivation of the peptidyl-transferase center (PTC), which is responsible for forming peptide bonds between amino acids. The second mechanism involves the prolonged residence of the peptidyl-RNA at the hybrid A/P site, which prevents the full-scale translocation of transfer RNA (tRNA).

     The researchers used an in vitro translation system from E. coli to purify ribosome-nascent chain complexes (RNCs) stalled by SecM and analyzed their structures using cryo-EM. They found that the SecM protein interacts with the ribosome in a way that causes conformational changes in the 23S rRNA nucleotides, leading to the inactivation of the PTC. This inactivation prevents the stable binding and accommodation of the incoming A-site tRNA, causing the ribosome to stall.

     The researchers also identified two predominant forms of stalled ribosomes, one in the post-state and the other in the hybrid rotated state. They found that a collection of interactions between SecM and the exit tunnel cooperatively induce conformational changes of the PTC, leading to translation arrest at distinct elongation steps. These findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanism of SecM-dependent translational stalling and demonstrate how regulatory peptides can control the translation cycle by dynamically reshaping the functional center of the ribosome.
Summary
     Biomedical researchers used a new imaging technique called Adaptive Optics Scanning Laser Ophthalmoscope to study the cone photoreceptors in the fovea of the human eye. The fovea is the part of the eye responsible for sharp central vision. The study found that the density of cone photoreceptors in the fovea varies with the length of the eye. In longer eyes, the foveal cone mosaic is expanded at the fovea, but not in proportion to the eye length. This means that longer eyes have more cone photoreceptors packed into a smaller area at the fovea.

     The researchers also found that people with myopia, or nearsightedness, have a higher angular sampling density of cone photoreceptors in and around the fovea compared to people with normal vision. This suggests that myopes may have better visual acuity than emmetropes, or people with normal vision, despite reports of deficits in best-corrected foveal vision in myopes. The study's findings contradict the idea that increased spacing between photoreceptors caused by retinal stretching during myopic progression is the cause of visual deficits in myopes.

     The researchers used a new generation of Adaptive Optics Scanning Laser Ophthalmoscope to image cones at and near the fovea in 28 eyes of 16 subjects. Cone density and other metrics were computed in units of visual angle and linear retinal units. The study provides the first measures of foveal cone density as a function of axial length in living eyes. The findings have implications for our understanding of the physical and visual effects of eye length on vision.

     Previous studies have implicated retinal stretching as a possible cause of myopic visual deficits, but the structural changes at the foveal center during myopic progression were not well understood. The current study aimed to investigate how the length of the eye affects cone density at and near the foveal center. The researchers found that the foveal cone mosaic in longer eyes is expanded at the fovea, but not in proportion to eye length. This suggests that the retina is not stretched uniformly during myopic progression.

     The study's findings have implications for our understanding of the relationship between eye length and visual acuity. The results suggest that myopes may have better visual acuity than emmetropes, despite reports of deficits in best-corrected foveal vision in myopes. The study's findings also contradict the idea that increased spacing between photoreceptors caused by retinal stretching during myopic progression is the cause of visual deficits in myopes. Further research is needed to fully understand the relationship between eye length and visual acuity.
Biomedical researchers have made significant progress in understanding the factors that influence the duration of Streptococcus pneumoniae carriage in the nasopharynx. This bacterium is a leading cause of invasive disease in infants, particularly in low-income settings, and asymptomatic carriage is a prerequisite for disease. A new study has developed a model to calculate the duration of carriage episodes from longitudinal swab data and combined these results with whole genome sequence data.

The analysis revealed that genomic variation in S. pneumoniae accounted for 63% of the variation in carriage duration, while host traits such as age and previous carriage accounted for less than 5%. The study further partitioned this heritability into lineage and locus effects, identifying serotype, drug-resistance, and other significant locus effects as the largest sources of variation in carriage duration. Specifically, serotype accounted for 17% of the variation, drug-resistance accounted for 9%, and other locus effects accounted for 7%.

A pan-genome-wide association study identified prophage sequences as being associated with decreased carriage duration, independent of serotype. This suggests that prophage sequences may disrupt the competence mechanism, which is essential for the bacterium's ability to acquire new genetic material. These findings support theoretical models of pneumococcal competition and antibiotic resistance.

The study's analysis of heritability shows that variants other than serotype affect carriage duration, consistent with recent theoretical work. The researchers have identified candidates for the loci that contribute to this effect and suggest that variants in the pneumococcal genome alter carriage duration, which is linked to antibiotic resistance. The study provides a quantitative estimate of how closely transmission pairs share their carriage duration and shows evidence for differences both between and within serotypes.

The findings imply that the genetics of S. pneumoniae is an important factor in determining carriage duration in this population. However, the heritability estimate may differ if environmental conditions, such as host vaccination status, are associated with streptococcal genotype between populations. The study's results have significant implications for our understanding of pneumococcal disease and the development of effective prevention and treatment strategies.
Summary
     The slow delayed rectifier current (IKs) plays a crucial role in stabilizing repolarization and shortening action potential duration in the heart. This current is conducted by the tetrameric voltage-gated ion channel KCNQ1 and its Î²-subunit KCNE1. However, the stoichiometry between Î± and Î²-subunits has been a topic of controversy, with some studies suggesting a strict 2:4 ratio and others proposing a variable ratio up to 4:4.

     To clarify this issue, researchers created fusion channels where KCNE1 was linked to one, two, or four KCNQ1 subunits. These channels were evaluated in mammalian cells using whole cell patch clamp, single-channel recordings, and UV-crosslinking unnatural amino acid approaches. The results showed that channels with two or four KCNQ1 subunits linked to KCNE1 (EQQ and EQQQQ) had increasingly hyperpolarized activation, reduced conductance, and shorter first latency of opening compared to channels with one KCNQ1 subunit linked to KCNE1 (EQ). These effects were abolished by the addition of KCNE1.

     Furthermore, using a UV-crosslinking unnatural amino acid in KCNE1, the researchers found that EQQQQ and EQQ crosslinking rates were progressively slowed compared to KCNQ1, indicating that no intrinsic mechanism limits the association of up to four Î²-subunits within the IKs complex. This suggests that the flexibility in KCNQ1's Î²-subunits association allows for a powerful mechanism to modulate repolarization in the heart through changes in expression.

     The findings of this study demonstrate a KCNE1:KCNQ1 stoichiometry of up to 4:4, resolving the controversy surrounding the ratio of Î± and Î²-subunits in the IKs channel complex. This discovery has important implications for understanding the regulation of repolarization in the heart and may provide new insights into the mechanisms underlying cardiac arrhythmias and other heart conditions.
