     Biomedical research has long sought to understand the cellular responses to injury that enable complete tissue regeneration. A recent study in zebrafish has shed light on the role of interleukin-1 beta (Il1b) in this process. Il1b is a pro-inflammatory cytokine that is typically associated with inflammation and tissue damage. However, the study found that Il1b plays a crucial role in the regeneration of the zebrafish fin fold.

     The researchers discovered that Il1b is expressed by epithelial cells in response to tissue injury and initiates an inflammatory response. This response is necessary for the survival of regenerative cells, which are essential for tissue regeneration. The study also found that macrophages, a type of immune cell, are responsible for attenuating Il1b expression and resolving the inflammatory response. This process is critical for the proper progression of tissue regeneration.

     The study revealed that Il1b signaling acts as a double-edged sword in tissue regeneration. While it is necessary for regeneration, excessive Il1b signaling can impair tissue regeneration. The researchers found that a proper level of Il1b expression and the inflammatory response, which are tuned by macrophages, are necessary for the progression of tissue regeneration.

     The study also highlighted the importance of macrophages in tissue regeneration. Macrophages were found to play a critical role in attenuating Il1b expression and resolving the inflammatory response. The researchers demonstrated that genetic ablation of macrophages in adult zebrafish affects fin ray patterning and fin growth, underscoring the importance of these cells in tissue regeneration.

     The findings of this study have significant implications for our understanding of tissue regeneration and the role of Il1b in this process. The study suggests that a delicate balance of Il1b expression and the inflammatory response is necessary for proper tissue regeneration. This knowledge may have important implications for the development of therapies aimed at promoting tissue regeneration in humans.
summary
     Biomedical research has shown that the surface properties of ribosomes may impose limits on the composition of the cytoplasmic proteome in prokaryotic cells. The study used surface-modified fluorescent proteins (FPs) to investigate the dependence of protein mobility on protein surface properties and ionic strength in the cytoplasm of Escherichia coli, Lactococcus lactis, and Haloferax volcanii. The results indicate that the translational diffusion coefficient (D) of proteins in E. coli depends on the net charge and its distribution over the protein surface, with positive proteins diffusing up to 100-fold slower than negative ones. This effect is weaker in L. lactis and Hfx. volcanii due to electrostatic screening.

In E. coli, the decrease in mobility is primarily caused by the interaction of positive FPs with ribosomes, as shown in in vivo diffusion measurements and confirmed in vitro with purified ribosomes. The binding of positive proteins to ribosomes is a non-selective interaction, with dissociation constants (KD) on the order of micromolar. This finding suggests that ribosome surface properties may limit the composition of the cytoplasmic proteome.

The study also found that in L. lactis and Hfx. volcanii, the slowdown in diffusion with increasing positive surface charge is less pronounced due to electrostatic screening. The diffusion coefficients of proteins in the cytoplasm of E. coli depend on their net charge and the distribution of charge over the protein surface, with positive proteins moving up to 100-fold slower. The diffusion becomes even slower when cells are exposed to an osmotic upshift.

The researchers analyzed the proteomes of E. coli, L. lactis, Hfx. volcanii, and four endosymbiotic bacteria to determine the consequences of their findings. They found that ribosomal proteins with a net charge of more than +10 experience a drop in diffusion coefficient of more than 5-fold. The study highlights the importance of ribosome surface properties in limiting the composition of the cytoplasmic proteome and has implications for understanding the functioning of prokaryotic endosymbionts.
summary
     Biologists have long been fascinated by the ability of certain animals, such as planarians, to regenerate lost body parts. However, the mechanisms that trigger this regenerative process are not yet fully understood. A recent study has shed new light on this process by identifying a key molecule called Smed-follistatin, which is required for regeneration in planarians. Smed-follistatin is a homolog of a protein called follistatin, which is known to inhibit the activity of another protein called Activin.

     The researchers found that when Smed-follistatin is inhibited, planarians are unable to regenerate lost tissue, but are still able to undergo normal tissue turnover. This suggests that Smed-follistatin plays a specific role in triggering the regenerative response. The study also found that two Activin homologs, Smed-activin-1 and Smed-activin-2, are required for the Smed-follistatin phenotype, indicating that Smed-follistatin inhibits Smed-Activin proteins to trigger regeneration.

     The researchers also discovered that Smed-follistatin is wound-induced and is expressed at higher levels following injuries that cause tissue absence. This suggests that Smed-follistatin is specifically activated in response to injuries that result in the loss of tissue. The study proposes that wound-induced Smed-follistatin expression allows for regenerative responses to be initiated specifically as a consequence of tissue absence.

     The findings of this study have important implications for our understanding of regeneration in animals. Regeneration is a complex process that involves the coordinated action of multiple cell types and signaling pathways. The identification of Smed-follistatin as a key regulator of regeneration provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying this process. The study suggests that the inhibition of Activin signaling by Smed-follistatin is a critical step in triggering the regenerative response, and that this response is specifically activated in response to injuries that result in tissue absence.

     The results of this study have broad implications for our understanding of regeneration across the animal kingdom. Regeneration is an essential process that allows animals to recover from injury and maintain tissue homeostasis. The identification of Smed-follistatin as a key regulator of regeneration in planarians provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying this process, and highlights the importance of understanding the complex interactions between different signaling pathways in the regulation of regeneration.
Biomedical researchers have identified a distinct genetic signature in tumors that are deficient in mismatch repair (MMR), a process that corrects errors in DNA replication. This signature is characterized by a high frequency of indel mutations, which are insertions or deletions of one or more nucleotides, particularly in mono- and di-nucleotide repeats. The researchers used whole-genome and whole-exome sequencing to analyze 33 tumor-normal pairs, of which 25 were MMR-deficient. They found that MMR-deficient tumors had a significantly higher number of somatic mutations, with an average of 2015 mutations per tumor, compared to 39 in MMR-proficient tumors.

The researchers also identified a set of recurrent indels that may serve as markers for microsatellite instability (MSI), a condition associated with MMR deficiency. They developed a novel marker set that can detect MSI with greater specificity and selectivity than standard tests. Furthermore, they found that recurrent indels are enriched for the DNA double-strand break repair by homologous recombination pathway, suggesting that MMR-deficient tumors may be more sensitive to DNA double-strand break inducers.

The study's findings have implications for the diagnosis and treatment of MMR-deficient tumors. MMR-deficient tumors are often resistant to standard chemotherapy, but the researchers showed that they are sensitive to DNA double-strand break inducers. This suggests that targeting MMR-deficient tumors with DNA double-strand break inducers may be an effective therapeutic strategy. Additionally, the novel marker set developed in this study may improve the detection of MSI and MMR deficiency in tumors.

The researchers also found that a substantial fraction of human genetic variation arises from mutations that escape MMR. This suggests that MMR plays a critical role in maintaining genome stability and that its deficiency can lead to a unique set of mutations. The study's findings provide new insights into the biology of MMR-deficient tumors and may lead to the development of more effective diagnostic and therapeutic strategies for these tumors.
Summary
     Biomedical researchers have developed an improved version of a tool called Magnets, which is used for optogenetics in mammalian cells. Optogenetics is a technique that uses light to control biological processes in a non-invasive way. Magnets are small modules made from a protein called Vivid, which is found in the fungus Neurospora crassa. They work by forming a dimer, or pair, when exposed to light, allowing for precise control over biological processes.

     The original Magnets required concatemerization, a process where multiple copies of the protein are linked together, to function efficiently. They also needed to be preincubated at a low temperature of 28Â°C to work properly. To overcome these limitations, the researchers engineered an optimized version of Magnets, called eMags, which do not require concatemerization or low-temperature preincubation.

     The eMags were validated by using them to recruit proteins to subcellular organelles, induce organelle contacts, and reconstitute a process called OSBP-VAP ER-Golgi tethering, which is involved in the transport and metabolism of a type of lipid called phosphatidylinositol-4-phosphate. The results show that eMags are a highly effective tool for optogenetically manipulating physiological processes in mammalian cells.

     The eMags were engineered by introducing complementary charges to the Vivid homodimer, resulting in two components called nMag and pMag. These components are small, fast-acting, and can be fused to a wide range of proteins. The eMags have greater thermal stability and dimerization efficiency, as well as faster association and dissociation kinetics compared to the original Magnets.

     The researchers used a variety of applications to test the effectiveness of eMags, including protein recruitment to different organelles, the generation and expansion of organelle contact sites, and the rapid and reversible reconstitution of VAP-dependent inter-organelle tethers. The results demonstrate that eMags are a powerful tool for optogenetic manipulation of biological processes in mammalian cells.
Activation of GCN2 Kinase by Ribosome Stalling Links Translation Elongation with Initiation
     Researchers have long known that ribosome stalling during protein synthesis can lead to neurodegeneration, but the underlying signaling pathways were unknown. A recent study investigated the effects of ribosome stalling in mice with a genetic mutation that causes elongation complexes to stall at specific codons. The study found that the GCN2 kinase is activated in response to ribosome stalling, leading to the phosphorylation of eIF2Î± and the induction of ATF4, a key transcription factor in the integrated stress response (ISR) pathway.

     The researchers used a unique mouse model with the Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mutation to study the effects of ribosome stalling. They found that GCN2 is activated in the brain of these mice in a tRNA-deacylation-independent manner, prior to the onset of neurodegeneration. Activation of GCN2 results in the phosphorylation of eIF2Î± and the induction of ATF4-target genes in the cerebellum and hippocampus of B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mice.

     The study also demonstrated that GCN2 activation functions to attenuate neurodegeneration in the B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- brain. This suggests that ribosome stalling activates GCN2 as a previously unknown regulatory mechanism that links the elongation and initiation steps of translation. Failure to resolve stalled elongation complexes in mammalian neurons results in degeneration of these cells, but the signaling pathways evoked by these complexes were previously unknown.

     The researchers found that the eIF2Î±/ATF4 pathway is vital for the survival of neurons during ribosome stalling. In the hippocampus of B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/-; Gcn2-/- mice, they observed degeneration of CA1 pyramidal cells that is not observed in B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mice. ATF4 activation also occurs in the brains of B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mice in which the n-Tr20 is entirely deleted and thus no unprocessed tRNA is produced. These results raise the possibility that ribosome stalling may also contribute to GCN2 activation in other conditions, such as amino acid deprivation, in which lower levels of acylated tRNA are present. The exact mechanism for GCN2 activation by ribosome stalling remains unclear.
summary
     Over the past century, adult height has changed substantially and unevenly across the world's countries, with no indication of convergence. A reanalysis of 1472 population-based studies involving over 18.6 million participants found that the largest gains in adult height occurred in South Korean women and Iranian men, who became 20.2 cm and 16.5 cm taller, respectively, over the past century. In contrast, there was little change in adult height in some sub-Saharan African countries and in South Asia.

     The tallest people over the past century were men born in the Netherlands in the last quarter of the 20th century, with an average height of 182.5 cm, while the shortest were women born in Guatemala in 1896, with an average height of 140.3 cm. The height differential between the tallest and shortest populations was approximately 19-20 cm a century ago and has remained the same for women and increased for men over the past century.

     Changes in adult height varied drastically across countries. While European men's heights increased significantly in the 19th and 20th centuries, the largest gains since the 1896 birth cohort occurred in South Korean women and Iranian men. Women in Norway, Iceland, the USA, and American Samoa were also taller than 158 cm. In contrast, women in El Salvador, Peru, Bangladesh, South Korea, and Japan were among the shortest, with an average height of 140.3 cm.

     The tallest populations a century ago lived in Central and Northern Europe, North America, and some Pacific islands. Men born in Sweden, Norway, and the USA surpassed 171 cm, while Swedish women had an average adult height of 160.3 cm. In contrast, men born in 1996 in the Netherlands, Belgium, Estonia, Latvia, and Denmark surpassed an average height of 181 cm, with Dutch men being the tallest people on the planet at 182.5 cm. The gap with the shortest countries, including Timor-Leste, Yemen, and Laos, increased by approximately 4 cm over the past century.

Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand how the hepatitis B virus (HBV) and its satellite virus, hepatitis D virus (HDV), infect liver cells. These viruses cause significant public health problems worldwide, and co-infection with both viruses can lead to more severe disease. A key step in the infection process is the binding of the viruses to a receptor on the surface of liver cells. However, the identity of this receptor has remained unknown.

     To identify the receptor, scientists used a combination of biochemical and virological approaches. They created a synthetic peptide that mimics a region of the HBV and HDV proteins and used it to probe the surface of liver cells. This revealed a specific interaction between the peptide and a protein called sodium taurocholate cotransporting polypeptide (NTCP). NTCP is a multiple transmembrane transporter that is predominantly expressed in the liver.

     Further experiments confirmed that NTCP interacts with the large envelope protein of HBV and HDV and functions as a common receptor for both viruses. The pre-S1 domain of the large envelope protein is a key determinant for entry of both viruses, and it is believed to mediate viral interaction with the cellular receptor on hepatocytes.

     To test the role of NTCP in viral infection, scientists silenced the gene that encodes NTCP in liver cells and found that this inhibited HBV and HDV infection. Conversely, expressing NTCP in cells that are normally resistant to these viruses made them susceptible to infection. This suggests that NTCP is a functional receptor for HBV and HDV.

     The researchers also identified a specific region of NTCP, amino acids 157-165, that is crucial for binding to the receptor-binding region of the pre-S1 domain of the HBV and HDV proteins. This region is highly conserved among different strains of the viruses, which may explain why NTCP is a common receptor for both viruses. Overall, these findings provide new insights into the mechanisms of HBV and HDV infection and may lead to the development of new treatments for these diseases.
summary
     Sepsis, a life-threatening condition caused by an overwhelming immune response to an infection, can have long-lasting effects on the body's immune system, particularly on memory CD8 T cells. These cells play a crucial role in fighting off infections by rapidly recognizing and killing infected cells. Research has shown that sepsis can impair the function of memory CD8 T cells, making individuals more susceptible to re-infection.

     A recent study investigated the effects of sepsis on memory CD8 T cells in patients and mice. The study found that sepsis leads to an increase in the proliferation of central memory CD8 T cells (TCM), a type of memory CD8 T cell, in both patients and mice. This increase in proliferation results in a change in the composition of the memory CD8 T cell pool, with TCM becoming over-represented.

     The study also found that the increased proliferation of TCM leads to changes in the localization of memory CD8 T cells, with some cells moving to different tissues. Furthermore, the study showed that memory CD8 T cells from sepsis survivors have an altered transcriptional profile and chromatin accessibility, indicating long-lasting changes in the cells.

     These changes in the memory CD8 T cell pool and transcriptional landscape result in altered T cell function and a reduced capacity to control infections. Specifically, the study found that memory CD8 T cells from sepsis survivors were less effective at controlling Listeria monocytogenes infection.

     The study's findings suggest that sepsis leads to long-term changes in the phenotype, function, and localization of memory CD8 T cells, potentially changing the host's ability to respond to re-infection. The increased proliferation of TCM and the resulting changes in the memory CD8 T cell pool may contribute to the increased susceptibility to re-infection observed in sepsis survivors. Overall, the study provides new insights into the long-term effects of sepsis on the immune system and highlights the need for further research into the mechanisms underlying these changes.
Biomedical researchers have long been aware of the threat posed by Long INterspersed Element-1 (LINE-1 or L1) retrotransposition to human genomes. To counter this threat, human cells have evolved mechanisms to regulate L1 retrotransposition. One such mechanism involves the APOBEC3 (A3) gene family, which consists of seven enzymes that catalyze the deamination of cytidine nucleotides to uridine nucleotides in single-strand DNA substrates. Among these enzymes, APOBEC3A (A3A) is the most potent inhibitor of L1 retrotransposition in cultured cell assays. However, the molecular mechanism by which A3A inhibits L1 retrotransposition has remained unclear.

Previous studies have shown that over-expression of the wild-type APOBEC3A cytidine deaminase protein (A3A) can potently inhibit L1 retrotransposition in cultured human cells. However, deamination events were not detected in L1 retrotransposition events generated in the presence of wild-type A3A. This has left the molecular mechanism by which A3A inhibits L1 retrotransposition a mystery.

To elucidate this mechanism, researchers employed an in vitro assay that recapitulates the first strand cDNA synthesis step of the L1 integration. They found that A3A can inhibit L1 retrotransposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-strand DNA that arises during the process of L1 integration. This deamination leads to inexact TSDs (target site duplications) in the post-integration site.

Further experiments demonstrated that engineered L1 retrotransposition events derived from cultured human cells that co-express A3A and the uracil DNA glycosylase inhibitor protein (UGI) contain strand-specific, A3A-mediated C-to-U deamination events. These data indicate that A3A can inhibit L1 retrotransposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-strand DNA that arises during the process of L1 integration.

The degradation of deaminated retrotransposition intermediates could reduce L1 retrotransposition levels in cultured cell assays and eliminate evidence of A3A-mediated deamination events in the resultant retrotransposed L1 sequences. This scenario could explain why previous studies failed to detect A3A-mediated deamination events in engineered L1 retrotransposition events. Overall, these findings provide a mechanistic explanation of how the A3A cytidine deaminase protein can inhibit L1 retrotransposition.
summary
     Biomedical research has shown that sensory experience plays a crucial role in shaping neural circuitry by influencing synaptic connectivity and intrinsic properties of individual neurons. To understand how external stimuli are converted into altered neuronal output, it is essential to identify the molecular players involved in experience-dependent plasticity and circuit function. A recent study investigated the role of the activity-regulated, non-canonical Ras-like GTPase Rem2 in visual circuit plasticity.

     The study found that mice lacking the Rem2 gene (Rem2-/- mice) failed to exhibit normal ocular dominance plasticity during the critical period. At the cellular level, the research established a cell-autonomous role for Rem2 in regulating the intrinsic excitability of layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons, prior to changes in synaptic function. In vitro and in vivo recordings revealed increased spontaneous firing rates in the absence of Rem2. These findings demonstrate that Rem2 is a key molecule that regulates neuronal excitability and circuit function in the context of changing sensory experience.

     Rem2 functions at the nexus of a signaling network that senses and responds to changes in neuronal activity to preserve proper circuit function in the face of changing sensory experience. The study suggests that Rem2 may play an important role in regulating the absolute responsiveness of the cortex to visual stimulation, such as through regulation of neuronal excitability or synaptic scaling. To gain more insight into the circuit mechanisms underlying the observed ocular dominance plasticity deficits of Rem2-/- mice, the researchers examined adult ocular dominance plasticity.

     The study's findings provide an important step forward in connecting molecular regulators of neuronal morphology with broader circuit function. Given that Rem2 expression is acutely regulated by visual experience, the researchers sought to determine if Rem2 plays a role in activity-dependent processes in the visual cortex following sensory deprivation by investigating ocular dominance plasticity. The activity-regulated Ras-like GTPase Rem2 is an excellent candidate to link the activity of a neural circuit with functional plasticity. Rem2 is a member of the Rad/Rem/Rem2/Gem/Kir (RGK) protein family, a Ras-related subfamily of small GTPases, whose expression and function are regulated by neuronal activity in response to calcium influx through voltage-gated calcium channels.
Biomedical researchers have been exploring the possibility of using magnetic fields to control biological molecules and cells, a concept known as magnetogenetics. Recent studies have reported promising results, including the identification of a protein complex that acts like a compass needle to guide magnetic orientation in animals and the use of magnetic fields to control membrane conductance by attaching ferritin to an ion channel protein. However, a closer examination of these claims reveals significant discrepancies with basic laws of physics.

The forces, torques, and temperatures produced by magnetic fields on biological molecules are too small by many orders of magnitude to achieve the desired effects on molecular orientation or membrane channels. If the reported phenomena were to occur, they would require entirely different mechanisms than those proposed by the authors. The paramagnetic nature of protein complexes, such as ferritin, limits their utility for engineering magnetically sensitive cells.

One study reported the design of a molecular system intended to couple magnetic fields to ionic current across the cell membrane. The system consisted of a putative mechano-sensitive cation channel fused to two subunits of ferritin. The authors hoped that the paramagnetic protein would enable magnetic torque to tug open the channel and depolarize cells. Experimental results suggested that neural activity could be modulated by static magnetic fields, but the evidence was limited and difficult to interpret.

The biophysical mechanism underlying these effects is unclear. Ferritin is a large protein complex with 24 subunits that forms a spherical shell about 12 nm in diameter. Its paramagnetic nature would require extremely strong magnetic fields to produce significant effects, which is unlikely given the forces and torques produced by magnetic fields on biological molecules.

The study concludes that the proposed routes to magnetogenetics, based on either pulling or heating a ferritin/channel complex with magnetic fields, have no chance of success without dramatic new discoveries about the structure of biological matter. The analysis highlights the need for a deeper understanding of the biophysical basis for magnetosensation and magnetogenetics, including the effects of magnetic fields on biological molecules and cells.
Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand how stem cells switch between self-renewal, differentiation, and quiescence, a state of dormancy. Drosophila neural progenitors, also known as neuroblasts, have been a valuable model system for studying self-renewal and differentiation. However, the mechanisms governing quiescence in these cells have remained poorly understood.

     A recent study has shed light on this process by investigating the role of the differentiation factor Prospero in Drosophila neuroblasts. The researchers found that when neuroblasts enter quiescence, the Prospero protein is transiently detected in the nucleus at low levels. This pulse of Prospero precedes the establishment of a unique molecular profile characterized by the absence of most progenitor and differentiation markers.

     The study demonstrated that loss of Prospero prevents neuroblasts from entering quiescence, while a pulse of low-level nuclear Prospero can drive proliferating larval neuroblasts into quiescence. This suggests that Prospero levels may distinguish between three progenitor fates: the absence of Prospero for self-renewal, low levels for quiescence, and high levels for differentiation.

     The researchers used Drosophila neuroblasts as a model system to study the relationship between Prospero and quiescence. They found that nearly all neuroblasts that were quiescent at 12 hours after induction had reactivated proliferation and re-expressed progenitor markers by 24 hours. Additionally, they showed that precocious low-level nuclear Prospero could advance the timing of neuroblast quiescence.

     The study also investigated whether Prospero is required for neuroblast entry into quiescence. By quantifying the number of EdU+ neuroblasts in prospero null mutant embryos, the researchers found that loss of Prospero can delay the timing of neuroblast quiescence. Furthermore, they observed that the pulse of nuclear Prospero precisely matched the timing of neuroblast quiescence, suggesting a functional relationship between the two.

     Overall, this study provides new insights into the mechanisms governing quiescence in Drosophila neuroblasts and highlights the importance of Prospero in regulating this process. The findings have implications for our understanding of stem cell biology and may inform the development of stem cell-based therapeutics.
Biomedical researchers have developed a new method to study the process of HIV-1 capsid uncoating, a critical step in the virus's life cycle. Using single-particle fluorescence microscopy, they were able to observe the uncoating kinetics of individual HIV capsids in real-time. This approach allowed them to identify the rate-limiting step of uncoating as the opening of the first defect in the capsid lattice, which is followed by a rapid and catastrophic collapse.

The researchers found that the capsid-binding inhibitor PF74 accelerates the opening of the capsid but stabilizes the remaining lattice. In contrast, binding of a polyanion to a conserved arginine cluster in the lattice delays the initiation of uncoating but does not prevent subsequent lattice disassembly. These findings suggest that different stages of uncoating can be controlled independently, and the interplay between different capsid-binding regulators determines the overall uncoating kinetics.

The study used an in vitro single-molecule fluorescence imaging assay to follow the uncoating kinetics of hundreds of individual HIV capsids in a single experiment. This approach allowed the researchers to resolve intermediates in the disassembly pathway that are otherwise averaged out in traditional ensemble assays. They observed that the integrity and stability of capsids in a population is highly heterogeneous, with the majority containing defects and/or uncoating immediately after removal of the viral membrane.

In the absence of capsid binders, uncoating is an all-or-none process that proceeds rapidly after the first defect is created. The researchers also found that the half-life of the HIV capsid is sufficiently long to use PFO-permeabilized viral particles as a platform to quantify the binding of labeled proteins to authentic viral capsids at the single-particle level. These findings provide new insights into the mechanisms of HIV-1 capsid uncoating and suggest that different stages of uncoating can be controlled independently with the interplay between different capsid-binding regulators determining the overall uncoating kinetics.
Biomedical research has identified the cJUN NH2-terminal kinase (JNK) signaling pathway as a potential tumor suppressor in breast cancer. Contrary to expectations, loss-of-function mutations in the JNK pathway are frequently found in breast cancer genomic DNA. These mutations were thought to be associated with reduced cell proliferation, but studies have shown that JNK signaling actually promotes cell growth by activating the AP1 transcription factor.

     To investigate the role of JNK signaling in breast cancer, researchers examined the effect of JNK deficiency in the murine breast epithelium. The results showed that loss of JNK signaling caused genomic instability and the development of breast cancer. Furthermore, JNK deficiency accelerated tumor formation in a murine model of breast cancer, characterized by widespread early neoplasia and genomic instability.

     The study found that JNK plays a key role in the initiation of tumor development, rather than the growth of established tumors. This suggests that JNK pathway defects are 'driver' mutations that promote genome instability and tumor initiation. The frequent loss-of-function mutations in the JNK pathway in human breast cancer may therefore represent 'driver' mutations that promote tumor development.

     In contrast to the expected role of JNK signaling in promoting tumor growth, the results of this study indicate that JNK actually acts to prevent tumor initiation. The JNK signaling pathway may be suppressed in many breast cancers, and the association of breast cancer with reduced JNK signaling represents a correlation rather than a causal relationship.

     The study's major conclusions are that JNK deficiency in the mammary epithelium can cause murine breast cancer, and that JNK deficiency rapidly accelerates tumor development in a mouse model of breast cancer. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of the role of JNK signaling in breast cancer development and suggest that JNK pathway defects may be a key factor in the initiation of breast cancer.
summary
     A recent study has identified a crucial enzyme called peptidylglycine Î±-amidating monooxygenase (PAM) as essential for the formation of cilia in various cell types. Cilia are hair-like structures on the surface of cells that play a vital role in sensing the environment and transmitting signals. The researchers found that PAM is necessary for the normal assembly of motile and primary cilia in Chlamydomonas, a type of algae, planaria, a type of flatworm, and mice.

     In Chlamydomonas, PAM knockdown lines failed to assemble cilia beyond the transition zone, had abnormal Golgi architecture, and altered levels of cilia assembly components. Similarly, in planaria, decreased PAM gene expression reduced the density of motile cilia on the ventral surface and resulted in the appearance of cytosolic axonemes lacking a ciliary membrane. In mice, the architecture of primary cilia on neuroepithelial cells in PAM-deficient embryos was also aberrant.

     The study suggests that PAM activity and alterations in post-Golgi trafficking contribute to the observed ciliogenesis defects. The researchers propose that PAM plays a key conserved role during the early steps of ciliogenesis, revealing a novel link between amidation and cilium assembly in multiple cell types. Amidation is the process of adding an amino group to a molecule, and PAM is responsible for this process in bioactive signaling peptides.

     The findings of this study have significant implications for our understanding of ciliogenesis and the role of PAM in this process. Cilia defects have been linked to various human diseases, including polycystic kidney disease and Bardet-Biedl syndrome. Further research on PAM and its role in ciliogenesis may provide new insights into the mechanisms underlying these diseases and potentially lead to the development of new treatments. The study highlights the importance of PAM in ciliogenesis and its potential as a therapeutic target for cilia-related disorders.
summary
     Biologists have long known that class III myosins (Myo3) and the actin-bundling protein Espin play crucial roles in the development and maintenance of stereocilia in vertebrate hair cells. Defects in these proteins can lead to hereditary hearing impairments. Myo3 interacts with Espin1 through its tail homology I motif (THDI), but the exact mechanism of this interaction was unclear.

     Researchers have now discovered that Myo3 THDI contains two repeat sequences that can independently bind to the ankyrin repeats of Espin1 with high affinity. This binding mechanism, known as cross-linking, allows Myo3 to regulate the assembly and stabilization of actin bundles in stereocilia. The structures of Myo3 in complex with Espin1 have been elucidated, revealing a Myo3-induced release of Espin1 auto-inhibition mechanism.

     The study also found that Myo3-mediated cross-linking can enhance the actin fiber bundling activity of Espin1. This is consistent with previous research suggesting that Myo3 binding can release auto-inhibition and increase the diameter of Espin1-promoted actin bundles. Myo3 and Espin1 have been shown to co-localize at the tips of stereocilia in hair cells, and co-expression of Myo3 and Espin1 can stimulate the elongation of stereocilia in cultured organ of Corti hair cells.

     The researchers propose that a key function of the Myo3/Espin1 interaction is to promote the formation of thicker parallel actin bundle fibers in cellular structures such as stereocilia. The THDI region of Myo3 mediates binding to Espin1, allowing Myo3 to transport Espin1 to the tips of actin bundle-based structures. Once localized, Espin1 promotes the elongation of actin protrusions.

     The study reveals a previously unrecognized direct myosin binding-induced actin bundling activity regulation mechanism of an actin filament cross-linker protein. The full-length Myo3/Espin1 complex may have even higher actin fiber bundling activity than the Myo3-ABR12/Espin1 complex studied in this work. The findings provide new insights into the molecular basis of the Myo3 and Espin1 interaction and its role in the development and maintenance of stereocilia.
Voltage-gated calcium channels play a crucial role in various cellular functions, including synaptic transmission, muscle contraction, and gene expression. These channels translate membrane depolarization into calcium influx, enabling cells to respond to electrical signals. Researchers have long sought to understand the molecular mechanisms governing the specific voltage-sensing properties of these channels.

     To address this question, scientists used a combination of structure modeling, mutagenesis, and electrophysiology to analyze the structures, free energy, and transition kinetics of two functionally distinct voltage-sensing domains (VSDs) of the eukaryotic calcium channel CaV1.1. The study aimed to identify the molecular mechanisms governing specific voltage-sensing properties in these channels.

     The researchers generated advanced structure models of the activated and resting states of CaV1.1 VSDs, filling a gap in the understanding of voltage-sensing mechanisms in eukaryotic voltage-gated cation channels. The models were based on the cryo-EM structure of CaV1.1 and used the Rosetta computational modeling software to build a homology model of the human eukaryotic CaV1.1 channel.

     The study found that both VSDs displayed the typical features of the sliding helix model, but differed significantly in ion-pair formation of the outer gating charges. Stabilization of the activated state enhanced the voltage dependence of activation, while stabilization of resting states slowed the kinetics. This mechanism provides a mechanistic model explaining how specific ion-pair formation in separate VSDs can realize the characteristic gating properties of voltage-gated cation channels.

     The researchers tested the interaction partners suggested by the model and found that they showed the predicted effects on channel gating when experimentally tested by mutagenesis and electrophysiology analysis. Conversely, alterations of kinetic properties first recognized experimentally were reliably reproduced and explained by the computer model. The structures of the lowest energy states of CaV1.1 VSDs display the basic features of the sliding helix model, supporting the understanding of voltage-sensing mechanisms in eukaryotic voltage-gated cation channels.
summary
     Biomedical research has long been interested in the adult brain's ability to undergo plastic changes, particularly in the context of recovery from injuries or improving learning and cognition. Matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs) have been linked to juvenile experience-dependent primary visual cortex (V1) plasticity, but their role in this process in the adult V1 remains poorly understood. A recent study investigated the effects of MMP-inhibition on experience-induced plasticity in healthy and stroke-affected adult mice.

     The study found that 7-day application of an MMP-inhibitor prevented visual plasticity in healthy animals. However, treatment with the MMP-inhibitor once, but not twice, following a stroke rescued plasticity, which is normally lost under these conditions. These findings suggest that an optimal level of MMP-activity is crucial for adult visual plasticity to occur.

     The study's results highlight a critical role of MMPs in adult visual plasticity and suggest that upregulation of MMP-activity shortly after a cortical lesion compromises experience-induced visual plasticity, which can be rescued by brief MMP-inhibition. Precise regulation of MMP-activity appears to be essential for facilitating plasticity in the adult brain, offering new opportunities in treatment and recovery after stroke.

     The study's objective was to examine if MMPs are crucial for adult visual plasticity and if inhibition of their upregulation following cortical stroke may be beneficial for rescuing lost plasticity. A combination of in vivo optical imaging and behavioral vision tests revealed that an optimal level of MMP-activity is essential for adult visual plasticity to occur in the healthy and stroke-affected brain.

     The study's findings are consistent with previous research showing that MMP-activity supports healthy brain development and function, but a different outcome of MMP-action has been described for diseased brain. Increased MMP-activity was observed within 24 hours of a photothrombotically induced stroke, and application of a broad-spectrum MMP-inhibitor at the time of the stroke partially rescued impaired barrel cortex plasticity.

     The study's results demonstrate a vital role of MMPs for adult visual cortex plasticity, particularly for the increase of open eye responses in V1 after monocular deprivation. The findings expand previous studies from juvenile rats and suggest that MMP-activity has to be within a narrow window for experience-induced plasticity to occur. Overall, the study provides new insights into the critical role of MMPs in adult visual plasticity and offers potential opportunities for treatment and recovery after stroke.
Biomedical research has made significant progress in understanding the structure and function of AMPA (Î±-amino-3-hydroxy-5-methyl-4-isoxazole propionate) receptors, a type of glutamate-gated ion channel involved in signal transduction at vertebrate excitatory synapses. AMPA receptors are composed of four subunits that assemble as a dimer-of-dimers, and recent advances in cryo-electron microscopy have provided full-length structures of these receptors, both alone and in combination with auxiliary subunits.

Studies have shown that the extracellular layer of AMPA receptors may undergo substantial lateral motions during gating, opening up along the central twofold symmetry axis. However, the presence of auxiliary subunits, such as Stargazin, has been found to limit this conformational flexibility. Using bifunctional methanethiosulfonate cross-linkers, researchers were able to calibrate the conformations of functional AMPA receptors in the presence and absence of Stargazin. The results indicate that the extracellular layer of AMPA receptors can become trapped in stable, opened-up conformations, especially upon long exposures to glutamate.

In contrast, the presence of Stargazin limits this conformational flexibility, suggesting that under synaptic conditions, where brief glutamate exposures and the presence of auxiliary proteins dominate, the extracellular domains of AMPA receptors are likely to remain compact during gating. This is consistent with the idea that AMPA receptors work in a fairly compact conformational regime during synaptic transmission, unless faced with long exposures to glutamate in pathological conditions.

Recent cryo-EM structures of AMPA receptors in complex with auxiliary subunits have been acquired in resting, active, and desensitized states, providing valuable insights into the structural dynamics of these receptors. However, the exact mechanisms underlying their rapid signaling are still unclear. Further research is needed to fully understand the role of auxiliary subunits in regulating the conformational flexibility of AMPA receptors and their function in synaptic transmission.
Biomedical researchers have long faced challenges in isolating immune cells from the intestines of mice infected with helminths, a type of parasitic worm. This is due to the strong immune response triggered by the infection, which leads to the production of mucus, tissue remodeling, and the infiltration of immune cells. A team of scientists has developed a new protocol to overcome this challenge, allowing them to isolate millions of immune cells from the heavily infected duodenum, the first part of the small intestine.

To validate the accuracy of their protocol, the researchers used a high-dimensional spectral flow cytometry panel to analyze the isolated cells. This technique allowed them to identify many known hallmarks of anti-parasite immune responses throughout the entire course of helminth infection. The team also used confocal microscopy to confirm their findings, observing differences in cell ratios between naive and infected samples.

The new protocol and high-dimensional analysis enabled the researchers to characterize many known hallmarks of innate and adaptive anti-parasite immune responses, including the presence of specific immune cells such as B220+, Siglec F+, CD3+ CD4+, CD3+ CD4-, and CD64+ cells. These changes were observed locally, highlighting the importance of good cell isolation techniques to investigate intestinal responses against helminths directly.

The development of this protocol has the potential to accelerate single-cell discoveries of local helminth immune responses, which have previously been unfeasible due to the difficulties in isolating immune cells from infected intestines. This breakthrough could lead to a better understanding of the complex interactions between helminths and the host immune system, ultimately contributing to the development of new treatments for helminth infections. The protocol's ability to accurately represent intestinal immune responses also opens up new avenues for research in the field of immunology.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have developed a new model to understand how the proteasome, a key enzyme involved in protein degradation, works. The proteasome plays a crucial role in various cellular processes, including metabolism and the immune response. However, the mechanisms regulating proteasome activity are not yet fully understood.

     To address this knowledge gap, the researchers used a combination of experimental and computational approaches to develop a comprehensive model of proteasome dynamics. They focused on the 20S proteasome, a dynamic enzyme whose activity changes over time due to interactions between substrates, products, and regulatory sites. The model predicted the locations of these sites and the interactions between them, which were experimentally confirmed.

     The analysis revealed that the rate-limiting step of protein degradation is the transport of substrates into the proteasome. This transport efficiency varies between human standard and immuno-proteasomes, affecting the total degradation rate and substrate cleavage-site usage. The researchers used carefully designed experimental assays and detailed modeling to shed light on these mechanisms.

     The study used short fluorogenic peptides, which have a single proteasome-catalyzed cleavage site, to follow the kinetics of protein degradation in real-time. The results showed similar proteasome dynamics over time when using purified 20S proteasome or protein homogenates, suggesting that the model describes a core of proteasome features that could reflect proteasome dynamics in more complex cellular environments.

     The findings have implications for our understanding of protein degradation and its role in various cellular processes. Poly-ubiquitin tagging, transport, and peptide-bond hydrolysis regulate protein half-life and affect the majority of metabolic processes in the cell. The study provides new insights into the mechanisms of proteasomal action, including the causes and extent of differences in substrate-specific proteolysis between different proteasome isoforms.
summary
     Biomedical research has shown that the recycling of neurotransmitters is essential for sustained synaptic transmission. In Drosophila, histamine recycling is required for visual synaptic transmission. Histamine is rapidly taken up by laminar glia and converted to carcinine. Carcinine is then delivered back to photoreceptors, where it is hydrolyzed to release histamine and Î²-alanine. However, the mechanism by which Î²-alanine is returned to laminar glial cells was unclear.

     A new Î²-alanine transporter, named BalaT, has been identified in Drosophila. Null mutants of BalaT exhibited lower levels of Î²-alanine and reduced Î²-alanine accumulation in the retina. BalaT is expressed and required in retinal pigment cells for maintaining visual synaptic transmission and phototaxis behavior. These results suggest that retinal pigment cells play a critical role in visual neurotransmission and that a BalaT-dependent Î²-alanine trafficking pathway is necessary for histamine homeostasis and visual neurotransmission.

     The study found that BalaT functions in retinal pigment cells to recycle Î²-alanine. Disrupting histamine and carcinine synthesis abolishes photoreceptor synaptic transmission. While the transport of Î²-alanine seems less important, Î²-alanine recycling is essential for maintaining histamine homeostasis and photoreceptor synaptic transmission. Establishing and maintaining a Î²-alanine pool also plays a key role in sustaining visual synaptic transmission.

     High levels of Î²-alanine were detected in retinal pigment cells, suggesting that pigment cells may be critical for the transport and storage of Î²-alanine. The study aimed to determine whether there was a Î²-alanine transporter required for Drosophila vision transmission. Mutations in BalaT disrupted transport and storage of Î²-alanine in the retina, resulting in reduced photoreceptor synaptic activity and visual behavior.

     The findings suggest a conserved function for Î²-alanine trafficking in mammals and Drosophila. The study provides evidence for a more complete histamine recycling pathway, which is critical for sustaining photoreceptor synaptic transmission. In this pathway, carcinine is synthesized in epithelial glial cells within the optic lamina and is transported back to photoreceptor cells via the synaptic cleft. The BalaT-dependent Î²-alanine trafficking pathway is essential for histamine homeostasis and visual neurotransmission in Drosophila.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand the role of oxidants in aging and lifespan. A recent study used the yeast Saccharomyces cerevisiae as a model system to investigate the relationship between redox events and aging. The study found that chronologically aging yeast cells undergo a sudden redox collapse, resulting in the oxidation of over 80% of identified thiol-containing proteins. This redox collapse occurs several days before cell death and is not triggered by an increase in endogenous oxidants, as previously thought.

     Instead, the study suggests that a substantial drop in cellular NADPH levels is the primary cause of the redox collapse. NADPH normally provides the electron source for maintaining cellular redox homeostasis. The decrease in NADPH levels occurs early in the aging process and sets off a cascade that down-regulates most cellular processes. Caloric restriction, a well-known lifespan-extending measure, was found to increase NADPH levels and delay the onset of the redox collapse.

     The study provides a timeline of events leading up to the system-wide oxidation of the proteome in aging yeast cells. The results suggest that chronological aging may represent a physiological stress condition that pushes the redox buffering capacity of the thiol proteome to its limits. The study also raises the possibility that the oxidation of certain key proteins may contribute to the beneficial down-regulation of cellular processes that would otherwise shorten lifespan.

     The findings of this study have implications for our understanding of aging in postmitotic cells. They suggest that the decrease in NADPH levels and the concomitant increase in cellular redox potential are not unique to yeast, but may be shared with other aging organisms whose metabolism changes. The study also highlights the potential of using yeast as a model system to uncover protein targets whose oxidative thiol modifications may contribute to the physiological alterations observed in aging organisms.
summary
     Biologists have long recognized the importance of homochirality in the biological macromolecular world, where molecules are predominantly composed of either left-handed (L) or right-handed (D) forms. To maintain this homochirality, cells have developed mechanisms to selectively remove D-amino acids that are mistakenly attached to transfer RNAs (tRNAs) during protein synthesis. A recent study has shed light on the molecular basis of one such mechanism, known as D-aminoacyl-tRNA deacylase (DTD), which is responsible for removing D-amino acids from tRNAs in the parasite Plasmodium falciparum.

     The researchers used X-ray crystallography to determine the three-dimensional structure of DTD in complex with a substrate-mimicking analog. This revealed how the enzyme captures the chiral center of the incoming D-aminoacyl-tRNA using a unique "cross-subunit" Gly-cisPro dipeptide. This dipeptide is inserted into the active site of the dimeric enzyme and plays a crucial role in recognizing the D-amino acid. Notably, no protein residues are directly involved in catalysis, and the enzyme's ability to act on multiple D-amino acids is due to a side chain-independent mode of substrate recognition.

     The study's findings provide a clear explanation for how DTD selectively removes D-amino acids from tRNAs while allowing L-amino acids to pass through. The researchers propose that the strict chiral specificity of DTD ensures that the cellular pool of L-aminoacyl-tRNAs is enriched, preventing the incorporation of D-amino acids into proteins. This mechanism is essential for maintaining homochirality in proteins and preventing the incorporation of D-amino acids, which could disrupt protein function.

     The study's results also highlight the importance of weak hydrogen bonds in D-chirality selection. The researchers found that if an L-amino acid were to bind in the active site, it would have to adopt one of three possible conformations, making it less likely to be recognized by the enzyme. Overall, the study provides new insights into the enantioselective mechanism of DTD and its crucial role in enforcing homochirality in proteins.
summary
     The cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator (CFTR) is a chloride ion channel that is mutated in cystic fibrosis (CF) patients. CFTR channels are gated by cytosolic ATP, which induces the dimerization of two cytosolic nucleotide-binding domains (NBDs). This dimerization opens the pore, while disruption of ATP hydrolysis closes it. In wild-type CFTR, spontaneous openings without ATP are rare, but in certain CF mutants, they constitute the only gating mechanism, stimulated by ivacaftor, a clinically approved CFTR potentiator.

     Researchers have correlated energetic coupling between residues across the dimer interface with spontaneous pore opening and closure in single CFTR channels. They found that spontaneous openings are strictly coupled to NBD dimerization, which may occur even without ATP. This suggests that coordinated NBD/pore movements are intrinsic to CFTR, and ATP alters the stability, but not the fundamental structural architecture, of open- and closed-pore conformations.

     The study shows that the presence of bound ATP does not alter the global arrangement of conformations, but rather acts as a'molecular glue' to stabilize the transition state and the open state. The researchers also found that the ligand binding site is occluded in open channels, making it impossible to define an open-state 'binding affinity'. This means that even when disruption of ATP hydrolysis forces CFTR channels to gate at equilibrium, that gating follows a reversible linear sequence, rather than a cycle subject to the principle of detailed balance.

     The study provides insights into the mechanism of CFTR channel opening and closure, and helps explain the effects of mutations, phosphorylation, and drugs on ATP-driven and spontaneous activity. It also constrains mechanistic models of ATP-dependent channel gating, and understanding of the underlying driving forces, which are likely conserved among ABC proteins. The findings have implications for understanding CF mutation and drug mechanisms, and may lead to the development of new treatments for cystic fibrosis.
Biomedical researchers have identified a previously unknown force that drives the assembly of transmembrane proteins in lipid bilayers. This force, referred to as the "orderphobic effect," arises from the interaction between the protein and the membrane's phase transition from an ordered to a disordered state. The study used large-scale molecular simulations to demonstrate that a protein embedded in an ordered membrane can stabilize a microscopic interface between the ordered and disordered phases. This interface has a finite stiffness, and when two such proteins approach each other, they assemble due to the reduction in interfacial energy.

The orderphobic effect is analogous to the hydrophobic effect, where the strength and range of the force depend on the protein's ability to induce a disordered layer in its vicinity. This ability is influenced by the proximity to the membrane's phase transition and the protein's size and hydrophobic mismatch with the membrane. The study found that a transmembrane protein with a hydrophobic thickness equal to that of the disordered phase can melt the ordered phase near the protein, resulting in the formation of an order-disorder interface.

The researchers also predicted that the orderphobic effect can lead to enhanced lipid mobility surrounding the protein, which may be experimentally testable using single molecule tracking techniques. The study's findings have implications for understanding the behavior of biological membranes and transmembrane proteins, which are complex systems with multiple components and multiple order-disorder transitions. The orderphobic effect is a dual phenomenon, where a transmembrane protein in the disordered phase can also induce order in its vicinity, leading to the formation of an order-disorder interface and a force for assembly.

The study's results suggest that the orderphobic effect is a significant force that can drive the assembly of transmembrane proteins, and its strength and range are larger than those that could arise from membrane elasticity. The findings provide new insights into the behavior of biological membranes and transmembrane proteins, and may have implications for understanding various cellular processes, including protein-protein interactions and membrane organization.
Biomedical researchers have long used fluorescent calcium indicators to study neural dynamics, but the relationship between these indicators and action potentials (APs) has remained unclear. To address this, a recent study characterized the AP-fluorescence transfer function in vivo for 48 layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons in the primary visual cortex of transgenic mice expressing GCaMP6s or GCaMP6f. The researchers used simultaneous calcium imaging and cell-attached recordings to investigate the relationship between APs and fluorescence signals.

The study found that under optimal conditions, most APs were detected, but under conditions typical of population imaging studies, only a minority of 1 AP and 2 AP events were detected, often less than 10% and 20-30%, respectively. This highlights the limits of AP detection under more realistic imaging conditions. The researchers also noted that population imaging studies, which are necessary for studying large populations of neurons, compromise AP detection due to the large field of view and reduced photon flux per neuron.

To construct AP-calcium fluorescence response curves, the researchers identified isolated AP events and summed APs over 250 ms. They found that only a minority of APs were used to construct these curves. The study also tested the performance of several spike inference models, detecting APs under optimal and less optimal conditions. The results provide insight into the relationship between spiking activity in vivo and fluorescence signals and will aid the interpretation of existing and future calcium imaging datasets.

The study's findings have implications for the use of genetically encoded calcium indicators (GECIs) in population imaging studies. GECIs are widely used with two-photon laser scanning microscopy to report neuronal activity within local populations in vivo. However, the study's results suggest that the relationship between APs and fluorescence signals is more complex than previously thought, and that AP detection is compromised under conditions typical of population imaging studies. The study's results will facilitate the interpretation of existing and future calcium imaging studies using mainstream transgenic mouse lines, such as the Allen Institute's Brain Observatory Visual Coding dataset.
summary
     Biologists have discovered a unique biological switching valve in the female genitalia of the Brazilian cave insect Neotrogla. This valve allows females to control the direction of seminal flow, enabling them to receive multiple seminal packages from the same or different males within a short time span. The valve complex is composed of two plate-like sclerites, a closure element, and in-and-outflow canals. Females of Neotrogla have a penis-like intromittent organ that they use to coercively anchor males and obtain voluminous semen. The semen is packed in a capsule, which is not only used for fertilization but also serves as a source of nutrition for the female.

     The switching valve has two slots for insemination, allowing Neotrogla females to continue mating while the first slot is occupied. This adaptation enables females to compete for seminal gifts in their nutrient-poor cave habitats through long copulation times and multiple seminal injections. The evolution of this switching valve may have been a prerequisite for the reversal of the intromittent organ in Neotrogla.

     In Neotrogla, males are held by the female's spiny penis during copulation, which can last up to 40-70 hours. This is significantly longer than in close relatives, where copulation lasts around 50 minutes. The long copulation duration in Neotrogla is thought to be an adaptation for females to obtain more semen from males. The switching valve allows females to selectively use one of two slots for insemination, leaving the other slot empty. This enables them to immediately receive an additional seminal package from the same or other males.

     The presence of a spermathecal plate with a valve function to control seminal flow may further refine our understanding of the causes and consequences of the reversed sexual selection in this genus. Female-female competition for males to receive nutritious seminal substances is considered a key factor driving the evolution of the female penis in Neotrogla. In contrast, a closely related species lacking genital structure reversal, Lepinotus patruelis, has a much shorter copulation duration of 50 minutes. The female penis of Neotrogla bears spines, which females use to anchor males coercively during copulation, likely to obtain more semen.
Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand the complex process of synaptic exocytosis, the release of neurotransmitters from neurons. A crucial step in this process is the assembly of soluble N-ethylmaleimide-sensitive factor attachment receptor (SNARE) proteins, which couple their stage-wise folding and assembly to rapid exocytosis. However, the functions of the different assembly stages and the role of Munc18-1, a protein that regulates SNARE assembly, were not well understood.

    Using optical tweezers, researchers observed four distinct stages of assembly in SNARE N-terminal, middle, C-terminal, and linker domains (NTD, MD, CTD, and LD, respectively). They found that SNARE layer mutations differentially affect SNARE assembly, with the NTD and CTD responsible for vesicle docking and fusion, respectively, and the MD regulating SNARE assembly and fusion. Munc18-1 initiates SNARE assembly and structures the t-SNARE C-terminus independent of the syntaxin N-terminal regulatory domain (NRD) and stabilizes the half-zippered SNARE complex dependent upon the NRD.

    The researchers used optical tweezers to study the forces associated with Munc18-1-mediated de novo SNARE assembly. They found that Munc18-1 enhances de novo SNARE assembly by holding the SNAP-25 molecule together with the tethered syntaxin or VAMP2 as the t-SNARE complex unfolds. In the absence of Munc18-1, no SNARE reassembly was observed due to dissociation of the SNAP-25 molecule. In contrast, in the presence of Munc18-1, 45% of completely unfolded SNARE complexes showed full reassembly.

    The researchers also observed three force-dependent activities of Munc18-1 in SNARE assembly, consistent with previously reported multiple modes of binding between Munc18-1 and the SNARE complex. They propose a working model to account for the multiple roles of Munc18-1 in stage-wise SNARE zippering and membrane fusion. According to this model, Munc18-1 regulates every stage of SNARE assembly, from initiation to stabilization of the half-zippered SNARE complex. The researchers conclude that Munc18-1 mainly acts on the folding intermediates of SNAREs to chaperone their assembly. This study provides new insights into the complex process of synaptic exocytosis and the role of Munc18-1 in regulating SNARE assembly.
summary
     Biologists have long sought to understand how cells form diverse adhesion sites on their surface to interact with the extracellular matrix. These adhesion sites, called focal adhesions, are crucial for cell movement, growth, and signaling. A team of researchers investigated how the integrin adhesome, a group of proteins that make up focal adhesions, assembles into these sites.

     The study found that the integrin adhesome is already pre-assembled in the cell's cytosol as multi-protein building blocks. These building blocks can rapidly and modularly assemble into adhesion sites on the cell surface. The researchers used two techniques, fluorescence cross-correlation spectroscopy (FCCS) and fluorescence recovery after photobleaching (FRAP), to observe the dynamic exchange of these building blocks between adhesion sites and the cytosol.

     The study revealed that stationary focal adhesions release the same types of protein complexes that they recruit, maintaining a uniform pool of building blocks in the cytosol. This symmetric exchange ensures that the building blocks are not altered as they move between adhesion sites and the cytosol. The researchers propose a model in which the pre-assembled building blocks enable rapid and modular self-assembly of adhesion sites.

     The study also found that the building blocks in the cytosol cannot form larger structures due to dependencies between protein interactions. However, at the plasma membrane, the system can be "switched on" to self-assemble adhesion sites by passing through checkpoints that induce additional protein interactions in the integrin adhesome.

     The researchers conclude that the integrin adhesome is extensively pre-assembled in the cytosol, forming multi-protein building blocks for cell-matrix adhesion sites. These building blocks are size-confined and correspond to functional layers across focal adhesions, including anchoring and integrin regulation, force transduction, and actin regulation. The symmetric material exchange between adhesion sites and the cytosol ensures that the building blocks enter and exit adhesion sites without being altered, preserving the assembly logic of the system.
Researchers have identified a common electrophysiological marker that can track states of reduced arousal in humans, including different sleep stages and anesthesia. This marker is based on the 1/f spectral slope of the electrophysiological power spectrum, which reflects the non-oscillatory, scale-free component of neural activity. The study used intracranial and surface EEG recordings from four independent data sets to demonstrate that the spectral slope can distinguish wakefulness from propofol anesthesia, non-rapid eye movement (NREM) sleep, and rapid eye movement (REM) sleep.

The researchers found that the spectral slope becomes more negative, or steeper, in sleep and under anesthesia compared to wakefulness. This suggests that the spectral slope can be used to track changes in arousal levels in both propofol anesthesia and sleep in humans with high temporal precision and on a wide range of timescales. The study also showed that the spectral slope can discriminate wakefulness from NREM and REM sleep based solely on the neurophysiological brain state.

The researchers used two datasets obtained during a full night of sleep to examine if the spectral slope could discern wakefulness from different sleep stages. They found that the spectral slope reliably differentiates wakefulness from all three states of reduced arousal level, namely REM, N3 sleep, and general anesthesia with propofol. The study also demonstrated that the non-oscillatory, aperiodic part of the power spectrum, which is devoid of prominent low-frequency oscillatory components, can be approximated by the 1/f decay of the power spectrum estimated from the 30 to 45 Hz frequency range.

The findings of this study provide empirical evidence for the notion that the slope in the range from 30 to 45 Hz correlates well with all stages of arousal. The study's results also provide a mechanistic link between the spectral slope and population synchrony during slow oscillations. Overall, the study's findings describe a common electrophysiological marker that can track states of reduced arousal in humans, including different sleep stages and anesthesia. This marker has the potential to be used in various clinical and research settings to monitor and study arousal levels in humans.
summary
     Biologists have discovered a new mechanism by which plant cells move chloroplasts to the nucleus during an innate immune response. Chloroplasts are organelles found in plant cells that play a crucial role in photosynthesis and other cellular processes. During an immune response, chloroplasts need to move to the nucleus to communicate with the cell's genetic material and coordinate a defense response.

     The researchers found that dynamic tubular extensions from chloroplasts called stromules play a key role in this process. Stromules extend along microtubules, which are part of the cell's cytoskeleton, and anchor to actin filaments, another component of the cytoskeleton. The direction of stromule extension is correlated with the direction of chloroplast movement, suggesting that stromules guide chloroplasts to the nucleus.

     The study showed that microtubules are essential for stromule extension and movement, while actin filaments provide anchor points for stromules. When microtubules are stabilized, stromule numbers and length increase, and when microtubules are depolymerized, stromules retract. This suggests that microtubules play a crucial role in regulating stromule dynamics.

     The researchers propose a model in which stromules extend along microtubules and attach to actin anchor points surrounding the nucleus, guiding chloroplasts to the nucleus during an innate immune response. This process is not limited to immune responses, as stromule-directed chloroplast movement was observed in steady-state conditions without immune induction, suggesting it is a general function of stromules in epidermal cells.

     The study provides new insights into the role of the cytoskeleton in plant cell biology and has implications for our understanding of innate immunity in plants. Further research is needed to investigate the molecular components required for stromule dynamics and the importance of perinuclear chloroplast clustering during innate immunity. The findings of this study contribute to our understanding of the complex interactions between chloroplasts, the nucleus, and the cytoskeleton in plant cells.
Summary
     Scientists have developed a new method to predict how proteins interact with each other at the molecular level. This prediction is based on the idea that amino acid sequences of residues that make contact across protein interfaces covary during evolution. In other words, if two proteins interact with each other, their corresponding amino acid sequences are more likely to change in a coordinated way over time.

     To test this idea, researchers applied their method to 50S ribosomal units and 28 additional bacterial protein complexes with known structures. They found that the predicted residue pairs were almost always in contact in the complex, provided that there were enough aligned sequences. This suggests that the method is accurate and reliable.

     The researchers then used this method to predict contacts across protein interfaces for 36 evolutionarily conserved complexes of unknown structure. They presented structure models for four of these complexes, including the tripartite ATP-independent periplasmic (TRAP) transporter, the tripartite efflux system, the pyruvate formate lyase-activating enzyme complex, and the methionine ABC transporter.

     The study found that covarying pairs of positions are almost always in contact in the three-dimensional structure, provided there are sufficient aligned sequences. They also found that significant residue-residue covariance occurs frequently between physically interacting protein pairs but very rarely between non-interacting pairs. This suggests that the method can be used to predict whether two proteins interact with each other.

     The researchers used a pseudo-likelihood-based statistical method to examine residue-residue covariation across protein-protein interfaces. They applied this method to a large set of complexes of known structure and found that it was effective in predicting contacts across interfaces. This method has the potential to contribute to the determination of the structures of biologically important complexes.

     The study provides a new tool for predicting protein-protein interactions and has the potential to advance our understanding of protein structure and function. The predicted residue-residue interactions across protein interfaces of unknown structure are presented in Figure 3, which shows strongly co-evolving residue pairs for complexes without known structure that had at least one prediction with a GREMLIN score greater than or equal to 0.85.
Summary of Biomedical Research Article: Active Sensing in the Categorization of Visual Patterns

     Researchers investigated how people interpret visual scenes by accumulating information from multiple locations. They used a novel approach called active sensing, where participants' eye movements were tracked while they categorized visual patterns. The results showed that participants' scan paths followed an active sensing strategy, incorporating information already acquired about the scene and knowledge of the statistical structure of patterns.

     The study used a gaze-contingent paradigm, where small apertures of the image were successively revealed at each fixation location. This allowed the researchers to estimate the efficiency of eye movements in a high-level task on a fixation-by-fixation basis. The findings showed that participants' eye movements were substantially more efficient than heuristic strategies that only employed a subset of the elements of a fully closed-loop active sensing strategy.

     The researchers used a combination of a Bayesian ideal observer and an active sensor algorithm to estimate that a major portion of the apparent suboptimality of fixation locations arises from prior biases, perceptual noise, and inaccuracies in eye movements. The central process of selecting fixation locations was found to be around 70% efficient in the task.

     The study suggests that participants select eye movements with the goal of maximizing information about abstract categories that require the integration of information from multiple locations. The results provide new insights into how people interpret visual scenes and how eye movements contribute to this process. The study's approach can be applied to multiple tasks in different perceptual domains, allowing for a more principled quantitative analysis of eye movements.

     The researchers dissociated the contributions of the eye-movement selection process from those of perceptual, motor, and decision processes. This allowed them to identify predominant sources of apparent sub-optimality in active sensing and quantify the efficiency of choosing each individual fixation throughout scanning a whole scene. The study's findings have implications for understanding how people process visual information and how eye movements contribute to this process.
Biologists have long understood that the nuclear pore complex (NPC) plays a crucial role in regulating the transport of molecules between the nucleus and cytoplasm of eukaryotic cells. Despite its importance, the mechanisms governing the transport of large cargoes, such as pathogens, mRNAs, and pre-ribosomes, across the NPC are not well understood. These large cargoes can be up to 15 nanometers in size and must pass through the NPC intact.

    Researchers have investigated the nuclear import kinetics of 30 large cargo models, ranging in size from 17 to 36 nanometers, with varying numbers of nuclear localization sequences (NLSs). The NLSs allow the cargoes to bind to nuclear transport receptors, which facilitate their entry into the nucleus. The study found that the requirements for nuclear transport scale non-linearly with cargo size and can be recapitulated by a simple two-parameter biophysical model.

    The model correlates the import flux with the energetic requirements for nuclear transport, providing new insights into the molecular determinants of large cargo import in cells. For small cargo transport, the biochemical or physicochemical properties of the cargo surface have been shown to influence nuclear transport. The researchers anticipate that their findings could also contribute to a better understanding of the export of large cargoes, such as pre-ribosomal subunits and large RNA export complexes.

    The study used a novel approach to assay nuclear import kinetics, allowing for the examination of a wide range of cargo sizes and NLS numbers.
