The results demonstrate that the transport of large cargoes is a complex process, influenced by both the size of the cargo and the number of NLSs. The findings have important implications for our understanding of nucleocytoplasmic transport and could potentially inform the development of new therapeutic strategies for diseases related to nuclear transport dysfunction.

    Nuclear transport is a critical process in eukaryotic cells, with over 2,000 nuclear pore complexes spanning the nuclear envelope in a typical HeLa cell. The transport of large cargoes across the NPC is essential for cellular viability, and disruptions to this process have been implicated in various diseases. The research presented here provides new insights into the molecular determinants of large cargo import, shedding light on the complex mechanisms governing nucleocytoplasmic transport.
summary
     Biomedical research has long recognized the importance of the co-stimulatory molecule CD28 in the activation of helper T cells. However, its role in maintaining T cell responses following activation has remained unclear. A recent study aimed to determine the function of CD28 after T cell priming by generating a strain of mice where CD28 is removed from CD4+ T cells after priming. The results show that continued CD28 expression is essential for the expansion of effector CD4+ T cells, particularly T helper type 1 (Th1) cells, and for the differentiation and maintenance of T follicular helper (Tfh) cells during viral infection. Furthermore, the study found that persistent CD28 expression is also required for the clearance of the bacterium Citrobacter rodentium from the gastrointestinal tract.

The study demonstrates that CD28 plays a previously undescribed role in helper T cell polarization in response to infection, distinct from its well-established function in T cell priming. CD28 expression is required after T cell priming for the expansion of Th1 cells and for the differentiation and maintenance of Tfh cells. These findings suggest that CD28 expression is necessary for intact effector CD4+ T cell responses during infection. The study also highlights the importance of CD28 in the immune response to bacterial infection, specifically in the context of Citrobacter rodentium infection, which elicits a robust humoral and cellular immune response.

Previous research has established that CD28 signaling is essential for multiple facets of CD4+ T cell activation, including proliferation, survival, glucose metabolism, and migration. CD28-deficient mice have been shown to have reduced expansion of effector CD4+ T cells and are unable to form Th1 and Tfh cells after infection or immunization. In contrast, Th1 cells do not require CD28 for their maintenance, but do require it for their expansion following T cell activation. The study's findings provide new insights into the role of CD28 in the immune response and highlight its importance in maintaining T cell responses following activation.
summary
     Helicobacter pylori, a type of bacteria that colonizes the stomach, has a unique helical shape that allows it to efficiently colonize the stomach lining. However, the molecular mechanisms that generate this helical shape are not well understood. Researchers investigated how H. pylori maintains its helical shape and found that two cytoskeletal proteins, MreB and CcmA, play a crucial role in defining zones of enhanced cell wall synthesis.

     The researchers used two metabolic probes to track cell wall growth in H. pylori and found that synthesis is enhanced at both negative and positive Gaussian curvatures on the cell surface. They also used superresolution microscopy and 3D quantitative image analysis to visualize the localization of MreB and CcmA. The results showed that MreB is most abundant at negative Gaussian curvature, while CcmA is most abundant at positive Gaussian curvature.

     The researchers propose that MreB promotes cell wall synthesis at negative Gaussian curvature, while CcmA promotes synthesis at positive Gaussian curvature. They suggest that this patterning of cell wall synthesis is one mechanism necessary for maintaining the helical shape of H. pylori. The study also found that H. pylori uses distinct mechanisms to achieve the broad distribution of sidewall Gaussian curvature compared to other curved-rod bacteria.

     The findings of this study provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms that generate the unique helical shape of H. pylori. The researchers propose a model where MreB and CcmA work together to promote cell wall synthesis at different regions of the cell surface, allowing H. pylori to maintain its helical shape and efficiently colonize the stomach lining. This study contributes to our understanding of the complex interactions between cytoskeletal proteins and cell wall synthesis in bacteria, and has implications for the development of new treatments for H. pylori infections.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have identified a new function of the protein Cdc48 in regulating mitochondrial dynamics. Cdc48, a ubiquitin-selective chaperone, works with two deubiquitylases (DUBs), Ubp12 and Ubp2, to control the balance between mitochondrial fusion and fission. Mitochondrial fusion is a process by which mitochondria combine to form a single, larger mitochondrion, while fission is the process by which a single mitochondrion divides into two smaller ones.

     The researchers found that Ubp12 and Ubp2 have opposing effects on mitochondrial fusion. Ubp12 promotes fusion by stabilizing ubiquitin chains on the mitofusin protein Fzo1, while Ubp2 inhibits fusion by removing ubiquitin chains from Fzo1. Cdc48 integrates the activities of these two DUBs by promoting the proteolysis of Ubp12, which in turn stabilizes pro-fusion ubiquitylation on Fzo1. On the other hand, the loss of Ubp12 stabilizes Ubp2, allowing it to remove ubiquitin chains from Fzo1 and inhibit fusion.

     The study reveals a new cascade of ubiquitylation events involving Cdc48, Ubp12, and Ubp2, which fine-tunes the fusogenic activity of Fzo1. This cascade ensures a tight control of Fzo1 ubiquitylation, allowing for mitochondrial fusion while preventing unnecessary ubiquitylation that targets Fzo1 for proteasomal turnover. The researchers also found that Ubp12 recognizes ubiquitylated chains on Fzo1 composed of a small number of ubiquitin moieties, which are too short to target proteasomal turnover. In contrast, Ubp2 recognizes and cleaves longer ubiquitin chains on Fzo1 that target Fzo1 for proteasomal degradation and inhibit mitochondrial fusion.

     The study provides new insights into the regulation of mitochondrial dynamics and the role of ubiquitylation in controlling the balance between fusion and fission. The findings suggest that Cdc48 plays a crucial role in integrating the activities of Ubp12 and Ubp2 to regulate mitochondrial fusion, and that the balance between these two DUBs is critical for maintaining mitochondrial homeostasis.
Biomedical researchers investigated whether vision is necessary for the development of the categorical organization of the Ventral Occipito-Temporal Cortex (VOTC), a region of the brain involved in processing visual information. They used functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) to study how the VOTC responds to sound and visual categories in sighted and blind individuals.

The study found that the VOTC reliably encodes sound categories in both sighted and blind people, using a similar representational structure and connectivity to the one found in vision. However, sound categories were more reliably encoded in the blind group, using a representational format closer to the one found in vision. Crucially, the VOTC in blind individuals represents the categorical membership of sounds rather than their acoustic features.

The results suggest that sounds can trigger categorical responses in the VOTC of both congenitally blind and sighted people, which partially match the topography and functional profile of the visual response. However, there are qualitative nuances in the categorical organization of VOTC between modalities and groups.

The study demonstrates that a categorical organization similar to the one found in vision can emerge in VOTC in the absence of visual experience. In fact, the categorical response to sounds is enhanced and more stable in congenitally blind people. This is consistent with previous studies showing that the occipital cortex enhances its response to non-visual information processing in the absence of vision.

The researchers used representational similarity analysis to show that categorical membership is the main factor that predicts the representational structure of sounds in VOTC in blind people, rather than lower-level acoustic attributes of sounds. The study also found that the categorical representation of auditory stimuli in VOTC of both blind and sighted individuals exhibits different qualitative nuances compared to the categorical organization generated by visual stimuli in sighted people.

The findings suggest that crossmodal plasticity, or the brain's ability to reorganize itself in response to changes in sensory input, may play a role in the development of categorical organization in VOTC. Specifically, the overlap in categorical responses between groups may be due to the fact that VOTC represents visual attributes in the sighted and acoustic attributes in the blind.
Researchers have discovered a strain of the fungal pathogen Entomophthora muscae that manipulates the behavior of Drosophila melanogaster, the common fruit fly, in the laboratory. This fungus, known as E. muscae 'Berkeley', causes infected flies to exhibit moribund behaviors, such as climbing upward, extending their proboscides, and raising their wings, which allows the fungus to launch infectious spores from their abdomens. The fungus invades the nervous system of the fly, suggesting a direct means by which it induces behavioral changes.

The researchers established a method to infect D. melanogaster with E. muscae 'Berkeley' in the laboratory, allowing for the study of this behavior-changing fungus in a controlled environment. This new system is expected to facilitate rapid progress in understanding how E. muscae manipulates host behavior, given the vast molecular toolkit available for D. melanogaster.

The invasion of the nervous system by E. muscae 'Berkeley' may be a key factor in achieving behavioral manipulation of the host fly. However, the researchers caution that other mechanisms may also be at play. They propose four possible ways in which E. muscae 'Berkeley' could alter host behavior.

The researchers also tested whether E. muscae 'Berkeley' can infect hosts outside of Drosophilidae, the family of flies to which D. melanogaster belongs. They exposed house flies to E. muscae 'Berkeley'-infected D. melanogaster and observed that the house flies died and sporulated, indicating that they had been killed by the fungus.

To gain a comprehensive understanding of how E. muscae 'Berkeley' infection progresses in D. melanogaster at the molecular level, the researchers measured transcription changes in both the host and the fungus at 24-hour time points over five days. They found that infected and uninfected animals were phenotypically indistinguishable early on, making it challenging to track the progression of the infection.

The study highlights the potential of the E. muscae 'Berkeley'-D. melanogaster system as a model for developing a mechanistic understanding of parasitic manipulation of host behavior. Further research is needed to elucidate the molecular basis of behavioral manipulation and to establish genetic tools in both the fungus and the host, as well as neurobiological tools in the host.
Summary of Transcriptome Maps of General Eukaryotic RNA Degradation Factors

     Researchers have mapped the binding locations of 30 RNA degradation factors in the yeast Saccharomyces cerevisiae to better understand RNA processing and degradation in eukaryotic cells. The study used a technique called photoactivatable ribonucleoside-enhanced crosslinking and immunoprecipitation (PAR-CLIP) to generate transcriptome-wide protein binding profiles for the degradation factors.

     The analysis revealed how degradation factors vary in their binding specificities for different classes of RNAs and their preferred locations on RNA transcripts. The study found that most degradation factors bind intact mRNAs, while decapping factors are recruited only for mRNA degradation. This suggests that decapping is a rate-limiting step in the degradation process.

     The study also found that decapping factors preferentially bind mRNAs with non-optimal codons, consistent with the rapid degradation of inefficiently translated mRNAs. Additionally, the nuclear surveillance machinery, including the complexes Nrd1/Nab3 and TRAMP4, targets aberrant nuclear RNAs and processes small nucleolar RNAs (snoRNAs).

     Global comparisons of the profiles alongside previously published profiles of other RNA-binding factors revealed clusters of factors that co-occupy RNAs or co-localize on RNAs. The large variety of different RNA degradation factors poses the question of how RNA degradation pathways are selected and whether the RNA sequence can influence this selection.

     The study provides a rich resource for future studies of eukaryotic RNA degradation pathways, mechanisms, and the integration of mRNA metabolism. The findings have implications for understanding the regulation of RNA levels, the surveillance of aberrant or poorly functional RNAs, and the processing of RNAs in eukaryotic cells.
summary
     Biologists have long sought to understand how plants rapidly respond to pathogen threats. Arabidopsis thaliana, a small flowering plant, produces camalexin, a potent defense compound, in response to bacterial and fungal infections. While several transcription factors controlling camalexin biosynthesis genes have been identified, the mechanisms enabling rapid activation of these genes upon pathogen signal remain unclear. Researchers combined publicly available epigenomic data with in vivo chromatin modification mapping to investigate this process. They found that camalexin biosynthesis genes are marked with two epigenetic modifications: trimethylation of histone 3 lysine 27 (H3K27me3), which represses gene expression, and acetylation of histone 3 lysine 18 (H3K18ac), which activates gene expression. These modifications form a previously uncharacterized type of bivalent chromatin, which the researchers named kairostat. Mutants with reduced H3K27me3 or H3K18ac showed that both modifications are required to determine the timing of gene expression and metabolite accumulation at an early stage of the stress response. The study suggests that kairostat plays a crucial role in controlling the timely induction of gene expression upon stress stimuli in plants. By integrating epigenomic profiles with a genome-wide metabolic network, the researchers found that H3K27me3 and H3K18ac are enriched at camalexin biosynthesis and several other specialized metabolic pathways. In mutants with defective deposition of H3K27me3 or H3K18ac, the induction kinetics of camalexin biosynthesis genes and metabolite accumulation was accelerated or delayed, respectively. The findings provide new evidence for how chemical defense mediated by camalexin may be regulated at the epigenetic level. The discovery of kairostat offers a molecular handle for future investigations into the regulation of camalexin biosynthesis genes.
summary
     Biomedical research has long sought to understand the epigenetic mechanisms that enable specialized astrocytes to retain neurogenic competence throughout adult life. Recent studies have shed light on the role of the histone methyltransferase EZH2 in this process. EZH2, a Polycomb repressive factor, is expressed in astrocytes that serve as neural stem cells ( NSCs ) in the adult mouse subventricular zone ( SVZ ). This epigenetic regulator is required for neurogenesis, independent of its role in SVZ NSC proliferation.

     Research has shown that EZH2 directly represses the Ink4a/Arf locus to enable SVZ NSC self-renewal. Furthermore, EZH2 prevents the inappropriate activation of genes associated with non-SVZ neuronal subtypes. Olig2, a bHLH transcription factor, is a direct target of EZH2, and repression of this factor is critical for neuronal differentiation.

     Studies have also identified distinct and separable roles for EZH2 in the production of new neurons from a multipotent stem cell population. In the human brain, EZH2 is expressed in astroglia and neuronal precursors of the early infant SVZ, suggesting that this PRC2 factor plays key roles in postnatal human neurogenesis. These findings indicate that EZH2 is a critical epigenetic determinant for the maintenance of multipotent astroglia, enabling both proliferation and cell fate specification of an adult stem cell population.

     Acute deletion of Ezh2 in SVZ NSCs resulted in decreased neurogenesis, and this deficiency in part related to reduced cell proliferation. However, Ink4a/Arf-deficiency in Ezh2-deleted SVZ NSCs rescued cell proliferation, but neurogenesis remained defective. This suggests that EZH2 has a role in SVZ neurogenesis that is distinct from its function in Ink4a/Arf repression.

     These results provide novel insights into the complex role that EZH2 plays in normal development and gliomagenesis. Future studies may address whether re-expression of EZH2 in non-SVZ astrocytes can enable neurogenesis or their directed transdifferentiation into neurons in vivo for potential therapeutic purposes. The identification of EZH2 as a distinguishing feature of astroglial cells in the postnatal neurogenic SVZ of both the mouse and human brain has important implications for regenerative medicine and oncogenesis.
summary
     Biologists have long known that viruses play a crucial role in shaping microbial communities and influencing ecosystem functions. However, studying the interactions between viruses and their hosts has been challenging due to the difficulty of cultivating certain microorganisms in the laboratory. A recent study used single-cell genomics to investigate the ecology and evolution of viruses infecting uncultivated SUP05 bacteria in a model marine oxygen minimum zone (OMZ).

     The researchers isolated 127 single-cell amplified genomes (SAGs) from SUP05 bacteria in the OMZ and identified 69 viral contigs representing five new genera within the dsDNA Caudovirales and ssDNA Microviridae families. They found that approximately one-third of the SUP05 bacteria were infected with viruses, with higher infection frequencies in areas with more severe oxygen deficiency. The study also revealed that Microviridae viruses were clonal, suggesting that they were recovered from bloom-terminating viruses, while systematic co-infection between dsDNA and ssDNA viruses indicated previously unrecognized cooperation modes.

     The researchers analyzed 186 microbial and viral metagenomes from the OMZ and found that SUP05 viruses persisted for years but remained endemic to the OMZ. Notably, they identified virus-encoded dissimilatory sulfite reductase, which suggests that SUP05 viruses can reprogram their host's energy metabolism. This finding has significant implications for our understanding of biogeochemical cycling in climate-critical and expanding OMZs.

     The study used single-cell genomics to directly link SUP05 viruses and their hosts across defined gradients of oxygen and sulfide over a three-year time interval in the OMZ ecosystem. The researchers obtained a SUP05 single-cell genomic dataset spanning defined redox gradients in the Saanich Inlet water column and identified SUP05-associated viruses infecting SAGs. They used the resulting virus-host pairs as recruitment platforms to estimate viral diversity, activity, dispersion, and potential impact on SUP05 population dynamics and metabolic capacity.

     The study found that SUP05 Microviridae contigs were inconsistently detected due to methodological biases, so they focused on dsDNA viral contigs. All four SUP05 Caudovirales contigs were absent from surface waters but repeatedly detected within and below the oxycline, consistent with SUP05 water column disposition. The researchers also found that putative SUP05 phage contained genes encoding reverse dissimilatory sulfite reductase A and C, positing a role for viruses in modulating the marine sulfur cycle.

     The study's findings demonstrate closely coupled SUP05 virus-host co-evolutionary dynamics with the potential to modulate biogeochemical cycling in climate-critical and expanding OMZs. The identification of virus-encoded dissimilatory sulfite reductase suggests that viruses can reprogram their host's energy metabolism, highlighting the complex interactions between viruses and their hosts in shaping ecosystem functions.
summary
     Biologists have long understood that cilia and flagella are assembled and maintained through a process called intraflagellar transport (IFT), which involves over 20 IFT proteins. However, the functions of individual IFT proteins have remained unclear. A recent study focused on TTC26/DYF13, a putative IFT protein, to better understand its role in IFT.

     The study found that TTC26/DYF13 is an IFT complex B protein in mammalian cells and the green algae Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. When TTC26/DYF13 was knocked down in zebrafish embryos or mutated in C. reinhardtii, the resulting cilia and flagella were short and had abnormal motility. Surprisingly, the assembly and movement of IFT particles were normal in the dyf13 mutant flagella, unlike in other IFT complex B mutants.

     Further analysis revealed that a specific set of proteins involved in motility was depleted in the dyf13 mutant. This suggests that different IFT proteins are responsible for transporting different subsets of cargo, providing a possible explanation for the complexity of the IFT machinery. The study also found that TTC26/DYF13 is not required for ciliogenesis, the process of forming cilia and flagella, but rather plays a specialized role in transporting motility-related ciliary proteins into cilia and flagella.

     In contrast to other IFT proteins, TTC26/DYF13 is not necessary for the assembly or movement of IFT particles. Instead, it specifically transports a subset of proteins involved in flagellar motility. This is supported by proteomic and biochemical analyses, which showed that specific inner dynein arm components were reduced in flagella of dyf13 mutants. The study also found that IFT56, another IFT protein, has a conserved role in cilia and flagella, and its knockdown in zebrafish embryos and C. reinhardtii dyf13 mutant cells resulted in motility defects.

     Overall, the study provides new insights into the role of TTC26/DYF13 in IFT and highlights the complexity of the IFT machinery. The findings suggest that different IFT proteins are responsible for transporting different subsets of cargo, and that TTC26/DYF13 plays a specialized role in transporting motility-related ciliary proteins into cilia and flagella. Further analysis is necessary to clarify the role of TTC26/DYF13 in determining the length of cilia and flagella.
summary
     Biomedical research has shown that the interaction between melanoma cells and lymphatic endothelial cells ( LECs ) plays a crucial role in the development of distant metastasis in melanoma. In a recent study, researchers investigated the molecular mechanisms underlying this interaction and its impact on melanoma progression. The study found that exposure of melanoma cells to LECs in 3D co-culture facilitated distant organ metastasis in mice. This effect was observed in expansively growing human WM852 melanoma cells, but not in singly invasive Bowes cells.

     To understand the underlying mechanisms, the researchers established LEC co-cultures with different melanoma cells originating from primary tumors or metastases. The results showed that the expansively growing metastatic melanoma cells adopted an invasively sprouting phenotype in 3D matrix, which was dependent on matrix-metalloproteinase-14 ( MMP14 ), Notch3, and Î²1-integrin. Notably, MMP14 was necessary for LEC-induced Notch3 induction and coincident Î²1-integrin activation.

     The study also found that MMP14 and Notch3 were required for LEC-mediated metastasis of zebrafish xenografts. The researchers used a combination of 2D and 3D LEC-melanoma co-culture models, transcriptome profiling, in vitro functional studies, and a zebrafish xenograft/metastasis assay to investigate the consequences of the interaction between melanoma cells and LECs.

     The results of the study provide new insights into the mechanisms of lymphatic dissemination in distant metastasis and highlight the importance of the interaction between melanoma cells and LECs in the tumor microenvironment. The findings suggest that LEC contact promotes melanoma metastasis by inducing a reversible switch from 3D growth to invasively sprouting cell phenotype. The study also identifies MMP14 and Notch3 as key molecules involved in this process, which may have implications for the development of new therapeutic strategies to prevent or treat melanoma metastasis.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have identified a crucial role for microRNA-9 (miR-9) in the development of dendrites, the branching extensions of neurons that receive and transmit signals. miR-9 is one of the most abundant microRNAs in the brain and has been well-characterized in neural progenitors, but its function in dendritic and synaptic development was not well understood.

     To study miR-9's role in vivo, the researchers developed a transgenic mouse line that allows for the conditional inactivation of miR-9 in a spatio-temporal-controlled manner. Using this novel approach, they found that miR-9 controls dendritic growth and synaptic transmission in the brain. Furthermore, they demonstrated that miR-9's downregulation of the transcriptional repressor REST is essential for proper dendritic growth.

     The study used a double reporter mouse line to characterize the activity pattern of miR-9 in different cell types in the brain. The analysis of the miR-9-SpNestin-Cre mouse line showed that inhibition of miR-9 results in aberrant dendritic growth in vivo, accompanied by a concomitant impairment in synaptic transmission. The researchers also developed a conditional transgenic sponge mouse model to study miR-9's role in postmitotic neuronal differentiation.

     The study focused on the postmitotic stages of neuronal differentiation and demonstrated a critical role for miR-9 in the control of dendritic development in vivo. Other microRNAs have also been shown to influence dendritic growth, suggesting that the expression of genes controlling this process is under complex post-transcriptional regulation. The researchers found that miR-9's regulation of REST expression is essential for proper dendritic growth and synaptic transmission.

     The study provides new insights into the role of miR-9 in the development of dendrites and synaptic transmission in the brain. The findings suggest that miR-9 is a key regulator of dendritic development and that its dysregulation may contribute to neurological disorders. Further research is needed to fully understand the mechanisms underlying miR-9's function and its potential therapeutic applications.
Biomedical researchers have identified a subtype of autism spectrum disorder (ASD) characterized by pronounced social visual engagement difficulties in early development. This subtype, known as GeoPref ASD, can be identified using a simple eye-tracking test that measures social or non-social visual preferences in toddlers. The study used resting-state functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) data from a large sample of ASD toddlers and non-ASD comparison groups to investigate the neural basis of social visual engagement difficulties in ASD.

The results show that ASD-related functional hypoconnectivity between the default mode network (DMN) and visual and attention networks is a key feature of early ASD development. Specifically, the DMN, which is involved in social cognition and self-referential thinking, is functionally disconnected from the occipito-temporal cortex (OTC), a region responsible for processing visual information. This hypoconnectivity is more pronounced in the GeoPref ASD subtype, which is characterized by early and pronounced social-visual circuit hypoconnectivity.

The study also found that increased DMN-OTC hypoconnectivity is related to increased severity of social-communication difficulties in GeoPref ASD, but not in other ASD subtypes. This suggests that early and pronounced social-visual circuit hypoconnectivity is a critical neurobiological feature of GeoPref ASD and may be a key factor in the development of social-communicative difficulties in these individuals.

The findings of this study provide new insights into the neural basis of social visual engagement difficulties in ASD and highlight the importance of early social-visual circuit hypoconnectivity in the development of social-communicative difficulties. The study's results also support the idea that heterogeneity in early social engagement is an important factor in explaining functional connectivity differences present in early ASD toddlers. The identification of GeoPref ASD as a distinct subtype of ASD with pronounced social visual engagement difficulties has important implications for the development of early intervention strategies and treatment targets for these individuals.
summary
     Biologists have long known that animals with a body cavity, or coelom, have blood cells that work together to maintain homeostasis, or a stable internal environment. This division of labor relies on a balance between blood cell production and differentiation, which occurs in specialized organs called hematopoietic organs. In fruit flies, also known as Drosophila melanogaster, the lymph gland produces two types of blood cells: plasmatocytes and crystal cells. Plasmatocytes are similar to macrophages in vertebrates and are involved in phagocytosis, or engulfing and digesting foreign particles. Crystal cells, on the other hand, have a different function, but their numbers increase during larval development.

    Until now, it was unclear how crystal cells increase in number, as mature crystal cells do not divide during larval stages. However, researchers have discovered that new crystal cells in third-instar larvae originate from plasmatocytes through a process called transdifferentiation, which is dependent on the Notch signaling pathway. This process occurs in sessile hemocyte clusters, which are clusters of blood cells attached to the body wall. The researchers found that the integrity of these clusters is essential for crystal cell differentiation.

    The study of hematopoietic organs and their structure is crucial for understanding how different types of blood cells arise and how their numbers are controlled. In Drosophila, embryonic hematopoiesis produces two types of mature hemocytes: plasmatocytes and crystal cells. Plasmatocytes are mitotically active and expand during larval development through self-renewal. In contrast, the numbers of crystal cells increase during larval development, but it was unclear whether this was due to self-renewal or de novo differentiation.

    The discovery of a hematopoietic tissue in Drosophila that relies on structure-dependent signaling events to promote blood homeostasis creates a new paradigm for addressing outstanding questions in Drosophila hematopoiesis. This finding also establishes further parallels with vertebrate systems, where macrophages are the most plastic cells in the hematopoietic tissue and their specialization depends on the local microenvironment. The study of hematopoietic organs and their structure is essential for understanding how different mature blood cells arise and how their absolute and relative numbers are controlled.
summary
     Biologists have long been puzzled by the phenomenon of aneuploidy, where cells have an abnormal number of chromosomes. Aneuploidy is linked to various diseases, but it also plays a role in the evolution of organisms. While laboratory strains are highly sensitive to aneuploidy, researchers have found that aneuploidy is surprisingly common in wild yeast isolates. These wild yeast strains show lower-than-expected expression at many amplified genes, suggesting that they have a mechanism to buffer the effects of aneuploidy.

     To investigate this further, the researchers generated diploid strain panels with cells carrying two, three, or four copies of specific chromosomes. They found that gene-dosage compensation functions at 10-30% of amplified genes in these strains. Genes subject to dosage compensation are under higher expression constraint in wild populations, but they show elevated rates of gene amplification. This suggests that copy-number variation is buffered at these genes, allowing them to tolerate aneuploidy.

     The researchers also found that aneuploidy provides a clear ecological advantage to certain wild yeast strains, such as oak strain YPS1009. This strain amplifies a causal gene that escapes dosage compensation, giving it a selective advantage. The study presents a model in which dosage compensation buffers gene amplification through aneuploidy, providing a natural but likely transient route to rapid phenotypic evolution.

     The findings provide new insights into how cells can tolerate the effects of aneuploidy long enough for new phenotypes to evolve. The study shows that up to 40% of amplified genes in naturally aneuploid strains show lower-than-expected expression. This reduced expression is due in part to heritable polymorphisms that down-regulate expression from amplified genes, some of which may have been selected for during adaptation to aneuploidy.

     The researchers investigated the expression of amplified genes in six aneuploid wild yeast strains, interrogating 2204 genes across eight amplified chromosomes. They found that some genes escape dosage compensation in response to aneuploidy and can contribute significantly to phenotypic variation. Whole and partial chromosome amplification is common in laboratory selection experiments, and the study suggests that dosage compensation may play a key role in buffering the effects of aneuploidy in natural populations.
summary
     Biologists have discovered a new type of light-sensitive cell in a flatworm called Maritigrella crozieri. These cells express a protein called xenopsin, which is a type of opsin photopigment. Traditionally, opsins were thought to be found only in the eyes of animals, but xenopsin is also found in extraocular cells around the brain in adult flatworms. These extraocular cells have hundreds of cilia, which are long, thin extensions of the cell membrane, enclosed within a vacuole called a phaosome.

     The researchers characterized the ultrastructure of these extraocular cells and found that they are similar to rod photoreceptors in jawed vertebrates, which also enclose their cilia within their own plasma membrane. To determine if xenopsin can form a photopigment and drive phototransduction, the researchers carried out functional assays in human cells. They found that xenopsin can drive phototransduction primarily by coupling to GÎ±i, a type of G protein.

     The discovery of xenopsin-expressing cells in flatworms challenges our understanding of opsin and photoreceptor evolution. Traditionally, it was thought that extraocular ciliary photoreceptors and ocular rhabdomeric/microvillar photoreceptors were distinct types of photoreceptors. However, the discovery of xenopsin-expressing cells in the eyes of protostome larvae and in extraocular cells in adult flatworms suggests that there may be more overlap between these types of photoreceptors than previously thought.

     The researchers also found that xenopsin is expressed in ciliary cells in the eyes of Maritigrella, which provides molecular and functional evidence for the presence of ciliary photoreceptors in flatworms. Ciliary photoreceptors are morphologically diverse, and the discovery of xenopsin-expressing cells in flatworms adds to our understanding of the evolution and diversity of photoreceptors in animals. Overall, the discovery of xenopsin-expressing cells in flatworms provides new insights into the evolution and function of photoreceptors in animals.
Summary
     Biologists have long known that spatial and temporal cues play a crucial role in specifying the diversity of neurons in the nervous system. However, it has remained unclear how these cues are integrated in neural progenitors, also known as neuroblasts. Researchers used the fruit fly Drosophila as a model system to investigate this question. They focused on two neuroblasts, NB5-6 and NB7-4, which give rise to different types of neurons.

     The researchers used a technique called Targeted DamID to identify the genomic binding sites of the temporal transcription factor Hunchback in the two neuroblasts. They found that Hunchback targets were different in each neuroblast, ruling out the possibility that spatial and temporal cues act independently to specify neuronal diversity. Instead, the researchers discovered that each neuroblast had distinct open chromatin domains, which correlated with differential Hunchback-bound loci in each neuroblast.

     Furthermore, the researchers found that the spatial factor Gsb/Pax3, which is expressed in NB5-6 but not NB7-4, had genomic binding sites correlated with open chromatin in NB5-6, but not NB7-4. This suggests that early-acting spatial factors like Gsb establish neuroblast-specific open chromatin domains, leading to neuroblast-specific temporal factor binding and the production of different neurons in each neuroblast lineage.

     The study identified over 100 loci that are differentially bound by Hunchback in NB5-6 or NB7-4, which are excellent candidates for lineage-specific neuronal specification. The findings support a model in which spatial and temporal cues are integrated in neural progenitors to generate lineage-specific neuronal diversity. This study provides new insights into the mechanisms of neurogenesis and has implications for our understanding of how neural diversity is generated in the nervous system.
Summary
     Biologists have long sought to understand how P2X receptors, a type of ion channel, open and close in response to the presence of ATP. These receptors play a crucial role in various physiological processes, including pain sensation and neurotransmission. Recent studies have provided insights into the molecular structure of P2X receptors, but the exact mechanism of pore opening remains unclear.

     To address this question, researchers used a novel approach called photo-switchable cross-linkers, or "molecular tweezers," to study the P2X2 receptor. These tweezers allowed the scientists to monitor the distance between specific amino acids in the receptor's transmembrane domain during activation. By combining this experimental data with computational simulations, the researchers were able to create high-resolution models of the channel in both open and closed states.

     The results showed that the outer pore of the receptor expands significantly less than previously thought, and that the inner and outer ends of adjacent pore-lining helices move closer together during activation. This movement is thought to occur through a "hinge-bending" motion, where the helices bend at a critical point, allowing the channel to open.

     These findings provide new insights into the gating mechanism of P2X receptors and offer a versatile strategy for studying other membrane proteins. The use of photo-switchable cross-linkers as molecular tweezers represents a powerful tool for understanding the complex dynamics of ion channels and other membrane proteins.

     The study also highlights the importance of considering the full-length, membrane-embedded structure of P2X receptors, rather than relying solely on X-ray crystal structures, which may not accurately represent the native, open-channel state. By combining experimental and computational approaches, the researchers were able to gain a more complete understanding of the P2X2 receptor's gating mechanism, shedding light on a long-standing question in the field of biophysics.
summary
     Biologists have long assumed that all animal cells divide using a similar molecular mechanism during cytokinesis, the physical division of one cell into two. However, recent evidence suggests that cytokinetic regulation in individual cell types has more variation than previously thought. A new study in the nematode worm Caenorhabditis elegans (C. elegans) reveals that cell-type-specific cytokinetic diversity is controlled by both cell-intrinsic and extrinsic mechanisms.

     In the four-cell C. elegans embryo, each blastomere has a distinct cell fate, specified by conserved pathways. The researchers used fast-acting temperature-sensitive mutants and acute drug treatment to identify cell-type-specific variation in the cytokinetic requirement for a robust forminCYK-1-dependent filamentous-actin (F-actin) cytoskeleton. They found that in one cell (P2), this cytokinetic variation is cell-intrinsically regulated, whereas in another cell (EMS), this variation is cell-extrinsically regulated, dependent on both SrcSRC-1 signaling and direct contact with its neighbor cell, P2.

     The study used thermogenetics, drug treatment, and embryo microdissection to probe the mechanisms of cell-type-based variation in the regulation of cytokinesis among individual blastomeres within the four-cell C. elegans embryo. The researchers found cell-type-specific differences in both the functional levels and temporal window of activity required for forminCYK-1 activity, but not myosin-IINMY-2 activity, during cytokinesis. They also found that inactivation of forminCYK-1 by thermal upshift to restrictive temperature resulted in cell-type-specific variation in the temporal requirement for activity among individual blastomeres.

     The study shows that both cell-intrinsic and extrinsic regulatory mechanisms contribute to cytokinetic diversity and promote robust cytokinesis when the contractile ring is weakened in specific cell types within the four-cell C. elegans embryo. The findings suggest that each cell in the four-cell C. elegans embryo has a unique cell identity and can be individually scored for contractile ring constriction during cytokinesis. The study provides new insights into the complex regulation of cytokinesis and highlights the importance of cell-type-specific mechanisms in ensuring proper cell division.

     Biomedical research has long recognized the importance of bacterial flagella in mediating host-microbe interactions, including tissue tropism during colonization and immune response activation. However, the flagellar shaft of certain bacteria, including human pathogens, is encased in a membranous sheath of unknown function. This sheath has been hypothesized to allow these bacteria to evade host responses to the immunogenic flagellin subunit, but its true purpose has remained unclear.

     A recent study has shed new light on the role of the flagellar sheath in the model symbiosis between the bacterium Vibrio fischeri and the Hawaiian bobtail squid. The researchers found that the rotation of the sheathed flagellum in both V. fischeri and its pathogenic congener Vibrio cholerae promotes the release of lipopolysaccharide (LPS), a potent bacteria-derived immunogen found in the flagellar sheath. This discovery reveals a new role for the flagellar sheath in triggering, rather than circumventing, host immune responses.

     The study used the model squid-vibrio symbiosis as a tool to probe the effect of the enigmatic flagellar sheath on host recognition. The researchers observed that the rotation of the sheathed flagellum in V. fischeri and V. cholerae leads to the release of LPS, which is recognized by the squid host. This finding has important implications for the study of bacterial pathogens with sheathed flagella and raises questions about the biophysical function of the flagellar sheath.

     The study's findings suggest that the flagellar sheath plays a previously unrecognized role in mediating the release of LPS, which is a key component of the bacterial secretome. The researchers propose that the rotation of the sheathed flagellum triggers the release of LPS, which in turn activates host immune responses. This new understanding of the flagellar sheath's function has significant implications for the study of bacterial virulence and the initiation of symbiotic relationships between bacteria and their hosts.

     Further research is needed to clarify the mechanisms underlying the signaling during the initiation of the squid-vibrio symbiosis. This includes characterizing the composition of LPS released by V. fischeri during flagellar rotation, identifying the squid receptors that recognize V. fischeri LPS, and determining any role flagellin may have in activating host responses. The study's findings have significant implications for our understanding of the complex interactions between bacteria and their hosts and highlight the importance of further research into the biophysical function of the flagellar sheath.
summary
     Biologists have discovered a previously unknown phenomenon in plants where chromosomes undergo catastrophic restructuring during a process called genome elimination. This process occurs when two plants with different versions of a protein called centromere histone H3 (CENH3) are crossed, resulting in the formation of shattered chromosomes from the genome of the haploid inducer. The researchers found that these shattered chromosomes are compartmentalized within a micronucleus and that breaks in the chromosomes are repaired through a process called non-homologous end joining (NHEJ) or stalled fork repair.

     The study, conducted in Arabidopsis plants, shows that the NHEJ pathway plays a crucial role in the repair of the haploid inducer chromosomes that contribute to diploid and aneuploid progeny. When the NHEJ pathway is inhibited, the frequency of haploid induction increases. The researchers also found that unrepaired double-stranded DNA breaks increase the elimination of the haploid inducer genome, similar to observations in mouse-human hybrid genome elimination.

     The study suggests that missegregated chromosomes enter a degradative pathway initiated by endonucleolytic breaks. Occasionally, such chromosomes are rescued, resulting in the formation of aneuploid and diploid progeny. The researchers propose that this phenomenon is a natural system for investigating the causes and mechanisms of complex genomic rearrangements similar to those associated with several human disorders.

     The findings provide evidence for the occurrence of chromosome restructuring when diverged individuals hybridize, identifying a centromere-based mechanism for genomic instability. This phenomenon depends on chimeric CENH3, but a similar effect was observed when the haploid inducer strain expresses CENH3 of a close species. The study's results have implications for our understanding of the consequences of mitotic malfunction on genome integrity and the potential for enduring genomic novelty.
Summary
     Epstein-Barr virus (EBV) is a common virus that can cause several types of cancer. To understand how EBV contributes to cancer development, researchers studied how the virus affects the survival of B cells, a type of immune cell. They found that EBV uses a unique mechanism to prevent B cells from dying, even in the early stages of infection.

     Normally, B cells rely on a protein called BCL-2 to survive. However, during EBV infection, the virus upregulates two other proteins, MCL-1 and BFL-1, which work together with BCL-2 to prevent apoptosis, or programmed cell death. This is a critical step in cancer development, as uncontrolled cell growth can lead to tumor formation.

     The researchers used a technique called BH3 profiling to study the regulation of apoptosis in EBV-infected cells. They found that the viral protein EBNA3A plays a key role in suppressing apoptosis by promoting the localization of MCL-1 to the mitochondria and increasing the transcription of BFL-1. This is a new and unique function of EBNA3A, and it is essential for the survival of EBV-infected B cells.

     In contrast, uninfected B cells rely solely on BCL-2 for survival, while EBV-infected cells use a combination of BCL-2, MCL-1, and BFL-1 to prevent apoptosis. The researchers also found that the viral oncoprotein LMP1, which is thought to be responsible for apoptotic suppression in latently infected cells, is not present during the initial burst of B cell proliferation induced by EBV.

     This study provides new insights into the mechanism of EBV-induced B cell survival and has implications for our understanding of lymphomagenesis, or the development of lymphomas, which are cancers of the immune system. The findings suggest that EBNA3A plays a critical role in the suppression of apoptosis during both early and late stages of EBV infection, and this may contribute to the development of EBV-associated cancers.

summary
     Biomedical research has traditionally viewed apoptosis, a process of programmed cell death, and its molecular mediators, caspases, as tumor suppressors. However, recent studies suggest that under certain conditions, apoptotic caspases can promote tumor growth and proliferation. A new study in Drosophila, a type of fruit fly, has uncovered a mechanism by which caspases can switch from tumor suppressors to tumor promoters.

     In the study, researchers used a well-studied neoplastic tumor model in Drosophila to investigate the role of caspases in tumor growth. They found that oncogenic mutations of the proto-oncogene Ras (RasV12) maintain tumorous cells in an "undead" state, where caspases no longer induce cell death but instead promote the generation of reactive oxygen species (ROS). ROS, in turn, recruit and activate macrophage-like immune cells, which signal back to tumorous epithelial cells to activate oncogenic JNK signaling.

     JNK signaling further amplifies caspase activity, creating a feedback amplification loop that is necessary for uncontrolled tumor growth and invasive behavior. The researchers demonstrated that interfering with this amplification loop significantly reduces the neoplastic behavior of these cells and improves organismal survival.

     The study also showed that oncogenic Ras suppresses the apoptotic activity of caspases, keeping tumorous cells in an "undead" state. While caspases may still induce apoptosis in a few tumor cells, they largely promote the generation of intra- and extracellular ROS, which are required for malignant growth and tissue invasion of surviving neoplastic cells.

     The findings of this study extend previous models of the conversion of Eiger and JNK signaling from anti-tumor to pro-tumor roles by oncogenic Ras and identify caspases as essential components of this switch. The study suggests that under certain conditions, such as the presence of oncogenic RasV12 in scrib mutant cells, apoptotic caspases can adopt a tumor-promoting role, challenging the traditional view of caspases as tumor suppressors.
Biomedical research has shown that the way we perceive time can be influenced by our social interactions. A recent study found that people tend to perceive social interactions as shorter in duration than non-social interactions. This effect, known as temporal compression, was found to be stronger in individuals with higher social cognitive competency. However, individuals with autistic-like tendencies showed a weaker temporal compression effect. The study also found that intranasal oxytocin administration restored the temporal compression effect in socially less proficient individuals, while the administration of atosiban, a competitive antagonist of oxytocin, diminished the effect in socially proficient individuals. These findings suggest that perceived time is not a faithful representation of physical time, but rather is influenced by an individual's personality traits and social cognition.

The study used a series of experiments to investigate the relationship between social interactions, temporal perception, and oxytocin. In one experiment, participants were shown videos of people engaging in social or non-social interactions and asked to estimate the duration of the interactions. The results showed that social interactions were perceived as shorter in duration than non-social interactions, and this effect was stronger in individuals with higher social cognitive competency. However, individuals with autistic-like tendencies showed a weaker temporal compression effect.

The study also found that oxytocin played a critical role in mediating the temporal compression effect. In socially less proficient individuals, intranasal oxytocin administration restored the temporal compression effect, while the administration of atosiban, a competitive antagonist of oxytocin, diminished the effect in socially proficient individuals. This suggests that oxytocin is involved in mediating time perception of social interaction, further supporting its role in human social cognition.

The study's findings have implications for our understanding of time perception and social cognition. They suggest that perceived time is not a fixed or objective measure, but rather is influenced by an individual's personality traits and social cognition. The study's results also highlight the importance of oxytocin in mediating social interactions and time perception, and suggest that oxytocin may be a potential therapeutic target for improving social cognition in individuals with autism spectrum disorder.

     The interaction between the SARS-CoV-2 virus Spike protein receptor binding domain (RBD) and the ACE2 cell surface protein is crucial for viral infection of cells.      Biologists have long understood that genes interact with each other to produce complex traits and modify the effects of mutations during development and disease. However, mapping these interactions has been a significant challenge. A team of researchers used a novel approach to create a comprehensive map of directional genetic interactions in Drosophila cells. They employed combinatorial RNA interference and automated single-cell phenotyping to study 21 phenotypic features of Drosophila cells. The resulting map comprises 1367 Drosophila genes involved in key biological processes such as signalling, chromatin biology, cell cycle regulation, and protein turnover control.

     The researchers developed a method that combines genetic interactions on multiple phenotypes to reveal directional relationships between genes. This approach allowed them to reconstruct the sequence of protein activities during mitosis and identify regulatory relationships between genes. Notably, the map revealed an interaction between the Ras pathway and the SWI/SNF chromatin-remodelling complex. This interaction was found to be conserved in human cancer cells.

     The study presents a powerful approach for reconstructing directional regulatory networks and provides a valuable resource for interpreting the functional consequences of genetic alterations. The researchers demonstrated that their method can be used to identify epistatic relationships between genes at a large scale and map these interactions to other genomic data sets. The resulting epistatic network revealed a detailed circuit diagram of regulatory, temporal, and causal relationships of complexes that function during the M-phase of the cell cycle.

     One of the key findings of the study is the unexpected link between chromatin remodelling and RAS signalling. The researchers found genetic interactions between the SWI/SNF complex and the SWI/SNF-associated chromatin remodeller dalao. This interaction was confirmed in vivo and highlights the importance of chromatin remodelling in regulating RAS signalling. The study provides new insights into the complex relationships between genes and offers a valuable resource for understanding the functional consequences of genetic alterations in development and disease.

     Biologists have long been interested in understanding the genetic basis of heterostyly, a common floral adaptation that promotes outbreeding in plants. In the genus Primula, also known as primroses, heterostyly is controlled by the S-locus supergene, which determines the reciprocal arrangement of reproductive organs and incompatibility between the two morphs. However, the identities of the component genes remained unknown until now.

     Researchers have identified the CYP734A50 gene as the G locus that determines the style-length dimorphism in Primula. This gene encodes a putative brassinosteroid-degrading enzyme and is only present on the short-styled S-morph haplotype. CYP734A50 is specifically expressed in S-morph styles and its loss or inactivation leads to long styles. The gene arose by a duplication specific to the Primulaceae lineage and shows an accelerated rate of molecular evolution.

     The study found that CYP734A50 is absent or inactivated in all seven cases of derived long homostyly, and its presence strictly cosegregates with the morph type in all heterostylous species sampled across the Primula phylogeny. This strongly supports the conclusion that CYP734A50 is the G locus of the Primula heterostyly supergene that determines the style-length polymorphism.

     The researchers propose that the duplication and gain of style-specific expression of CYP734A50 in the ancestor of the Primulaceae likely represents the dominant style-shortening mutation. This finding provides a mechanistic explanation for the Primula style-length dimorphism and sheds light on the evolution of the S-locus as a prime model for a complex plant supergene. The discovery of CYP734A50 as a causal locus in a supergene underlying an adaptive polymorphism opens the way for understanding the evolutionary origin of the Primula S-locus.

     Biomedical researchers have made a significant discovery about the role of kisspeptin neurons in the regulation of reproductive function. Kisspeptin neurons in the hypothalamic arcuate nucleus (Kiss1ARH) play a crucial role in pubertal development and fertility. These neurons co-express kisspeptin, neurokinin B (NKB), dynorphin, and glutamate, and are thought to provide an episodic, excitatory drive to gonadotropin-releasing hormone 1 (GnRH) neurons.

     Using optogenetics, whole-cell electrophysiology, molecular pharmacology, and single-cell RT-PCR in mice, the researchers characterized the cellular basis for synchronized Kiss1ARH neuronal activity. They found that high-frequency photostimulation of Kiss1ARH neurons evoked the local release of excitatory (NKB) and inhibitory (dynorphin) neuropeptides, which synchronized the firing of Kiss1ARH neurons. This synchronized activity caused robust excitation of GnRH neurons through a synaptic mechanism that also involved glutamatergic input to preoptic Kiss1 neurons from Kiss1ARH neurons.

     The researchers discovered that high-frequency photoactivation of Kiss1ARH neurons induced a NKB-mediated slow excitatory postsynaptic potential (EPSP), which was modulated by co-released dynorphin, and caused synchronization of Kiss1ARH neurons. Additionally, activation of Kiss1ARH neurons co-released glutamate that excited the rostral Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons. The combination of synchronization and unified excitation of Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons resulted in robust excitation of GnRH neurons.

     Kiss1ARH neurons also communicate with the Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons via the fast neurotransmitter glutamate, which stimulates burst-firing of Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons. Activation of these rostral Kiss1 neurons releases kisspeptin to robustly excite GnRH neurons via activation of the GPR54 signaling cascade, thereby stimulating the release of GnRH at the time of the preovulatory surge. The researchers propose that Kiss1ARH neurons play a dual role in driving episodic secretion of GnRH through the differential release of peptide and amino acid neurotransmitters to coordinate reproductive function.

     Biologists have long known that bone morphogenic protein (BMP) signalling plays a crucial role in maintaining the ability of cells to become any cell type in the body, a state known as pluripotency. However, the exact mechanisms by which BMP signalling prevents cells from differentiating into specific cell types, such as nerve cells or muscle cells, were not well understood.

     Researchers have now discovered that BMP signalling suppresses differentiation by maintaining the expression of a protein called E-Cadherin (Cdh1). E-Cadherin is a molecule that helps cells stick together and is involved in the earliest stages of neural and mesodermal differentiation. When E-Cadherin is lost, cells are more likely to differentiate into specific cell types.

     The study found that suppressing E-Cadherin activity is sufficient to overcome the block to differentiation imposed by BMP signalling. This suggests that E-Cadherin plays a key role in maintaining pluripotency and preventing cells from differentiating into specific cell types.

     BMP signalling also helps to prime pluripotent cells for mesodermal differentiation, which is the process by which cells become muscle, bone, or blood cells. This is in contrast to neural differentiation, which is the process by which cells become nerve cells. The study found that BMP signalling acts independently of E-Cadherin to prime cells for mesodermal differentiation.

     The researchers propose that BMP signalling feeds into a control mechanism that regulates the receptivity of cells to differentiation cues. This means that BMP signalling helps to determine whether cells are receptive to signals that promote differentiation into specific cell types.

     The study provides new insights into the mechanisms by which BMP signalling maintains pluripotency and promotes mesodermal differentiation. It also highlights the importance of E-Cadherin in regulating the earliest stages of neural and mesodermal differentiation. The findings have implications for our understanding of how cells develop and differentiate into specific cell types during embryonic development.

     Biologists have long known that the two centrioles of the centrosome in quiescent cells are inherently asymmetric structures that differ in age, morphology, and function. However, the mechanisms that establish and maintain these asymmetric properties during quiescence have remained unknown. A recent study has shed light on this process by identifying a critical role for a protein called Cep120 in regulating centriole maturation in quiescent cells.

     Cep120 is a protein associated with the daughter centriole, and its depletion in quiescent mouse and human cells leads to the accumulation of pericentriolar material (PCM) components, including pericentrin, Cdk5Rap2, ninein, and Cep170. This accumulation results in increased microtubule-nucleation activity at the centrosome, leading to aberrant dynein-dependent trafficking of centrosomal proteins, dispersal of centriolar satellites, and defective ciliary assembly and signaling.

     The study found that Cep120 plays an inhibitory role at the centrosome by regulating PCM levels during quiescence. Loss of Cep120 initiates a series of changes in protein localization and abundance that ultimately result in defective ciliary assembly and function. The researchers used immuno-electron microscopy to show that Cep120 resides on the outer wall of the centriolar microtubules, suggesting that it regulates the asymmetric distribution of PCM proteins at the centrosome.

     The study's findings indicate that Cep120 helps to maintain centrosome homeostasis by inhibiting the untimely maturation of the daughter centriole. This process is critical for the proper assembly and function of cilia, which are essential for various cellular processes, including signaling and sensing the environment. The study's results also suggest that loss of Cep120 may contribute to the pathogenesis of ciliopathies, such as Jeune Asphyxiating Thoracic Dystrophy and Joubert syndrome.

     The researchers propose that the dispersal of centriolar satellites upon Cep120 loss disrupts the formation and/or function of cilia during quiescence. This disruption is likely due to changes in PCM abundance, microtubule organization, and dynein-dependent trafficking of centrosomal proteins. The study's findings highlight the importance of centrosome homeostasis and its relationship to primary cilium assembly and signaling, and provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying ciliopathies.

     Biomedical research has shown that neuron-glia interactions play a crucial role in the maturation of neural circuits in the developing central nervous system (CNS). However, the pathways that mediate communication between neurons and glial cells, such as MÃ¼ller glial cells (MCs), in the developing retina are not well understood. A recent study investigated neuron-glia signaling in the developing retina and found that retinal waves induce calcium transients in MCs.

The study used a combination of whole-cell recordings and two-photon imaging to investigate the interaction between retinal ganglion cells (RGCs) and MCs. The results showed that retinal waves were correlated with calcium transients in a large proportion of MCs, indicating that spontaneous activity encompasses both neuronal and glial networks in the developing retina.

The study also found that the mechanism of interaction between neurons and MCs during cholinergic retinal waves is similar to previous findings in other brain regions, such as the visual cortex and hippocampus. However, the proportion of MCs that responded to waves with calcium transients decreased significantly from postnatal day 9 (P9) to P11, despite the presence of retinal waves.

The researchers used an optical glutamate sensor to show that the decline in MC calcium transients is caused by a reduction in the amount of glutamate reaching MCs. This suggests that neurons and MCs exhibit correlated activity during a critical period of retinal maturation that is enabled by neurotransmitter spillover from retinal synapses.

The study also found that MCs express multiple neurotransmitter receptor types throughout this period, allowing neuronal control of MC activity to be sustained as neuronal synchronization shifts from being dependent on cholinergic to glutamatergic signaling. This suggests that the responsiveness of MCs changes to match the neurotransmitter used to support retinal waves.

Overall, the study provides new insights into the mechanisms of neuron-glia signaling in the developing retina and highlights the importance of neurotransmitter spillover in enabling correlated activity between neurons and MCs during a critical period of retinal maturation. The findings have implications for our understanding of the development and function of neural circuits in the CNS.
Summary
     Estimating the time since death, or postmortem interval (PMI), is crucial in death investigations. However, current methods, such as forensic entomology, can be prone to errors and biases. Researchers have explored the potential of microbes to provide a more accurate estimate of PMI. A recent study used a mouse model system to investigate the feasibility of using microbial communities to estimate PMI.

     In the study, 40 mouse corpses were decomposed on soil under controlled conditions for 48 days. The researchers characterized the changes in microbial communities at three external sites and one internal site using DNA sequencing. The results showed that the microbial communities underwent dramatic, measurable, and repeatable changes over time. These changes allowed the researchers to estimate PMI with an accuracy of approximately 3 days over the 48-day period.

     The study provides a detailed understanding of the microbial ecology of corpse decomposition and suggests that microbial community data can be developed into a forensic tool for estimating PMI. This approach has the potential to complement existing methods and provide a more accurate estimate of the time since death. The researchers rigorously tested the predictability of microbial community changes over the decomposition timeline, which is essential for using microbes as a "clock" to estimate PMI.

     The study builds upon previous research by Pechal et al. (2013), which demonstrated consistent changes in skin and mouth bacterial communities during decomposition of swine corpses over 5 days. The current study expands on this work by characterizing microbial community change in multiple sites and providing a proof-of-principle demonstration of the potential of microbial community ecology to be developed into a complementary forensic tool for estimating PMI. The results of this study provide a foundation for further research into the use of microbial communities in forensic science.
Despite the vast diversity of body forms in nature, bilaterian animals share common sets of developmental genes that display conserved expression patterns in the embryo. Among these genes are the Hox genes, which define different identities along the anterior-posterior axis. Hox proteins exert their function by interacting with TALE transcription factors. However, the presence of Hox and TALE members in some but not all non-bilaterian phyla raises the question of how Hox-TALE interactions evolved to provide positional information.

     Researchers used proteins from unicellular and multicellular lineages to show that Hox-TALE networks emerged from an ancestral generic motif present in Hox and other related protein families. The study found that Hox-TALE networks experienced additional and extensive molecular innovations that were likely crucial for differentiating Hox functions along body plans. The results highlight how homeobox gene families evolved during eukaryote evolution to eventually constitute a major patterning system in Eumetazoans.

     The study proposes that Hox-TALE networks constituted an ancestral regulatory module that was later exploited for patterning functions in Bilateria. This network was effective as soon as different interaction modes could exist with duplicated Hox family members, allowing diversifying patterning functions along the body axis. The original molecular system was subsequently co-opted by the various contexts of embryogenesis in different eumetazoan phyla for axis or tissue patterning.

     Hox genes are expressed along the anteroposterior axis of all bilaterian animals, providing positional information during embryogenesis. Given their important patterning roles, Hox genes are thought to have strongly contributed to morphological diversification of bilaterian organisms during evolution. Although these networks rely on intricate molecular properties, they originated from an ancestral generic mode of interaction that was kept in other homeobox gene families.

     The study found that developmental genes can display highly dissimilar expression patterns or be absent in non-bilaterian lineages, showing that the genetic mechanisms underlying embryonic development are not universal. Interestingly, Cnidaria is the only non-bilaterian phylum with a bona fide Hox repertoire, whereas others lack Hox genes. This raises the question of how the Hox gene family acquired its crucial axial patterning functions during metazoan evolution. In Bilateria, Hox patterning functions rely on the presence of the PBC and Meis proteins, which are also present in non-bilaterian phyla.
Biomedical researchers used super-resolution microscopy to study the behavior of AMPA receptors (AMPARs) in synapses. AMPARs are proteins that play a crucial role in transmitting signals between neurons. The study aimed to understand how the size of fluorescent probes attached to AMPARs affects their movement and localization within and outside of post-synaptic densities (PSDs).

The researchers used different-sized fluorescent probes, including organic dyes, small quantum dots (sQDs), and large quantum dots (bQDs), to label AMPARs in rat neurons. They found that the majority of AMPARs labeled with sQDs or organic dyes were confined to nanodomains within PSDs, which are stable for at least 15 minutes. In contrast, most AMPARs labeled with bQDs were found outside of PSDs and were highly mobile.

The study suggests that AMPARs can rapidly enter stable nanodomains within PSDs, rather than accumulating in extrasynaptic membranes. This finding contradicts the hypothesis that AMPAR entry is limited by the occupancy of open PSD "slots." The researchers also found that the size of the fluorescent probe affects the behavior of AMPARs, with smaller probes (sQDs and organic dyes) allowing for more accurate tracking of AMPAR dynamics.

Using super-resolution microscopy, the researchers were able to observe the dynamics of AMPARs in synapses for extended periods, up to 60 minutes. They found that the localization of AMPARs is predominantly at synaptic regions, with typically one nanodomain per synapse. The study highlights the importance of using smaller fluorescent probes to accurately track the behavior of AMPARs and other neurotransmitter receptors in synapses.

The findings of this study have implications for our understanding of synaptic plasticity and neurotransmission. AMPARs play a critical role in learning and memory, and understanding their behavior and regulation is essential for developing new treatments for neurological disorders. The use of super-resolution microscopy and smaller fluorescent probes has enabled researchers to gain new insights into the behavior of AMPARs and their role in synaptic function.

     Biomedical research has long sought to understand the earliest response of central neurons to hypoxia, a condition characterized by insufficient oxygen supply to the brain. A recent study has shed light on this process, revealing that the Small Ubiquitin-like Modifier (SUMO) pathway plays a crucial role in the initial response of central neurons to acute hypoxia. Specifically, the study found that hypoxia activates the SUMO pathway in rat cerebellar granule neurons (CGN), leading to the SUMOylation of NaV1.2 channels, an increase in voltage-gated sodium current (INa), and a shift in the voltage-dependence of channel activation.

     The study demonstrated that the time-course for SUMOylation of single NaV1.2 channels at the cell surface and changes in INa coincide, and both are prevented by mutation of NaV1.2-Lys38 or application of a deSUMOylating enzyme. Within 40 seconds, hypoxia-induced linkage of SUMO1 to the channels is complete, resulting in larger sodium currents in response to depolarizing steps. The study also found that the SUMO pathway and NaV1.2 channels are potential targets for neuroprotective interventions, given the recognized role of INa in hypoxic brain damage.

     The research further showed that hypoxia-mediated SUMOylation of NaV1.2 is observed in the presence of tetrodotoxin (TTX), a potent blocker of sodium channels, indicating that Na+ flux is not a prerequisite for channel SUMOylation. Additionally, the study found that the SUMO pathway tonically controls INa under normoxic conditions, with SUMO1 and SENP1 increasing and decreasing the current, respectively. The response of INa to hypoxia was ablated by Âµ-Conotoxin-TIIIA (CnTX), a potent blocker of NaV1.2 channels, supporting the implied mechanism of rapid SUMOylation of NaV1.2 channels at the CGN plasma membrane in response to hypoxia.

     The study's findings suggest that NaV1.2 channels and SUMO pathway enzymes may be valuable therapeutic targets for reducing damage due to acute hypoxic insults to central neurons. The research has significant implications for the development of neuroprotective interventions aimed at mitigating the effects of hypoxia on the brain.
Biomedical researchers have developed a model to predict the transmission of Ross River virus (RRV), a mosquito-borne pathogen found in Australia, the Pacific Islands, and potentially emerging worldwide. The model shows that RRV transmission peaks at moderate temperatures of 26.4Â°C and declines to zero at temperatures of 17.0Â°C and 31.5Â°C. This means that RRV transmission is likely to be year-round in tropical areas, but seasonal in temperate regions, resulting in a nationwide seasonal peak in human cases.

The study's findings suggest that climate warming will likely increase RRV transmission in temperate areas, where most Australians live, but decrease transmission in tropical areas where mean temperatures are already near the thermal optimum. The researchers used a mechanistic model based on empirical, unimodal thermal responses to parameterize the temperature-dependent transmission of RRV. They used data from two important vector species, Cx. annulirostris and Ae. vigilax, to develop the model.

The study's results support a general pattern of intermediate thermal optima for transmission, where the optimal temperature is driven by adult mosquito lifespan. However, the upper and lower thermal limits of transmission are more uncertain and may be determined by unique traits for different vector-pathogen systems. The researchers found that the strongest statistical signal of temperature on disease is expected in temperate regions, where mean temperature varies along the rapidly rising portion of the R0 curve.

The study highlights the importance of nonlinear models for inferring the role of temperature in disease dynamics and predicting responses to climate change. The researchers note that thermal responses vary substantially among diseases and vector species, and that identifying transmission optima and limits by characterizing nonlinear thermal responses can more accurately predict geographic, seasonal, and interannual variation in disease. The study's findings have implications for understanding the effects of climate change on mosquito-borne disease transmission and for predicting the emergence and spread of new mosquito-borne pathogens.
