     Biomedical researchers have long been interested in understanding the role of beta band oscillations in the motor cortex, a region of the brain involved in movement planning and execution. These oscillations, which occur at a frequency of 13-30 Hz, have been linked to changes in underlying neural activity, but the specific behavioral manifestations of these changes have remained unclear.

     To investigate how changes in population neural activity, beta oscillations, and behavior are linked, researchers recorded multi-scale neural activity from the motor cortex of three macaques while they performed a novel neurofeedback task. The task required the animals to volitionally bring their beta oscillatory power to an instructed state and then execute an arm reach.

     The results showed that reaches preceded by a reduction in beta power exhibited significantly faster movement onset times than reaches preceded by an increase in beta power. Furthermore, population neural activity was found to shift farther from a movement onset state during beta oscillations that were neurofeedback-induced or naturally occurring during reaching tasks. This finding establishes a population neural basis for slowed movement onset following periods of beta oscillatory activity.

     Notably, this shift in neural population was also seen in naturally occurring beta oscillations during reaching tasks, suggesting that beta oscillations reflect a common signature of spiking patterns even in different task contexts. This study ties together existing works on behavioral correlates of beta oscillations with hypotheses of how the motor cortex encodes movement onset through the lens of population-level neural activity.

     The results of this study support the hypothesis that beta oscillations are linked to neural patterns that slow the onset of new movements. This is consistent with previous findings of elevated beta power prior to and after well-trained movements. However, individual unit responses during beta activity were found to be different across tasks, suggesting that beta oscillations may be generated by different mechanisms in different contexts.

     Despite these differences, population-level analyses showed that beta oscillations promote a consistent movement-slowing state during both neurofeedback-induced and naturally occurring beta oscillations. This finding has implications for our understanding of how the motor cortex encodes movement onset and suggests that beta oscillations play a key role in slowing the initiation of new movements.

Biomedical researchers have long understood that synaptic vesicles (SVs) play a crucial role in transmitting signals between neurons. After releasing neurotransmitters, SVs are rapidly recycled through a process called endocytosis, where their membranes are retrieved and reused to form new SVs. A key aspect of this process is the formation of large endocytic vacuoles, or "bulk" endosomes, which are thought to be involved in the recycling of SV membranes.

Recent studies have suggested that clathrin-mediated endocytosis (CME) and dynamin 1 and 3, proteins involved in the formation of clathrin-coated vesicles, are essential for this process. However, a new study has challenged this understanding by investigating the role of CME and dynamin 1 and 3 in the formation of new SVs from bulk endosomes. The researchers used neurons that lacked dynamin 1 and 3, making them unable to form clathrin-coated vesicles, and found that the formation of bulk endosomes was not impaired.

In fact, the absence of dynamin 1 and 3 led to an increase in the formation of bulk endosomes. Furthermore, the conversion of these endosomes into new SVs occurred independently of clathrin, as evidenced by the lack of clathrin-coated buds on the surface of the endosomes and the continued conversion of endosomes into SVs even after clathrin levels were depleted. These findings suggest that there is an alternative pathway for SV reformation that bypasses the need for clathrin and dynamin 1 and 3.

This study provides new insights into the complex process of synaptic vesicle recycling and suggests that bulk endosomes play a more significant role in this process than previously thought. The discovery of a dynamin 1-, dynamin 3-, and clathrin-independent pathway for SV reformation has important implications for our understanding of synaptic function and plasticity, particularly during intense synaptic activity. Further research is needed to fully elucidate the mechanisms underlying this process and its role in neurological disorders.
Biomedical researchers have long sought a reliable biomarker to measure the effects of glucocorticoids, a class of commonly prescribed medications. Glucocorticoids play a crucial role in regulating various bodily functions, including metabolism, immune response, and stress response. However, there is currently no established biomarker to quantify their action in the body. To address this gap, a recent study aimed to identify and validate circulating biomarkers of glucocorticoid action in humans.

The study involved 10 participants with primary adrenal insufficiency, a rare disorder characterized by absent or very low endogenous glucocorticoid production. The participants were admitted to an in-patient clinic and underwent a randomized, crossover trial, where they received physiological glucocorticoid exposure and withdrawal. The researchers collected and analyzed various biological samples, including peripheral blood mononuclear cells, adipose tissue, plasma microRNAs, and serum metabolites.

Using integrated multi-omic analysis, the researchers identified a transcriptomic profile derived from both peripheral blood mononuclear cells and adipose tissue that was predictive of glucocorticoid exposure. They also identified a microRNA, miR-122-5p, which was correlated with genes and metabolites regulated by glucocorticoid exposure. Notably, this microRNA was replicated in independent studies with varying glucocorticoid exposure, suggesting its potential as a reliable biomarker.

The study's findings have significant implications for the optimization of glucocorticoid therapy and the understanding of disease etiology related to unphysiological glucocorticoid exposure, such as cardiovascular disease and obesity. The identification of miR-122-5p as a putative biomarker of glucocorticoid action opens up new avenues for research into the effects of glucocorticoids on human health and disease. Further studies are needed to validate the use of miR-122-5p as a biomarker and to explore its potential applications in clinical practice.
Biologists have long understood that amino acids play a crucial role in activating the mTORC1 complex, a key regulator of cell growth and metabolism. Recent research has shed light on the dynamics of this process, revealing a complex interplay between amino acids, lysosomes, and the mTORC1 complex.

The study found that amino acids trigger the translocation of mTORC1 to lysosomes, a process that is essential for its activation. Using live imaging techniques, researchers observed that this translocation occurs rapidly, within 2 minutes of amino acid addition, and peaks within 5 minutes. This process is accompanied by the appearance of fluorescent leucine in lysosomes, suggesting that amino acids are being sensed and processed within these organelles.

The study also identified key regulators of mTORC1 activity, including Sestrin2 and the vacuolar ATPase. Sestrin2 acts as a negative regulator, while the vacuolar ATPase is a positive regulator of mTORC1 activity. Notably, the phosphorylation of mTORC1 targets is delayed compared to lysosomal translocation, indicating that mTORC1 undergoes a dynamic and transient passage from the lysosomal surface before targeting its substrates elsewhere.

The researchers used a cell-permeant fluorescent analogue of di-leucine methyl ester to image RAPTOR, a component of the mTORC1 complex, in live cells. This allowed them to visualize the dynamics of mTORC1 activation in response to amino acids. The results showed that the lysosome is a crucial locus for mTOR activation, but the process is more complex than previously thought.

The study provides new insights into the mechanisms of mTORC1 activation, highlighting the importance of lysosomes in sensing amino acids and regulating cell growth and metabolism. The findings also suggest that multiple types of amino acid sensing may be involved in mTORC1 activation, including the sensing of leucine by Sestrin1/2 proteins and the transport of arginine into lysosomes via the SLC38A9 transporter. Overall, this research advances our understanding of the complex interplay between amino acids, lysosomes, and the mTORC1 complex.

     Biomedical research has shown that the glycosylation profile of antigens plays a crucial role in determining how they are processed and presented to the immune system. A recent study has demonstrated that modifying a model antigen, Ovalbumin (OVA), with a specific glycan structure, LewisX (LeX), alters its intracellular routing in dendritic cells and enhances the priming of T cells.

     The researchers found that the LeX modification of OVA directs it towards the C-type lectin receptor MGL1, which is involved in the recognition of glycan structures. This re-routing of OVA leads to an enhanced cross-presentation of the antigen to CD8+ T cells, a type of immune cell that plays a key role in cell-mediated immunity. The study also showed that the LeX modification of OVA favors the skewing of CD4+ T cells towards a Th1 response, which is characterized by the production of interferon-gamma (IFN-Î³) and tumor necrosis factor-alpha (TNF-Î±).

     Importantly, the researchers found that the LeX modification of OVA reduces the amount of antigen required for cross-presentation by 100-fold and eliminates the need for Toll-like receptor (TLR) signaling, which is typically required for the activation of dendritic cells. The study also showed that the cross-presentation of OVA-LeX is independent of TAP-transporters and Cathepsin-S, which are typically involved in the processing and presentation of antigens to CD8+ T cells.

     The researchers used MGL1-deficient mice to confirm that MGL1 is the primary lectin involved in the cross-presentation of OVA-LeX. They found that mice lacking MGL1 expression on dendritic cells did not show enhanced frequencies of OVA-specific effector T cells after immunization with LeX-conjugated OVA.

     Overall, the study demonstrates that controlled neo-glycosylation of antigens can influence their intracellular routing, the nature, and strength of immune responses. The findings suggest that modifying antigens with specific glycan structures, such as LeX, may be a useful strategy for optimizing current vaccination strategies and enhancing the efficacy of immune responses.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have discovered a novel mechanism by which certain bacteria, Burkholderia thailandensis, control the growth of mutants that do not respond to quorum sensing signals. Quorum sensing is a process by which bacteria communicate with each other through chemical signals to coordinate their behavior. In B. thailandensis, quorum sensing regulates hundreds of genes, including those involved in the type VI secretion system (T6SS). The T6SS is a complex system that allows bacteria to kill or inhibit the growth of other bacteria through the secretion of toxins and the production of immunity proteins.

     The researchers found that the T6SS is activated by quorum sensing in B. thailandensis and is essential for controlling the growth of mutants that do not respond to quorum sensing signals. These mutants, which lack the BtaR1 quorum-sensing signal receptor, have a significant growth advantage over wild-type cells in liquid broth cultures. However, when grown on agar surfaces, where cell-cell contact is possible, the wild-type cells are able to outcompete the mutants due to the T6SS-mediated growth inhibition.

     The researchers propose that quorum sensing activates the T6SS in wild-type cells, allowing them to produce toxins and immunity proteins that inhibit the growth of quorum-sensing mutants. This mechanism serves as a form of "microbial policing," where quorum-sensing competent bacteria are able to control the growth of mutants that do not respond to quorum sensing signals.

     This study provides new insights into the role of T6SS in controlling the growth of quorum-sensing mutants and highlights the importance of quorum sensing in regulating bacterial behavior. The findings have implications for our understanding of how bacteria interact with each other and their environment, and may have potential applications in the development of new strategies for controlling bacterial populations. The study also suggests that T6SS-mediated killing is not limited to interspecies competition, but can also be used to control the growth of mutants within the same species.

     The researchers used a combination of genetic and biochemical approaches to investigate the role of T6SS in B. thailandensis. They found that the T6SS is essential for controlling the growth of quorum-sensing mutants and that quorum sensing activates the T6SS in wild-type cells. The study provides evidence for a novel mechanism of microbial policing, where quorum-sensing competent bacteria are able to control the growth of mutants that do not respond to quorum sensing signals. The findings have important implications for our understanding of bacterial behavior and may have potential applications in the development of new strategies for controlling bacterial populations.
summary
     Biomedical research has shown that the connection between the prefrontal cortex and hippocampus plays a crucial role in cognitive performance, and this connection is established early in life. However, the underlying cellular mechanisms of this connection are not yet fully understood. A recent study aimed to elucidate the contribution of glutamatergic projection neurons in the hippocampus to the emergence of prefrontal oscillations in neonatal mice.

     The study used optogenetic manipulation to selectively target glutamatergic projection neurons in the CA1 area of either the dorsal or intermediate/ventral hippocampus in neonatal mice. The results showed that the prefrontal cortex is mainly coupled with the intermediate/ventral hippocampus by phase-locking of neuronal firing via dense direct axonal projections. Theta band-confined activation by light of pyramidal neurons in the intermediate/ventral but not dorsal CA1 that were transfected with high-efficiency channelrhodopsin boosted prefrontal oscillations.

     The study found that theta frequency-specific activation of pyramidal neurons in the CA1 area of the intermediate and ventral hippocampus, but not the dorsal hippocampus, elicited broad band oscillations in the prefrontal cortex of neonatal mice via dense axonal projections. The tight coupling by synchrony and the directed information flow from the hippocampal CA1 area to the prefrontal cortex via direct axonal projections suggest that the hippocampus acts as a drive for prefrontal activation already at neonatal age.

     The study also identified differences in communication between the dorsal and intermediate/ventral hippocampus and the prefrontal cortex, arguing for a prominent augmentation of driving force along the septo-temporal hippocampal axis. The findings provide causal evidence that theta frequency-specific activation of pyramidal neurons in the intermediate and ventral hippocampus drives the oscillatory entrainment of local circuits in the prefrontal cortex.

     The study's optogenetic protocol represents a new tool for assessing the adult behavioral readout of long-range communication in the developing brain. Further research is needed to understand how the hippocampal theta drive shapes the maturation of prefrontal-hippocampal networks. The study's findings have implications for our understanding of the development of cognitive functions and the underlying neural mechanisms.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have identified a new type of post-translational modification on the largest subunit of RNA polymerase II (RNAPII), a crucial enzyme responsible for transcribing genetic information into RNA. This modification involves the methylation of lysine residues on the C-terminal domain (CTD) of RNAPII, specifically at position 7 (K7). The study found that mono- and di-methylation of K7 residues (K7me1 and K7me2) occur during the earliest stages of transcription, preceding or accompanying the phosphorylation of serine residues at positions 5 and 7 (S5p and S7p). In contrast, acetylation of K7 residues (K7ac) is associated with productive elongation, serine-2 phosphorylation, and mRNA expression.

     The researchers developed new antibodies to detect K7me1 and K7me2 and used biochemical and genome-wide approaches to show that these modifications are present at the promoters of genes that are productively transcribed into mature RNA. The study also found that mutation of CTD-K7 residues is compatible with the viability of mouse fibroblasts, suggesting that global serine phosphorylation and RNAPII localization are independent of the presence or absence of K7 residues.

     The results indicate that the extent of productive transcription at active genes with similar RNAPII recruitment to their promoters is modulated at the level of CTD-K7 modifications. Methylation of K7 residues is associated with less productive transcription, while K7 acetylation is most enriched at the highest expressed genes. This balance between methylation and acetylation of K7 residues fine-tunes gene expression levels. The study provides new insights into the regulation of transcription and the role of post-translational modifications in modulating gene expression.
summary
     Biomedical research has shed light on the mechanisms governing the quiescence of skeletal muscle satellite cells (SCs). These cells play a crucial role in muscle tissue repair and regeneration. In their quiescent state, SCs remain dormant until muscle injury triggers their activation, proliferation, and self-renewal to replenish the SC population. To understand how SCs maintain quiescence, researchers identified differentially expressed gene transcripts following muscle injury. Among these, the mRNA decay factor Tristetraprolin (TTP) was found to be significantly changed.

     TTP promotes the decay of MyoD mRNA, a transcriptional regulator of myogenic commitment. Upon SC activation, the p38Î±/Î² MAPK signaling pathway phosphorylates and inactivates TTP, stabilizing MyoD mRNA. This allows MyoD to accumulate and drive SCs to commit to myoblasts. Satellite cell-specific knockdown of TTP precociously activates SCs in vivo, leading to MyoD accumulation, differentiation, and cell fusion into myofibers.

     The study suggests that TTP regulates mRNAs as one of several critical post-transcriptional mechanisms controlling SC homeostasis. MyoD up-regulates its own transcription, creating a rapid feed-forward loop that drives SCs to commit to myoblasts upon muscle injury. TTP may also be involved in down-regulating MyoD mRNA and other TTP-target transcripts during the initial formation of a quiescent SC population or upon reacquisition of quiescence following an injury.

     The research proposes that TTP suppresses transcripts for SC activation as a mechanism for immediate exit from quiescence. Upon muscle injury, p38Î±/Î² MAPK is rapidly activated, resulting in the phosphorylation and inactivation of TTP. This inactivation allows MyoD mRNA to accumulate, driving SCs to commit to the myogenic lineage. The study highlights the importance of post-transcriptional regulation of RNA in skeletal muscle homeostasis and the role of TTP in maintaining SC quiescence.
summary
     Infants with defects in the interleukin 10 receptor (IL10R) are prone to developing very early onset inflammatory bowel disease. Researchers investigated whether IL10R regulates lamina propria macrophage function during infant development in mice and whether macrophage-intrinsic IL10R signaling is required to prevent colitis in infancy. The study found that signs of colitis were absent in IL10R-deficient mice during the first two weeks of life, but intestinal inflammation and macrophage dysfunction began during the third week of life, concomitant with weaning and changes in the intestinal microbiota. Macrophage depletion with clodronate inhibited the development of colitis, while the absence of IL10R specifically on macrophages sensitized infant mice to the development of colitis. These results indicate that IL10R-mediated regulation of macrophage function during the early postnatal period is crucial for preventing the development of murine colitis.

The study used detailed time-series experiments to evaluate histology, inflammatory gene expression, cellular composition of the lamina propria, and luminal bacterial communities from birth to 14 weeks of age in IL10R-deficient infant mice and their littermate controls. The results suggest that IL10R does not directly regulate the microbial ecology during infant development. Instead, the study found that macrophage-intrinsic IL10R signaling is required to prevent colitis in infancy. The requirement for macrophage-intrinsic IL10R signaling develops during a critical developmental transition, which has important implications for understanding factors that initiate colitis in infants with genetic deficiencies in IL10R signaling.

Previous studies have shown that the IL10/IL10R pathway is essential for intestinal mucosal homeostasis and prevention of intestinal inflammation in infancy. Modeling infantile-onset colitis in mice that lack functional IL10R signaling could lead to significant advances in our understanding of the etiopathogenesis of these diseases. The findings of this study highlight the importance of IL10R-mediated regulation of macrophage function during the early postnatal period in preventing the development of murine colitis.
summary
     Biologists have identified a protein called SIMR-1 in the nematode worm Caenorhabditis elegans that plays a crucial role in the regulation of transposons and other germline genes. Transposons are mobile genetic elements that can jump from one location to another in the genome, and their regulation is essential for maintaining genome stability. SIMR-1 is a Tudor domain protein that acts downstream of piRNA production and upstream of the mutator complex, which is responsible for synthesizing secondary siRNAs from piRNA-targeted mRNAs.

The piRNA pathway is a complex process that involves the recognition of target mRNAs by piRNAs, followed by the amplification of siRNAs by the mutator complex. However, the steps between mRNA recognition by the piRNA pathway and siRNA amplification by the mutator complex were previously unknown. The researchers found that SIMR-1 localizes to distinct subcellular foci adjacent to P granules and Mutator foci, which are phase-separated condensates that are the sites of piRNA-dependent mRNA recognition and mutator complex-dependent siRNA amplification, respectively.

SIMR-1 may function similarly to Krimper, a protein that coordinates Ago3 and Aubergine during ping-pong piRNA biogenesis in Drosophila. The researchers suggest that SIMR-1 bridges the gap between the primary and secondary phases of the C. elegans piRNA silencing pathway. They also found that SIMR-1 localizes to cytoplasmic foci near P granules, Z granules, and Mutator foci, implicating a series of distinct perinuclear condensates in the regulation of mRNAs by the piRNA pathway and mutator complex.

The study identifies SIMR-1 as a factor that acts downstream of PRG-1 to mediate the production of secondary siRNAs by the mutator complex. This suggests a role for multiple perinuclear condensates in promoting mRNA regulation by the piRNA pathway and mutator complex. The researchers propose that SIMR-1 may interact with other proteins, such as RDE-12, to shuttle piRNA-targeted mRNAs into SIMR-1 foci and ultimately to Mutator foci. Overall, the study provides new insights into the regulation of transposons and other germline genes in C. elegans and highlights the importance of perinuclear condensates in the piRNA pathway.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have long been interested in understanding the behavior of Purkinje neurons, which play a crucial role in cerebellar function. These neurons have been shown to exhibit membrane bistability in vitro and in vivo under anesthesia, but the existence of bistability in awake animals has been disputed. To investigate this, a team of scientists recorded intracellularly from Purkinje neurons in unanesthetized larval zebrafish (Danio rerio). The results unequivocally demonstrate bistability in these neurons, with tonic firing observed in depolarized regimes and bursting at hyperpolarized membrane potentials.

     The researchers found that Purkinje neurons could switch between states spontaneously or with current injection, and that activation of AMPA receptors by climbing fibers (CFs) was sufficient to trigger bursts. Furthermore, the initiation of motor neuron spiking was correlated with increased incidence of CF excitatory postsynaptic potentials (EPSPs) and membrane depolarization. Developmentally, bistability was observed soon after Purkinje neuron specification and persisted at least until late larval stages.

     The study also showed that two types of glutamatergic excitation are observed in Purkinje neurons: strong excitation from CFs and weak excitation from parallel fibers (PFs), both mediated by AMPA receptors. Bistability in Purkinje neurons has been reported in vitro in amniotes and in vivo under anesthesia in rodents, but this is the first time it has been observed in vivo in an awake animal.

     The researchers recorded from Purkinje neurons of larval zebrafish from 4 days post-fertilization (dpf) to 19 dpf, allowing them to study intracellular membrane potential dynamics in the context of fictive motor output. While it is possible that the bistability observed is a property of a developing network and may not be present in the mature cerebellum of the adult, the conservation of other structural and functional properties of the cerebellum suggests that bistability may be a conserved feature of Purkinje neurons from fish to mammals.

     Overall, the study provides new insights into the behavior of Purkinje neurons in vivo and highlights the importance of AMPA receptor-mediated CF inputs in driving state-dependent bursting in these neurons. The findings have implications for our understanding of cerebellar function and motor control, and suggest that bistability may be a fundamental property of Purkinje neurons across species.
Biologists have long understood that the actin cytoskeleton plays a crucial role in the process of clathrin-mediated endocytosis, where cells internalize molecules and particles from their surroundings. A recent study has provided new insights into how the actin cytoskeleton self-organizes and adapts to generate the necessary force to internalize endocytic pits against the tension of the cell membrane.

    The study used a combination of experimental and computational approaches to model the behavior of the actin cytoskeleton during endocytosis. The researchers found that a minimal branched actin network, composed of actin, the Arp2/3 complex, and capping protein, is sufficient to generate the necessary force to internalize endocytic pits against mammalian plasma membrane tension. This network self-organizes into a radial branched array with growing ends oriented towards the base of the pit, and long actin filaments bend between attachment sites in the coat and the base of the pit.

    The study also showed that around 200 activated Arp2/3 complexes are required for robust internalization of endocytic pits. Using a newly developed molecule-counting method, the researchers determined that approximately 200 Arp2/3 complexes assemble at sites of clathrin-mediated endocytosis in human cells. Simulations predicted that the actin network self-organizes into a radial branched array with growing ends oriented towards the base of the pit, and that elastic energy stored in bent filaments contributes to endocytic internalization.

    The researchers also found that elevated membrane tension directs more growing filaments towards the base of the pit, increasing actin nucleation and bending for increased force production. This adaptive mechanism allows the actin network to change its organization and force-producing capacity under varying physical constraints. The study suggests that the spatially constrained actin filament assembly is essential for endocytosis under conditions of high membrane tension.

    The findings of this study provide new insights into the complex process of clathrin-mediated endocytosis and highlight the importance of the actin cytoskeleton in generating the necessary force to internalize endocytic pits against the tension of the cell membrane. The study's multiscale model of actin in mammalian endocytosis can be used to further investigate the mechanisms of endocytosis and the role of the actin cytoskeleton in this process.
summary
     Biologists have long studied macropinocytosis, a process by which cells engulf and internalize large volumes of extracellular fluid. A key step in this process is the formation of a macropinocytic cup, a cup-shaped projection of the plasma membrane driven by actin polymerization. Recent research has shed light on the molecular mechanisms underlying the formation of these cups in Dictyostelium cells.

     The study found that macropinocytic cups in Dictyostelium are organized around intense patches of PIP3, active Ras, and active Rac. These signalling patches are invariably associated with a ring of active SCAR/WAVE at their periphery. The formation of these patches does not depend on the enclosing F-actin ring, and they become enlarged when the RasGAP NF1 is mutated, indicating that Ras plays an instructive role in their formation.

     The researchers used lattice light sheet microscopy to examine macropinocytosis in 3D detail and mapped the spatial and temporal control of actin regulators such as SCAR and WASP with respect to signalling molecules like PIP3 and active Ras. This led them to propose a new hypothesis for the formation of cups from the plasma membrane.

     The study also found that new macropinocytic cups predominantly form by splitting from existing ones. The F-actin in de novo macropinosome cups is closely associated with signalling molecules, including active Ras. Circular ruffles can persist on the cell surface for prolonged periods before either closing successfully or regressing back into the cell body.

     The researchers propose that cup-shaped plasma membrane structures form from self-organizing patches of active Ras/PIP3, which recruit a ring of actin nucleators to their periphery. This ring of active SCAR drives a hollow ring of F-actin to extend the walls of the macropinocytic cup. The study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying macropinocytosis and the formation of macropinocytic cups.
Biologists have long understood the importance of the cohesin ring in holding together newly replicated sister chromatids until their separation at anaphase during cell division. A separate complex, Scc2NIPBL/Scc4Mau2 (Scc2/4), is responsible for loading cohesin onto DNA and determining its localization across the genome. Proper cohesin loading is crucial for cell division, and defects in this process can lead to chromosome missegregation and aberrant transcriptional regulation, resulting in severe developmental defects in multicellular organisms.

     Researchers have now determined the crystal structure of the interaction between Scc2 and Scc4, revealing that Scc4 is a TPR array that envelops an extended Scc2 peptide. Using budding yeast, the team demonstrated that a conserved patch on the surface of Scc4 is necessary to recruit Scc2/4 to centromeres and build pericentromeric cohesion. This finding shows that Scc4 plays a key role in determining the localization of cohesin loading and provides a molecular basis for Scc2/4 recruitment to centromeres.

     The study found that Scc4 targets cohesin loading to a specific genomic locus and that this function is separable from its essential role in establishing sister chromatid cohesion across the genome. The researchers used the structure of Scc4 bound to a minimal fragment of Scc2 to derive separation of function alleles that uncoupled cohesin loading from cohesin targeting to centromeres. These experiments demonstrated that cohesin targeting in yeast depends on Scc4, consistent with unpublished genetic evidence for an Scc4-dependent cohesin localization pathway.

     Further experiments showed that perturbation of the conserved patch on Scc4 eliminated centromeric localization of Scc2 in mitotic cells and reduced association of the cohesin subunit, Scc1, with the centromere and pericentromere, but not with chromosome arms. This pattern of Scc2 localization was also observed in cells progressing through S phase, the stage at which cohesin loading is initiated. The study suggests that the Scc4-dependent cohesin targeting pathway is a general feature of the control of cohesin loading across diverse eukaryotes.
Biomedical research has shown that being born late in the active season can have significant effects on the growth, fattening, and survival of hibernating animals. A study on garden dormice (Eliomys quercinus) investigated the differences in growth, fattening, energetic responses, winter survival, and fecundity between early-born (EB) and late-born (LB) juveniles. The results suggest that LB juveniles grow and gain mass twice as fast as EB individuals, but reach lower fat content before hibernation.

The study found that torpor use is low during intensive growth, but increases during pre-hibernation fattening. LB juveniles showed higher torpor use, reached similar body sizes, but lower fat content than EB individuals before hibernation. Additionally, LB individuals showed similar patterns of hibernation, but a higher proportion of breeders during the following year than EB dormice. These findings indicate that torpor is incompatible with growth, but promotes fattening and consolidates pre-hibernation fat depots.

The study also explored the effects of food availability on the growth and fattening of LB and EB juveniles. The results showed that LB juveniles grew and fattened faster than EB individuals, regardless of food availability. However, the use of energy-saving strategies, such as daily torpor, was higher in LB juveniles. The study also found that the pre-hibernation fat reserves of LB juveniles were sufficient to fuel their shorter winter duration due to the five-week delay of hibernation start.

The findings of this study are consistent with previous research on common dormice, which also showed no significant differences in winter survival between EB and LB juveniles. The study suggests that an extensive use of torpor is incompatible with structural growth, but supports pre-hibernation fattening late in the season and the preservation of fat reserves previously deposited prior to hibernation. Overall, the results indicate that being born late in the reproductive season is associated with a fast life history in garden dormice.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have long known that ADAR proteins play a crucial role in altering gene expression by converting adenosine to inosine in RNA. This process, known as A-to-I editing, is essential for proper neuronal function in all animals studied to date. However, the specific targets of ADAR proteins in the nervous system have remained unknown. A recent study in Caenorhabditis elegans, a type of nematode worm, aimed to identify critical ADAR targets in the neural transcriptome. The researchers used a software tool called SAILOR to analyze the effects of ADR-2, the only A-to-I editing enzyme in C. elegans, on the neural transcriptome. This analysis revealed over 7,300 A-to-I editing events across the neural transcriptome, including 104 novel edited genes. By intersecting the neural editome with gene expression changes associated with ADR-2, the researchers identified a specific mRNA, clec-41, whose neural expression is dependent on deamination. Restoration of clec-41 expression in ADR-2 deficient neural cells rescued the chemotaxis defect, providing the first evidence that neuronal phenotypes of ADAR mutants can be caused by altered gene expression. This study is the first to link a phenotype associated with loss of C. elegans ADAR enzymes to editing-dependent regulation of a specific gene. The findings suggest that altered neural gene expression, rather than recoding events, is responsible for the aberrant behavioral phenotypes caused by lack of C. elegans ADR-2. The study provides the first analysis of the tissue-specific role of ADR-2 in A-to-I editing and regulating neural gene expression in C. elegans. The results have significant implications for understanding the neurobiological effects of RNA editing in the nervous system.
summary
     Influenza A virus (IAV) uses a common strategy called host shutoff to repress cellular mRNA translation and allow efficient translation of viral mRNAs. To understand the mechanisms behind this process, researchers used RNA-sequencing and ribosome profiling to study IAV-induced host shutoff. The results show that viral transcripts are not preferentially translated, and instead, the decline in cellular protein synthesis is caused by the viral takeover of the mRNA pool. This takeover leads to a reduction in cellular mRNA expression, which is the main cause of host shutoff during IAV infection.

The study found that viral genes have translation efficiencies similar to those of host genes, indicating that direct co-opting of the translation machinery does not play a dominant role in cellular gene shutoff. Instead, the viral dominance over the mRNA pool is the primary mechanism behind host shutoff. At 8 hours post-infection (hpi), viral mRNAs take over 53.8% of the translation activity in the cells, while 57.3% of the mRNAs in the cells are viral. This suggests that the relative reduction in cellular mRNA expression is the main cause of host shutoff during IAV infection.

Interestingly, the study found that short transcripts are less affected by IAV infection, and these mRNAs are enriched in cell maintenance processes such as oxidative phosphorylation. Furthermore, the continuous oxidative phosphorylation activity is important for viral propagation. The results of this study advance our understanding of IAV-induced host shutoff and suggest a mechanism that facilitates the translation of genes with important housekeeping functions.

The findings of this study contradict previous suggestions that IAV uses direct co-opting of the translation machinery to repress cellular mRNA translation. Instead, the viral takeover of the mRNA pool is the primary mechanism behind host shutoff. This study provides new insights into the mechanisms of IAV-induced host shutoff and highlights the importance of oxidative phosphorylation in viral propagation. Overall, the results of this study contribute to our understanding of the complex interactions between IAV and the host cell.
Biologists have long known that gradients of signaling proteins play a crucial role in shaping the development of tissues and organs. However, the mechanisms behind the formation of these gradients are not yet fully understood. A recent study in Drosophila (fruit flies) has shed new light on this process by investigating how a signaling protein called FGF (Fibroblast Growth Factor) forms a gradient in the development of the wing imaginal disc.

In this study, researchers used fluorescently-tagged versions of FGF and its receptor, FGFR, to visualize their distribution in the wing imaginal disc. They found that FGF produced in the disc moves to the air-sac-primordium (ASP) through specialized structures called cytonemes that extend from the ASP to the wing disc. The number of cytonemes decreases with increasing distance from the FGF source, generating a gradient of FGF in the ASP.

The researchers also discovered that the transcription factors Pointed-P1 and Cut, which are activated by FGF, regulate the formation of FGFR-containing cytonemes. These transcription factors create regions with higher or lower numbers of FGF-receiving cytonemes, resulting in a precise gradient of FGF in the ASP. This gradient, in turn, regulates the expression of specific genes in the ASP, such as pointed-P1 at higher levels and cut at lower levels.

This study reveals a novel mechanism by which morphogens, such as FGF, can self-generate precise tissue-specific gradient contours through feedback regulation of cytoneme-mediated dispersion. The findings have important implications for our understanding of tissue morphogenesis and the development of complex tissues and organs. The study demonstrates that cytoneme-dependent signaling can provide precision and adaptability in shaping long-range positional gradients and tissue architectures in space and time.

The researchers used the Drosophila larval wing imaginal disc as a model system to investigate the formation of the FGF gradient. This system is unique because the wing disc and the ASP are spatially separated, allowing for the study of long-range signaling. The findings of this study provide new insights into the mechanisms of tissue morphogenesis and highlight the importance of cytoneme-dependent signaling in shaping the development of complex tissues and organs.
Despite being the most well-understood protein fold, Î±-helical coiled coils have been found to exhibit an unexpected property. Researchers have discovered that inserting two or six residues into the heptad repeat of a parallel, trimeric coiled coil can cause the formation of a new type of fiber. This insertion strains the supercoil, leading to the local formation of short Î²-strands. These Î²-strands move the path of the chain by 120Â° around the trimer axis, resulting in the creation of an Î±/Î² coiled coil.

The Î±/Î² coiled coil retains only one backbone hydrogen bond per repeat unit from the parent coiled coil. This novel structure is possible within the allowed regions of the Ramachandran space with only minor mutations to a known fold. The Î±-helical coiled coil is a ubiquitous protein domain found in a wide range of structural and functional contexts. It was the first protein fold described in atomic detail and is also the only one whose backbone structure can be computed with parametric equations.

Insertions of two and six residues were previously thought to cause the greatest strain on Î±-helical geometry. However, researchers have found that these insertions break the Î±-helices to form short Î²-strands, which associate into a triangular supersecondary structure called the Î²-layer. The Î²-layer is found repetitively in natural coiled coils, where it forms regular fibers with alternating Î±- and Î²-structure. This protein fold has not been described before.

The Î±/Î² coiled coil is a new class of protein fiber based on a novel supersecondary structure element. It consists of alternating Î±- and Î²-layers, with the first Î²-layer occurring after a position g of the preceding heptad. This g position is biased towards a c position, yielding the same g/c position. The discovery of the Î±/Î² coiled coil expands our understanding of protein structure and design possibilities.
summary
     Epsin is a protein that plays a crucial role in the process of endocytosis, which is the uptake of molecules into cells through the formation of clathrin-coated pits. Endocytosis is essential for various cellular functions, including cell signaling, nutrient uptake, and waste removal. However, the exact function of epsin in endocytosis has remained elusive until now.

     Researchers generated embryonic fibroblasts from mice lacking all three forms of epsin (epsin triple KO mice) to study its role in endocytosis. The results showed that the absence of epsin led to a significant impairment in clathrin-mediated endocytosis, resulting in the accumulation of early and U-shaped clathrin-coated pits. This defect was correlated with a disruption in the interaction between the clathrin coat and the actin cytoskeleton, which is essential for the invagination and fission of clathrin-coated pits.

     In a cell-free assay, the researchers confirmed that epsin is involved in the coupling between the clathrin coat and the actin cytoskeleton. They found that epsin helps generate the force necessary for the invagination and fission of clathrin-coated pits. This process is critical for the successful completion of endocytosis.

     The study also revealed a weak interaction between epsin's ENTH domain and the SNARE synaptobrevin 2/VAMP2, which is involved in the fusion of intracellular vesicles with their target membranes. However, the researchers found that epsin's most critical function is at the earlier stages of clathrin coat maturation, specifically at the transition between shallow/U-shaped and deep invaginated pits.

     The findings of this study provide new insights into the role of epsin in endocytosis and highlight its importance in the coupling of the endocytic clathrin coat to the actin cytoskeleton. The study suggests that epsin plays a key role in generating the force necessary for the invagination and fission of clathrin-coated pits, which is essential for the successful completion of endocytosis.
A study conducted in urban Uganda investigated the risk factors for asthma among schoolchildren aged 5-17 years. The researchers used a case-control study design, comparing 555 children with asthma (cases) to 1115 children without asthma (controls). The study found that several factors increased the risk of asthma in schoolchildren, including having a father or mother with a tertiary education, being born in a small town or city, and having a family history of asthma or allergic conditions.

The study showed a strong association between the area of residence at birth and the risk of asthma. Children born in small towns or cities had a higher risk of asthma compared to those born in rural areas. This risk increased further in the presence of other asthma risk factors, such as parental history of allergic disease, children's own atopy, higher socio-economic status, and urbanicity.

The researchers found that children with asthma were slightly older and more likely to have parents with a tertiary education and a reported history of asthma. The study also found that even among children with no parental history of allergic disease, being born in a town or city was associated with an increased risk of asthma compared to being born in a rural area.

The study's findings suggest that asthma is associated with a strong rural-town-city risk gradient, higher parental socio-economic status, and urbanicity. This is particularly concerning given the rapid urbanization in Africa and other low- and middle-income countries (LMICs), which is likely to increase the prevalence of asthma. The study provides a basis for future research to identify specific environmental and lifestyle factors that contribute to the increased risk of asthma among children in urban areas of LMICs.
summary
     Biomedical research has shed light on the molecular mechanisms underlying the formation of color patterns in zebrafish. A recent study has identified the importance of gap junctions composed of connexins 41.8 and 39.4 in this process. Gap junctions are specialized channels that allow direct communication between adjacent cells. In zebrafish, three types of pigment cells â melanophores, xanthophores, and iridophores â interact to form the characteristic striped pattern on their bodies and fins.

     Mutations in the leopard (leo) gene, which encodes connexin 41.8 (Cx41.8), result in a series of spotted patterns in zebrafish. A new dominant allele, leotK3, leads to a complete loss of the pattern, suggesting that it has a dominant negative effect on another component of gap junctions. Further investigation revealed that this component is connexin 39.4 (Cx39.4), encoded by the luchs gene. Loss-of-function alleles of luchs demonstrate that it is essential for stripe formation in zebrafish, but the fins are largely unaffected.

     Double mutants and chimeras showed that leo and luchs are only required in xanthophores and melanophores, but not in iridophores. This suggests that Cx41.8 and Cx39.4 form heteromeric gap junctions, which promote homotypic interactions between melanophores and xanthophores, respectively. These interactions instruct the patterning of iridophores.

     The study found that fish homozygous for leotK3 or trans-heterozygous for leotK3 over leot1 show an identical phenotype of a completely dissolved pattern. Individual melanophores that hardly cluster together are still present, mostly associated with blue iridophores. This indicates that the dramatic expansion of dense iridophores is an indirect effect, possibly caused by mutant xanthophores covering the iridophores.

     The research suggests that Cx41.8 and Cx39.4 interact to form heteromeric gap junctions, which are responsible for some of the interactions that take place among chromatophores while the pigment pattern is established. The study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying the formation of color patterns in zebrafish and highlights the importance of gap junctions in this process.
Summary of Biomedical Research Article: Evolution of Insect Olfactory Receptors

     Insects have a highly developed sense of smell that allows them to detect a wide range of behaviorally relevant odor molecules. This sense is mediated by different types of receptors, including olfactory receptors (ORs), ionotropic receptors (IRs), and gustatory receptors (GRs). While IRs are found in the olfactory organs of all protostomes, ORs are thought to have evolved as an adaptation to the terrestrial lifestyle of insects.

     However, a recent study investigated the olfactory system of three ancient insect species: the wingless bristletail Lepismachilis y-signata, the firebrat Thermobia domestica, and the neopteran leaf insect Phyllium siccifolium. The results suggest that ORs and the olfactory coreceptor (Orco) are likely absent in Lepismachilis, but present in Thermobia and Phyllium. This indicates that ORs did not evolve as an adaptation to a terrestrial lifestyle, but rather later in insect evolution.

     The study also found that the coreceptor Orco evolved after the bristletails split from their last common ancestor with the remaining insects. However, an olfactory system that relies on both ORs and Orco seems to have evolved after the emergence of wings. This suggests that the evolution of the insect olfactory system is more complex than previously thought.

     The researchers used a combination of approaches to identify the receptors involved in odor detection in these ancient insects. They found that in neopteran insects, most volatile stimuli are recognized by members of the olfactory receptor family (ORs). ORs are distinct from nematode and vertebrate olfactory receptors and have a unique membrane topology.

     The study provides new insights into the evolution of the insect olfactory system and suggests that ORs and Orco evolved at different times in insect evolution. The results also highlight the importance of studying ancient insects to understand the evolution of specific building blocks in olfaction. The findings have implications for our understanding of the evolution of the insect sense of smell and its adaptation to different environments.
summary
     Biomedical researchers conducted a study in Myanmar to investigate the effectiveness of mass antimalarial drug administration (MDA) in reducing the spread of Plasmodium falciparum malaria. The study took place in four villages along the Myanmar-Thailand border, where MDA had been implemented to control the disease. The researchers analyzed data on malaria episodes and asymptomatic infections in the villages before and after the MDA.

     The results showed that individuals living in neighborhoods with low MDA adherence had a higher risk of developing malaria episodes after the treatment, compared to those living in areas with high adherence. This suggests a "herd effect," where the protection provided by MDA benefits not only those who received the treatment but also those who did not. The study also found that high mosquito biting rates, living in a house with someone who has malaria, or having an asymptomatic malaria infection increased the risk of developing a clinical episode.

     The researchers used detailed spatial and temporal data to examine the relationship between individual and group-level participation in MDA, subclinical infections, and clinical episodes of P. falciparum post-MDA. They found that clinical episodes decreased among all groups for a longer period than the prophylactic effect of the administered antimalarials, indicating a group-level effect from MDA adherence.

     The study provides evidence of a herd effect conferred by MDA for P. falciparum malaria, where the protection provided by the treatment benefits not only those who received it but also those who did not. The findings suggest that spatial clustering of non-adherence to MDA, even in villages with high overall participation, may hinder elimination efforts. The study's results have important implications for further research, intervention, and policy work in the control and elimination of malaria.

     The study's primary objective was to investigate the potential herd effect and the impact of non-adherence with regard to MDA for P. falciparum malaria. The researchers found that the herd effect was evident from three lines of evidence: the decrease in clinical episodes among all groups for a longer period than the prophylactic effect of the administered antimalarials, the association between low MDA adherence and increased risk of malaria episodes, and the increase in clinical P. falciparum episodes post-MDA corresponding to an increase in village mosquito biting rates. The study's findings provide an evidence base for further research, intervention, and policy work in the control and elimination of malaria.
Summary of Conservation of Transcription Factor Binding Specificities across 600 Million Years of Bilateria Evolution
     Biologists have long sought to understand how the diverse forms of life on Earth arose from a common ancestor. One key factor in this process is the regulation of gene expression, which is controlled by proteins called transcription factors (TFs). These proteins bind to specific DNA sequences to turn genes on or off, and changes in their binding specificities can lead to the emergence of new cell types and body forms. However, it has been difficult to assess the importance of changes in TF binding specificities in the evolution of different species.

     To address this question, researchers studied the binding specificities of 242 TFs in the fruit fly Drosophila melanogaster and compared them to those in humans and mice. They found that the binding specificities of TFs are highly conserved between Drosophila and mammals, meaning that the same TFs bind to similar DNA sequences in both species. This conservation extends even to subtle differences in DNA binding preferences, suggesting that TF binding specificities are not subject to significant evolutionary drift.

     The researchers used a technique called HT-SELEX to determine the binding specificities of Drosophila TFs, which revealed a striking level of conservation with human TFs. They found that Drosophila has almost as complex a repertoire of TF binding motifs as humans, and that the conservation of specificity extends to secondary modes of binding and even subtle dinucleotide preferences.

     The study also showed that TFs that belong to the same structural family generally bind to similar sites, and that orthologous TFs in Drosophila and humans have almost identical core DNA binding profiles, flanking sequences, and secondary modes of binding. This suggests that changes in TF binding specificities contribute to the emergence of new cell types and body forms.

     The researchers identified a few human TFs with divergent specificities that function in cell types not found in fruit flies, indicating that the evolution of TF specificities plays a role in the emergence of novel types of differentiated cells. Overall, this study provides new insights into the conservation of TF binding specificities across 600 million years of bilateria evolution and sheds light on the mechanisms underlying the diversity of life on Earth.
Researchers have developed a new method to study how the heart and lungs develop together in the human body. This process, called cardio-pulmonary co-development, is critical for the proper formation and maturation of these organs. However, there has been a lack of models to investigate this process in humans.

     Scientists used human-induced pluripotent stem cells (hiPSCs), which can become any cell type in the body, to create a model of cardio-pulmonary co-development. They used a step-by-step approach to direct the simultaneous induction of cardiac and lung epithelial lineages within a single differentiation of hiPSCs. This was achieved by tuning the WNT and Nodal signaling pathways in the absence of external growth factors.

     The researchers used a 3D suspension culture system to create concentric cardio-pulmonary micro-tissues (Î¼Ts), which effectively segregated into separate cardiac and pulmonary Î¼Ts. The presence of cardiac accompaniment was found to promote alveolar maturation in the lung tissue. When the WNT agonist was withdrawn, the cardiac and pulmonary components within each dual-lineage Î¼T segregated from each other, and cardiac contraction was initiated.

     This study provides an experimentally tractable system for investigating human cardio-pulmonary interaction and tissue boundary formation during embryogenesis. The model allows for the study of the mechanistic interplay between the developing heart and lung, which is essential for their proper formation and maturation. The cardio-pulmonary mutual interaction during organogenesis has been well-documented in mouse models, but this study delivers an effective in vitro model for studying this process in humans.

     The researchers demonstrated that co-induction of mesoderm and endoderm drove simultaneous cardiac and pulmonary specification from their respective germ-layer progenitors. Upon transitioning the co-induced cardiac and pulmonary progenitors to 3D suspension culture, they observed expedited alveolar maturation within 3 days, supported by the accompanying cardiac lineage. This study offers a novel model for investigating the molecular and cellular mechanisms underlying human cardio-pulmonary co-development and tissue boundary formation. Further investigation is needed to explore the morphological and functional maturation of both cardiac and pulmonary lineages and their crosstalk during this process.
summary
     Biologists have long believed that an organism's mutation rate is a slowly-evolving parameter that is strictly regulated. However, a recent study on Escherichia coli (E. coli) has revealed an unexpected flexibility in cellular mutation rates as a response to changes in selective pressure. The researchers found that when E. coli cells are exposed to high ethanol stress, their mutation rate increases, allowing them to adapt quickly to the stressful environment. This increase in mutation rate is not a permanent change, but rather a transitory state that decreases once the cells are adapted to the stress.

     The study used E. coli as a model system to investigate the dynamics of hypermutation under near-lethal, complex stress. The researchers found that hypermutator states, which are characterized by high mutation rates, are essential for de novo adaptation to high ethanol stress. They also identified cellular mortality as the major force driving the quick evolution of mutation rates. In other words, the death of cells that are unable to adapt to the stress leads to an increase in mutation rates in the remaining cells, allowing them to adapt more quickly.

     The researchers used a defined collection of mutators with distinct mutation rates to identify a range of optimal mutation rates that enable rapid growth under high ethanol stress. They found that an increase in mutation rate is necessary for adaptation to high ethanol stress, but it is not a permanent change. Instead, the mutation rate decreases once the cells are adapted to the stress.

     The study provides new insights into the dynamics of mutation rate evolution and helps explain why maintaining high mutation rates is limited in time. The findings have implications for our understanding of the emergence of antibiotic resistance in microbes and cancer relapses upon chemotherapy. The study suggests that the balance between robustness and evolvability is crucial for an organism's ability to adapt to changing environments. The results also highlight the importance of cellular mortality in driving the evolution of mutation rates in response to stress.
summary
     Biologists have long sought to understand how plants adjust their growth and defense responses to changing environmental conditions. A key player in this process is the AMPK/Snf1/SnRK1 kinase, a metabolic master regulator found in animals, yeast, and plants. This kinase acts at two levels: modulating the activity of key metabolic enzymes and reprogramming gene expression. While its first function is well understood, its role in transcriptional reprogramming is less clear, particularly in plants.

     Researchers have identified the Arabidopsis transcription factor bZIP63 as a key regulator of the starvation response and a direct target of the SnRK1 kinase. Phosphorylation of bZIP63 by SnRK1 changes its dimerization preference, affecting target gene expression and primary metabolism. A bZIP63 knock-out mutant exhibited starvation-related phenotypes, which could be functionally complemented by wild-type bZIP63 but not by a version harboring point mutations in the identified SnRK1 target sites.

     The study found that the SnRK1 kinase AKIN10 is the major kinase responsible for the starvation-induced hyper-phosphorylation of bZIP63. The importance of bZIP63 phosphorylation at the SnRK1 target sites for its in planta function was further underlined by complementation of the bzip63 mutant with genomic bZIP63 constructs. Wild-type bZIP63, but not a mutant version, could complement the metabolic and senescence phenotypes of the bzip63 mutant.

     The researchers also showed that bZIP63 is an important metabolic regulator, especially under stress/starvation conditions, and that it is phosphorylated at multiple sites in vivo in a sugar- and energy-dependent manner. They identified SnRK1 as one of the kinases responsible for bZIP63 phosphorylation and found that it targets three highly conserved serine residues in the N- and C-terminus of bZIP63.

     To verify that SnRK1 plays an important role in the in vivo phosphorylation of bZIP63, the researchers compared the phosphorylation state of bZIP63 in plants overexpressing bZIP63-GFP or -YFP in the wild-type and akin10 background, respectively. They demonstrated that the phosphorylation of these sites is crucial for bZIP63's dimerization and activity in planta and proposed a molecular model for a phosphorylation-triggered switch of bZIP63 dimerization partners, which ultimately regulates metabolic reprogramming.
Biomedical researchers have identified a novel protein called ring finger protein 10 (RNF10) that acts as a messenger between synapses and the nucleus in the brain. Synapses are the connections between neurons where information is transmitted, and the nucleus is the control center of the cell where genes are expressed. RNF10 is activated by calcium signals at the synapse and triggers changes in gene expression in the nucleus.

RNF10 is found at excitatory synapses, where it interacts with a specific subunit of NMDA receptors called GluN2A. When NMDA receptors are activated, RNF10 is released from the synapse and transported to the nucleus through a process called importin-dependent long-distance transport. This transport mechanism allows RNF10 to carry information from the synapse to the nucleus, where it can influence gene expression.

The researchers found that RNF10 is essential for the maintenance of long-term potentiation (LTP), a process that strengthens the connection between neurons and is thought to be involved in learning and memory. When RNF10 is silenced, LTP is impaired, and the structure of dendritic spines, which are the sites of synaptic connections, is altered. This suggests that RNF10 plays a critical role in the long-lasting changes that occur in the brain as a result of synaptic activity.

The study also found that RNF10 interacts with other proteins, including calmodulin (CaM), which is involved in calcium signaling. This interaction suggests that RNF10 may be part of a larger signaling pathway that links synaptic activity to gene expression in the nucleus.

Overall, the discovery of RNF10 as a synaptonuclear messenger provides new insights into the mechanisms by which synaptic activity influences gene expression and long-term changes in the brain. The findings have implications for our understanding of learning and memory and may lead to the development of new treatments for neurological disorders.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand the mechanisms by which Swi2/Snf2 ATPases, a family of enzymes, remodel protein-DNA complexes to regulate chromatin structure and epigenetic state. The enzyme Mot1, a single-subunit remodeler, has been identified as a useful model system for studying Swi2/Snf2 enzymes due to its ability to dissociate TATA box-binding protein (TBP):DNA complexes. In a recent study, researchers have determined the crystal structure of the N-terminal domain of Mot1 in complex with TBP, DNA, and the transcription regulator negative cofactor 2 (NC2) at a resolution of 3.8 Ã.

     The structure reveals that Mot1 reduces DNA:NC2 interactions and unbends DNA compared to the TBP:DNA:NC2 state, suggesting that Mot1 primes TBP:NC2 displacement in an ATP-independent manner. Electron microscopy and cross-linking data suggest that the Swi2/Snf2 domain of Mot1 associates with the upstream DNA and the histone fold of NC2, revealing parallels to some nucleosome remodelers. This study provides a structural framework for how a Swi2/Snf2 ATPase interacts with its substrate DNA:protein complex.

     Swi2/Snf2-type ATPases remodel protein-DNA complexes to regulate chromatin structure and epigenetic state during transcription, DNA replication, and DNA repair. Mot1, apart from being an essential gene regulator, serves as an attractive model system for structural and mechanistic studies of Swi2/Snf2 enzymes. Binding of Mot1 leads to subtle but consistent conformational changes between substrate proteins and DNA, which could prime the complex for ATP-dependent disruption.

     The interface between Mot1 and NC2 observed in the crystal structure is small, but the contact between Mot1's insertion domain and NC2 could help separate NC2's main TBP anchor helix H5 from TBP and H4 from DNA. The study provides a pseudoatomic structure of the Swi2/Snf2 enzyme Mot1 bound to its protein-DNA substrate and a detailed structural framework for the interaction of Mot1 with the TBP:DNA:NC2 complex. Swi2/Snf2 enzymes typically remodel protein-DNA complexes by disrupting the interface between the nucleic and protein partners of the target complex.

     ATP hydrolysis-dependent translocation of the Swi2/Snf2 ATPase domain could provide most if not all of the chemomechanical force, but a question is whether binding of the remodeler induces conformational changes in the target. The association of Mot1 with the TBP:DNA:NC2 complex results in some unexpected conformational changes that may have implications for understanding the ATP-dependent dissociation mechanism. The study provides the first pseudoatomic view of a Swi2/Snf2 ATPase in complex with a DNA:protein substrate complex.
Summary
     Biomedical researchers have identified the molecular target of a natural compound called ternatin, which is toxic to cancer cells. Ternatin is a cyclic peptide that was previously known to inhibit the growth of fat cells, but its mechanism of action was unknown. By designing synthetic variants of ternatin, the researchers found that these compounds are up to 500 times more potent against cancer cells than the original compound.

     Further investigation revealed that ternatin and its variants bind to a complex of proteins called the elongation factor-1A ternary complex (eEF1AÂ·GTPÂ·aminoacyl-tRNA). This complex is essential for protein synthesis in cells. The researchers used a photo-affinity probe to confirm that ternatin binds to the eEF1A ternary complex and identified two other natural compounds, didemnin and cytotrienin, that also bind to this complex.

     Mutations in a specific domain of the eEF1A protein prevented ternatin from binding and made cells resistant to its toxic effects. This suggests that the hydrophobic surface of the eEF1A protein is a key site for ternatin binding. The researchers conclude that the eEF1A ternary complex is a common target for natural compounds that are toxic to cancer cells.

     The study also found that ternatin and its variants inhibit protein synthesis in cells, which correlates with their ability to block cell proliferation. The researchers used a photo-ternatin probe to identify the eEF1A ternary complex as a direct target of ternatin-related cyclic peptides. This is the first time a small molecule has been shown to bind specifically to the eEF1A ternary complex.

     The discovery of the eEF1A ternary complex as a target for ternatin and other natural compounds may lead to the development of new cancer therapies. The simplicity and accessibility of the ternatin structure make it an attractive candidate for future medicinal chemistry efforts. Further studies are needed to understand the mechanism of action of ternatin and its variants and to explore their potential as cancer treatments.
summary
     Biomedical research has shed new light on the role of OCRL in clathrin-coated pit dynamics and uncoating. OCRL is a protein that interacts directly with clathrin, a key component of clathrin-coated pits. Mutations in the OCRL gene cause Lowe syndrome and Dent's disease, which have primarily been attributed to intracellular sorting defects. However, studies of Lowe syndrome patient fibroblasts have revealed that OCRL loss impacts clathrin-mediated endocytosis, resulting in an endocytic defect. This defect is characterized by an accumulation of clathrin-coated vesicles and an increase in U-shaped clathrin-coated pits.

     Further investigation showed that OCRL acts as an uncoating factor, facilitating the recycling of endocytic factors necessary for the normal progression of endocytosis. The absence of OCRL in patient cells delays endocytosis by stalling late-stage pits, impairs uncoating, and triggers the nucleation of actin comets from clathrin vesicles that retain phosphatidylinositol 4,5-bisphosphate (PI(4,5)P2) and fail to uncoat.

     The study also found that SNX9, a clathrin-binding protein associated with late-stage endocytic clathrin-coated pits, directly binds OCRL. In Lowe syndrome patient fibroblasts, the absence of OCRL impairs endocytic clathrin coat dynamics, resulting in an accumulation of clathrin-coated vesicles that fail to lose their coat. These vesicles nucleate at least a cohort of the intracellular actin comets previously described in Lowe syndrome patient cells.

     The findings suggest that defects in clathrin-mediated endocytosis contribute to the pathology in patients with OCRL mutations. The study provides new insights into the role of OCRL in clathrin-coated pit dynamics and uncoating, and highlights the importance of OCRL in facilitating the normal progression of endocytosis. The results have implications for our understanding of Lowe syndrome and Dent's disease, and may lead to the development of new therapeutic strategies for these disorders.
Biomedical researchers have identified a new pathway that contributes to the development of colorectal cancer. The Wnt/Î²-catenin signaling pathway is known to play a crucial role in colorectal cancer, but finding effective inhibitors for this pathway has been a significant challenge. To better understand how this pathway is regulated, scientists investigated the role of FAK (focal adhesion kinase) and PYK2 (proline-rich tyrosine kinase 2) in intestinal tumorigenesis.

The study found that FAK and PYK2 are elevated in adenomas in mice and human colorectal cancer tissues. These proteins function redundantly to promote the Wnt/Î²-catenin pathway by phosphorylating GSK3Î² (glycogen synthase kinase 3 beta) at the Y216 site. This phosphorylation event reinforces the pathway's output, leading to the accumulation of Î²-catenin and intestinal tumorigenesis. The researchers also discovered that the phosphorylation of GSK3Î²Y216 is a molecular determinant of GSK3Î²'s recruitment of Î²-TrCP (Î²-transducin repeat-containing protein), which is necessary for Wnt-induced Î²-catenin accumulation.

Pharmacological inhibition of FAK/PYK2 using the inhibitor PF-562271 suppressed adenoma formation in APCmin/+ mice, which are genetically predisposed to intestinal tumorigenesis. This was accompanied by reduced levels of phospho-GSK3Î²Y216 and Î²-catenin in the intestine. These findings indicate that the FAK/PYK2/GSK3Î²Y216 axis is critical for the activation of Wnt/Î²-catenin signaling in APC-driven intestinal tumorigenesis.

The study also revealed that the loss of functional APC (adenomatous polyposis coli) leads to the expression of FAK and PYK2, which in turn phosphorylate GSK3Î²Y216 to stabilize Î²-catenin, initiating and supporting intestinal tumorigenesis. The phosphorylation of GSK3Î²Y216 was previously identified two decades ago, but the underlying mechanisms remained unclear. This study provides new insights into the regulation of the Wnt/Î²-catenin pathway and identifies a potential therapeutic target for the treatment of colorectal cancer.
summary
     Biomedical research has investigated the neural control of transitions between posture and movement, focusing on the regulation of reflex-stabilizing mechanisms to enable motion. A study examined the continuity of vestibular control of balance during transitions between quiet standing and locomotion, as well as between two standing postures. Healthy subjects initiated and terminated locomotion or shifted the distribution of their weight between their feet while exposed to electrical vestibular stimuli (EVS). The relationship between EVS and ground reaction forces was quantified using time-frequency analyses.

The results showed discontinuities corresponding to null coherence periods preceding the onset of movement initiation and during the step preceding locomotion termination. These findings indicate that humans interrupt the vestibular balance stabilizing mechanisms to transition between motor states, suggesting a discrete change between motor control policies as predicted by optimal feedback theory.

The study specifically examined the contribution of vestibular sensory signals to the control of balance during transitions between quiet standing and locomotion, as well as between two standing postures. The vestibular system encodes motion of the head in space and is vital to maintaining stability during both quiet standing and locomotion. Vestibular signals influence the activation of muscles engaged in the control of posture.

To transition between two motor states, balance correcting responses are disrupted, and the vestibular influence on whole-body balance responses is suspended. This suspension occurs while subjects are still maintaining quiet upright stance, well before the transition onset. The suppression of vestibular balance stabilizing mechanisms is not specific to one type of transition, suggesting it represents a general control process that occurs during transitions between standing balance and other motor states.

The period of suspension of vestibular-evoked responses appears related to the different relative sensory feedback gains needed to control whole-body movements by the quiet standing and locomotor control policies. During quiet standing, whole-body accelerations and oscillations within the limits of the base of support are small, and an adaptive feedback controller is used. The vestibular control of balance during locomotor or posture-to-posture transitions can be monitored by continuously quantifying the magnitude of vestibular-evoked balance responses over the transition. This approach has been used previously to monitor the time course of engagement and disengagement of the vestibular control of balance between self- and externally-driven states of balance control.
summary
     The circadian output pathway in cyanobacteria plays a crucial role in regulating metabolic programs in response to light availability. The transcription factor RpaA is the master regulator of circadian transcription in cyanobacteria, driving genome-wide oscillations in mRNA abundance. Deletion of rpaA has no effect on viability in constant light conditions, but renders cells inviable in cycling conditions when light and dark periods alternate. This study investigated the mechanisms underlying this viability defect and found that the rpaA- strain cannot maintain appropriate energy status at night, does not accumulate carbon reserves during the day, and is defective in transcription of genes crucial for utilization of carbohydrate stores at night.

The rpaA- strain exhibits defects in cell growth and viability in cyclic, but not in constant light environments, suggesting an important role for circadian regulation of gene expression in alternating light/dark cycles. The phosphorylated form of RpaA, regulated by the circadian clock, is critical for the coordination of carbon anabolism and catabolism and thus cell viability in light/dark cycles. Deletion of rpaA prevents glycogen accumulation in light and renders cells unable to utilize existing glycogen at dusk, leading to alterations in cellular energy charge and a severe reduction in fitness.

Reconstruction of carbon utilization pathways combined with provision of an external carbon source restores energy charge and viability of the rpaA- strain in light/dark cycling conditions. This suggests that functional circadian output mediated by the activity of RpaA is required for the anticipatory accumulation of carbon reserves during the day, as well as metabolic switching to carbon catabolism at dusk. These metabolic adaptations allow cells to stimulate energy production and sustain cell viability during periods of darkness that challenge and constrain resources for cyanobacterial growth.
