The study highlights how a circadian output pathway controls and temporally coordinates essential pathways in carbon metabolism to maximize fitness of cells facing periodic energy limitations. The findings provide insight into the role of the circadian output pathway in enhancing fitness of cyanobacteria through coordination of central carbon metabolism with the metabolic demands imposed by periodic changes in the external environment.
summary
     Biomedical research has shed light on the structure and ion-release mechanism of PIB-4-type ATPases, a subclass of heavy-metal transporting P-type ATPases. These enzymes play a crucial role in maintaining cellular homeostasis by transporting transition metals such as zinc, copper, and cobalt across cellular membranes. However, the metal specificity of PIB-4-ATPases has remained elusive, with some members showing specificity for cobalt, while others transport a broader range of ions, including zinc, nickel, copper, and calcium.

     A recent study has provided the first structural information on an archetypal PIB-4-ATPase, sCoaT from Sulfitobacter sp. NAS14-1. The data reveal that PIB-4-ATPases lack classical heavy-metal-binding domains (HMBDs) and display an eight-helix M-domain configuration. This finding provides new insights into the mechanism and diversity of heavy-metal transporters. The study also reveals a dual role for PIB-ATPases in heavy-metal release and as an internal counter ion of an invariant histidine.

     Furthermore, the research shows that the turnover of PIB-ATPases is potassium-independent, contrasting with many other P-type ATPases. This discovery opens up new avenues for drug discovery, as PIB-4-ATPases function as virulence factors in many pathogens. The study also identifies novel P-type ATPase features, including a dual role in heavy-metal release and as an internal counter ion of an invariant histidine.

     The study employed the established PIB-4 model sCoaT to shed light on the structure and mechanism of the entire PIB-4-class. The ATPase activity was assessed in vitro in lipid-detergent solution using the Baginski assay, in the presence of a range of different heavy metals. The results provide a promising lead for drug-discovery efforts or to aid the development of tools to manipulate heavy-metal accumulation in plants to prevent accumulation or for enrichment.
Biomedical researchers have long been interested in understanding the phenomenon of monoallelic expression (MAE), where a single allele of a gene is expressed while the other allele remains silent. MAE plays a crucial role in various human diseases, but its regulation has been challenging to study due to its clonal nature. A recent study has identified a chromatin signature that distinguishes MAE genes in human cells with high accuracy. This signature is characterized by the simultaneous presence of chromatin marks associated with active transcription (H3K36me3) and silencing (H3K27me3) in the gene body.

     The researchers applied this approach to various cell types and found that approximately 20% of ubiquitously expressed genes and over 30% of tissue-specific genes exhibited the MAE chromatin signature. Notably, this signature was enriched among key developmental genes that have bivalent chromatin structure in pluripotent cells. The study suggests that MAE is linked to cell differentiation and opens a new approach to studying MAE that is independent of polymorphisms.

     The researchers estimated that up to 20% of ubiquitously expressed genes and more than one-third of tissue-specific genes showed the chromatin signature of MAE. This detection method does not rely on single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) or sample clonality, making it feasible to analyze MAE in primary samples, including comparisons of normal and diseased tissues.

     The study also found that genes with the MAE chromatin signature share some pronounced features as a group. Importantly, the MAE identified in clones is reflected by the chromatin signature in a polyclonal sample. To test the chromatin signature as a predictor of the MAE state, the researchers identified novel genes with the MAE chromatin pattern and assessed their allelic bias in monoclonal cell lines. They found that a remarkably large fraction of known bivalent genes acquire the MAE chromatin signature in at least one of the differentiated cell types, much higher than other genes that are also silent in embryonic stem cells. This suggests that upon activation, these master regulator genes preferentially resolve into a state with the characteristic MAE chromatin signature.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have discovered a new mechanism for controlling the activity of AAA+ chaperones, a type of protein complex that plays a crucial role in protein folding and degradation. AAA+ chaperones, such as ClpC, are essential for bacterial virulence and stress resistance, but their activity must be tightly regulated to prevent damage to the cell.

     ClpC, a central AAA+ chaperone found in Gram-positive bacteria, forms a complex with the peptidase ClpP to create a bacterial protease. The activity of ClpC is controlled by the adaptor protein MecA, which targets substrates and stimulates ClpC's ATPase activity. However, ClpC's activity must be repressed in its ground state to prevent damage to the cell.

     Researchers used cryo-electron microscopy (cryo-EM) to determine the structure of ClpC with and without MecA. They found that ClpC forms large two-helical assemblies that associate via head-to-head contacts between coiled-coil middle domains (MDs). In its resting state, ClpC is repressed by these MDs, which sequester ClpC molecules in an inactive state.

     When MecA binds to the MD interaction sites, it converts the resting state to an active planar ring structure, allowing ClpC to unfold substrates and degrade them. However, loss of ClpC repression in MD mutants causes constitutive activation and severe cellular toxicity. This suggests that the repressing mode of MDs is a general mechanism for controlling bacterial AAA+ chaperone systems.

     The researchers also found that mutating a key regulatory MD residue of ClpE, another AAA+ chaperone, causes cellular toxicity, indicating that this regulatory mechanism is not unique to ClpC. These findings have implications for the development of antimicrobials that target AAA+ chaperones, which are essential for bacterial virulence and stress resistance. Overall, this study reveals a new regulatory concept executed by coiled-coil MDs to tightly control AAA+ chaperone activity.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand how stem cells remain in a dormant state and how they are reactivated. A recent study in Drosophila, the fruit fly, has shed light on this process by examining the behavior of adult muscle precursors (AMPs), the stem cells responsible for muscle growth and regeneration. The study found that AMPs send out thin filopodia that make contact with neighboring muscles, suggesting that muscles may play a role in AMP reactivation.

     Further investigation revealed that muscles send inductive signals, specifically dIlp6, that activate the Insulin pathway in closely associated AMPs. This activation leads to the activation of Notch, which in turn regulates AMP proliferation via dMyc. The study found that dMyc acts as a positive regulator of AMP reactivation, indicating that the proliferation of muscle stem cells is regulated by the same set of genes in both Drosophila and vertebrates.

     The study's findings suggest that muscles behave as an AMP niche, providing inductive signals that reactivate dormant AMPs and induce their proliferation. The Insulin-Notch-dMyc cascade plays a key role in setting the activated state of AMPs, allowing them to proliferate and provide a source of myoblasts for adult muscle growth and regeneration.

     The study's results have significant implications for our understanding of stem cell biology and muscle regeneration. The discovery of the muscle niche-driven Insulin-Notch-dMyc cascade provides new insights into the mechanisms governing AMP reactivation and proliferation. This knowledge may have important applications in the development of therapies for muscle-related diseases and injuries. The study's findings also highlight the conservation of genetic mechanisms between Drosophila and vertebrates, underscoring the importance of studying model organisms in understanding human biology.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have long been interested in understanding how certain neurons in the brain fire spontaneously at high rates. These neurons, found in the substantia nigra pars reticulata (SNr), play a crucial role in brain circuits by providing tonic inhibition to downstream brain areas. A depolarizing "leak" current is thought to support this firing pattern, but the molecular basis of this current has remained poorly understood.

     To investigate this, researchers used single-cell RNA sequencing to determine the transcriptome of individual mouse SNr neurons. They discovered that SNr neurons express the sodium leak channel, NALCN, and that SNr neurons lacking NALCN have impaired spontaneous firing. The researchers also found that NALCN is involved in the modulation of excitability by changes in glycolysis and by activation of muscarinic acetylcholine receptors.

     The study suggests that disruption of NALCN could impair the basal ganglia circuit, which may underlie the severe motor deficits in humans carrying mutations in NALCN. The researchers identified NALCN as the highest expressed nonselective cation channel (NSCC) in SNr neurons, making it a prime candidate for the channel carrying the depolarizing leak current. They found that SNr neurons require NALCN for appropriate tonic firing, and that mutations in NALCN could contribute to basal ganglia-related motor disorders.

     The study also found that SNr neurons express Unc80, Unc79, and Fam155a, three genes whose protein products are known to interact with NALCN. These findings suggest that NALCN plays a critical role in the function of SNr neurons and that disruption of NALCN could have significant consequences for brain function. Further research is needed to fully understand the role of NALCN in the basal ganglia circuit and its potential contribution to motor disorders.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have long relied on antibiotics derived from a limited number of culturable microorganisms to combat bacterial infections. However, the rise of antibiotic resistance has made these compounds increasingly ineffective, while the vast majority of microbial diversity remains inaccessible for cultivation and exploration. To address this issue, scientists have developed a new method for cultivating and prioritizing antibiotic producers from natural microbial communities.

     The new approach involves a highly parallelized droplet-based microscale cultivation system, which is directly coupled to a high-throughput screening for antimicrobial activity prior to strain isolation. This system allows for the cultivation of single cells in a miniaturized droplet volume of 200 picoliters, resulting in the cultivation of yet uncultured species and a significantly higher bacterial diversity than standard agar plate cultivation.

     The droplet-based system provides a genotype-phenotype linkage for secreted products, meaning that compounds stay confined with the cell from which they originated. This allows for the storage of droplets in bulk, enabling the generation and incubation of large droplet populations under controlled aerobic conditions. A high-throughput screening for antimicrobial products is also implemented using bacterial reporter strains in droplets.

     The combination of bacterial strain discovery during the in-droplet cultivation phase with subsequent selection of the most auspicious candidates for natural product biosynthesis through a highly robust reporter assay streamlines efforts towards new scaffold structures for the development of antibiotics. The practical relevance of the cultivation approach is increased by combining it with a screening step to detect and select droplets containing environmental cells producing compounds with antibiotic activity.

     The researchers demonstrated the effectiveness of this approach by culturing a complex bacterial community derived from a soil sample in a growth substrate designed accordingly and comparing the cultivation outcome to classical agar plate cultivation for the same sample. For one of the isolates that showed antimicrobial activity in the droplet screening, the secreted metabolites were identified and characterized. This innovative microscale cultivation strategy utilizing a modular droplet-microfluidic platform and mimicking environmental conditions in situ has the potential to revolutionize the discovery of new antibiotics.
summary
     Biomedical researchers conducted a Bayesian analysis to investigate the association between the leukotriene A4 hydrolase (LTA4H) genotype and survival in patients with tuberculous meningitis (TBM). TBM is a severe form of tuberculosis that causes excessive inflammation, leading to high mortality rates. While adjunctive anti-inflammatory corticosteroids have been shown to reduce mortality by 30%, the underlying inflammatory pathophysiology is not fully understood.

     A previous study in Vietnam found that patients with a C/T promoter variant in the LTA4H gene, which encodes an enzyme regulating inflammatory eicosanoids, had improved survival when treated with corticosteroids. In contrast, a study in Indonesia did not find a survival benefit associated with the LTA4H TT genotype. To reconcile these findings, the researchers used Bayesian methods to analyze both studies and found that the LTA4H TT genotype confers a survival benefit that begins early and continues long-term in both populations.

     However, this benefit is nullified in the most severe cases with high early mortality. The researchers suggest that LTA4H genotyping, combined with disease severity assessment, may help target glucocorticoid therapy to patients most likely to benefit from it. They propose that personalized genotype-directed adjunctive glucocorticoid treatment could be warranted, with the drug given only to TT patients.

     The researchers considered two possible explanations for why the LTA4H TT effect was thought to be limited to the least severe patients in both cohorts. Firstly, the Indonesia cohort was skewed towards more severe disease on presentation, which may have overridden the underlying association between LTA4H genotype and survival. Secondly, Indonesia Grade 3 patients may have presented with a greater degree of dysregulated inflammation not revealed by standard metrics of judging disease severity, making corticosteroids less effective.

     However, the researchers concluded that it is more likely that the beneficial effects of dexamethasone for the LTA4H TT genotype were nullified in more severe disease, rather than invoking an unknown genetic phenomenon. This concept has precedent in the idea that disease severity can be too great for outcomes to be influenced by intervention.
summary
     Biomedical research has long sought to understand how growth cones navigate axonal projection in response to guidance cues. A key challenge lies in deciphering how local spatial cues are transduced into protrusive forces that drive growth cone migration. A recent study has made significant progress in this area by investigating the role of shootin1 in netrin-1-induced axon guidance.

     The study found that knockout mice lacking shootin1 display abnormal projection of forebrain commissural axons, a phenotype similar to that of netrin-1 knockout mice. Further investigation revealed that shallow gradients of netrin-1 elicit highly polarized Pak1-mediated phosphorylation of shootin1 within growth cones. This phosphorylation event increases the interaction between shootin1 and the cell adhesion molecule L1-CAM, promoting F-actin-adhesion coupling and the generation of forces necessary for growth cone migration.

     The study demonstrated that the spatially regulated phosphorylation of shootin1 within growth cones is required for axon turning induced by netrin-1 gradients. In other words, the phosphorylation of shootin1 acts as a critical readout for netrin-1 gradients, resulting in a directional mechanoresponse for axon guidance.

     The research also showed that shootin1a is expressed at high levels in developing forebrain commissural axons and that its knockout leads to dysgenesis and misprojection of these axons. Notably, very small spatial differences in netrin-1 concentration elicited highly polarized directional phosphorylation of shootin1a within growth cones.

     Furthermore, the study found that netrin-1-elicited shootin1a phosphorylation promotes direct interaction between shootin1a and L1-CAM, generating traction force for growth cone motility. Disturbance of the spatially regulated shootin1a phosphorylation within growth cones inhibited axon turning, but not axon outgrowth, induced by netrin-1 gradients.

     Overall, the study defines a mechano-effector for netrin-1 signaling and demonstrates that shootin1 phosphorylation is a critical readout for netrin-1 gradients that results in a directional mechanoresponse for axon guidance. The findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying netrin-1-regulated axon guidance and highlight the importance of shootin1 in this process.
Summary
     Scientists have long been fascinated by the unique humming sound produced by hummingbirds in flight. To understand the biophysical origin of this sound, researchers used a combination of advanced measurement techniques to study six freely hovering Anna's hummingbirds. They employed a 2176-microphone array to record the acoustic nearfield of the birds, while simultaneously measuring the three-dimensional aerodynamic forces acting on their wings using a new aerodynamic force platform.

     The researchers developed an idealized acoustic model that integrated the aerodynamic forces with wing kinematics to explain how the hummingbird's hum arises from the oscillating lift and drag forces on each wing. By comparing the acoustic measurements with the model, they found that the characteristic humming timbre and radiated power of the birds' flapping wings originate from the higher harmonics in the aerodynamic forces that support their body weight.

     The study also compared the findings with those of insects, such as mosquitoes, and found that the characteristic humming timbre and radiated power of their flapping wings also arise from the higher harmonics in the aerodynamic forces. However, the researchers noted that allometric deviation, which refers to the scaling of body size with respect to wing size, affects the sound produced by larger birds and elongated flies. Larger birds tend to be quieter, while elongated flies tend to be louder.

     The study provides new insights into the complex bioacoustic behavior of flying animals and sheds light on the biophysical origin of the humming sound produced by hummingbirds. The researchers used a combination of advanced measurement techniques and idealized modeling to explain how the oscillating aerodynamic forces generated by flapping wings contribute to the production of sound. The findings have implications for our understanding of the complex interactions between aerodynamics, wing kinematics, and sound production in flying animals.
Biomedical researchers have long debated the roles of chance, contingency, and necessity in evolution. To address this question, a new study used a combination of ancestral protein reconstruction and continuous evolution technology to investigate the evolution of the B-cell lymphoma-2 (BCL-2) family of proteins. The BCL-2 family plays a crucial role in regulating cell death and is involved in various diseases, including cancer.

The researchers used a novel approach to mutate and select proteins in the BCL-2 family to acquire specific protein-protein interaction (PPI) specificities that occurred during animal evolution. By replicating evolutionary trajectories from multiple ancestral proteins, the study found that contingency, or the unique sequence of historical events, played a significant role in shaping the evolution of BCL-2 family proteins. Over long historical timescales, contingency steadily erased necessity and overwhelmed chance as the primary cause of acquired sequence variation.

The study also found that trajectories launched from phylogenetically distant proteins yielded virtually no common mutations, even under strong and identical selection pressures. This suggests that the patterns of variation in BCL-2 sequences are idiosyncratic products of a particular and unpredictable course of historical events. The researchers propose that present-day BCL-2 family proteins are largely physical anecdotes of their particular histories, reflecting the interaction of accumulated chance events during descent from common ancestors with necessity imposed by physics, chemistry, and natural selection.

The study's findings have significant implications for our understanding of evolution. They suggest that contingency, in addition to selection, drift, and mutation, is a primary factor shaping the outcomes of evolution. The results also indicate that the paths through sequence space leading to historical PPI specificities changed repeatedly during the BCL-2 family's history, even during intervals when the proteins' PPI binding profiles did not evolve. This highlights the complex and unpredictable nature of evolution, where multiple paths to a selected phenotype must be accessible with similar probabilities of being taken for chance to strongly influence the outcomes of adaptive evolution.
Summary of Admixture into and within Sub-Saharan Africa
     A recent genome-wide analysis of 48 sub-Saharan African groups has provided new insights into the structure of genetic diversity and gene-flow in the region. The study used a haplotype-based approach to examine the combination of genetic markers along chromosomes, which indicates shared ancestry and historical connections between different population groups due to admixture.

     The analysis revealed that coastal populations in sub-Saharan Africa experienced an influx of Eurasian haplotypes over the last 7,000 years. Additionally, Eastern and Southern Niger-Congo speaking groups were found to share ancestry with Central West Africans as a result of recent population expansions. Furthermore, most sub-Saharan populations were found to share ancestry with groups from outside their current geographic region as a result of gene-flow within the last 4,000 years.

     The study also identified clear evidence of the movement of Eurasian ancestry back into the continent as a result of admixture over various timescales. This is consistent with previous studies, such as the African Genome Variation Project, which described the influence of Eurasian and hunter-gatherer ancestry on the population genetic history of sub-Saharan Africa.

     At finer scales, the analysis revealed smaller groups and often differentiated closely related populations consistent with self-reported ancestry. The study measured gene-flow between populations and related it to potential historical movements of people into and within sub-Saharan Africa. Admixture is a common feature of human populations across the globe, and genome-wide analyses of African populations are refining previous models of the continent's history and its impact on genetic diversity.

     The findings of this study provide insight into haplotype sharing across different ethno-linguistic groups and the recent movement of alleles into new environments, both of which are relevant to studies of genetic epidemiology. The study's in-depth analysis provides a quantitative measure of the coancestry between groups and identifies the dominant events that have shaped current genetic diversity in sub-Saharan Africa.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand how the mitotic spindle adapts to changing cell dimensions during early animal development. The mitotic spindle is a complex structure composed of microtubules that separates chromosomes during cell division. In Xenopus laevis, a type of frog, the mitotic spindle undergoes significant changes in size as the embryo develops from a few cells to thousands of cells. To investigate this process, scientists created an in vitro system using cytoplasmic extracts from Xenopus embryos that recapitulates the spindle size differences between early and late developmental stages.

     The researchers identified a kinesin-13 protein called kif2a as a key driver of spindle scaling during Xenopus development. Kif2a's microtubule-destabilizing activity is inhibited in early-stage spindles by the transport receptor importin Î±. However, in late-stage spindles, importin Î± partitions to a membrane pool, allowing kif2a's activity to be activated. This ratiometric mechanism controls microtubule destabilization and couples spindle size to cell size.

     By altering spindle size in developing embryos, the researchers found that spindle orientation during metaphase was impaired, but chromosome segregation remained robust. This suggests that spindle size plays a critical role in maintaining spindle orientation in large cells during early Xenopus development. The study's findings are consistent with the gradual change from meiotic to mitotic spindle morphology observed during early mouse development.

     The researchers also found that importin Î±'s interaction with kif2a is influenced by its partitioning to a membrane pool. In early-stage extracts, kif2a retrieves significantly more importin Î± than in late-stage extracts, suggesting that the amount of importin Î± available to interact with kif2a is sufficient to alter spindle size. These observations suggest that changes in gradients and spindle assembly pathways accompany cell size changes and could influence spindle size.

     The study's findings provide new insights into the mechanisms controlling spindle size during vertebrate embryogenesis. By creating an embryo extract system capable of assembling spindles in cytoplasmic extracts at any stage of Xenopus development, the researchers were able to elucidate a mechanism that coordinates spindle size with cell size through differential activation of kif2a. This work has important implications for our understanding of how the physical changes in embryonic cells impact biochemical reactions that alter spindle architecture.
summary
     Biologists have discovered a natural system in bacteria that uses two types of RNA molecules to control the production of purine, a key component of DNA and RNA. This system, called a tandem riboswitch, can sense the presence of two different molecules, guanine and phosphoribosyl pyrophosphate (PRPP), to regulate the expression of genes involved in purine biosynthesis.

     The researchers found that the tandem riboswitch consists of two separate RNA molecules, one that binds to guanine and another that binds to PRPP. When both molecules are present, the riboswitch is activated, allowing the expression of genes necessary for purine production. However, when either molecule is absent, the riboswitch is inhibited, preventing the expression of these genes.

     This system is an example of a Boolean logic gate, which is a fundamental concept in computer science that allows for the processing of multiple inputs to produce a specific output. In this case, the tandem riboswitch acts as an "imply" logic gate, where the presence of guanine implies the presence of PRPP.

     The discovery of this system is significant because it suggests that ancient RNA-based life forms, known as the "RNA World," may have had the ability to regulate complex metabolic processes without the need for protein-based regulatory factors. The researchers propose that PRPP may have been used by these ancient organisms to generate the building blocks of RNA, and that the riboswitches that bind to PRPP may have persisted to the present day.

     The study also highlights the complexity and sophistication of RNA-based regulatory systems, which are capable of sensing multiple inputs and producing specific outputs. The researchers suggest that additional variations of two-input Boolean logic gates may exist in modern cells, waiting to be discovered. The discovery of these systems has implications for our understanding of the origins of life and the evolution of complex biological processes.
Biomedical researchers have long understood that the number of neurotransmitter-filled vesicles released into the synaptic cleft with each action potential determines the reliability of synaptic transmission. However, the source of variability in this fundamental property has remained unclear. A prevailing view held that the release of a single vesicle (univesicular release, UVR) or multiple vesicles (multivesicular release, MVR) reflected variability in vesicle release probability. This notion was supported by the calcium-dependence of release mode.

     Recent studies using mouse brain slices have challenged this view, demonstrating that the number of vesicles released is regulated by the size of the readily-releasable pool (RRP), which is upstream of vesicle release probability. The RRP is a functional measure of the number of release-competent vesicles per active zone, under the control of synapsins. These findings suggest that protein kinase A (PKA) and its vesicle-associated target, synapsin, dynamically control release site occupancy to dictate the number of vesicles released without altering release probability.

     Multivesicular release is a widespread phenomenon in the rodent central nervous system and appears to be the dominant release mode in human cortex. However, some synapses are limited to the release of a single vesicle per active zone, termed univesicular release. Understanding the mechanisms that determine whether an active zone supports UVR or MVR is crucial to understanding synaptic diversity and developing unifying models of presynaptic function.

     The number of vesicles released from an active zone is the product of vesicle release probability (Pr) and the number of vesicles available for release. The RRP constrains the number of vesicles released and is under the control of synapsins. This fundamental synaptic parameter has not been explored previously, leading to an exclusive focus on Pr as the principal regulator of UVR and MVR. However, some high Pr synapses display exclusively UVR, while some low Pr synapses can exhibit MVR, illustrating that Pr alone is insufficient to dictate vesicle release mode.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand how the human immunodeficiency virus type 1 (HIV-1) selectively packages its genetic material, known as RNA, into new virus particles. A key component of this process is the Gag protein, which assembles on the surface of the cell membrane and binds to the viral RNA. However, the exact mechanism by which Gag selects and packages the HIV-1 genome has remained unclear.

     In a recent study, scientists used a simplified system consisting of purified RNAs, recombinant Gag protein, and giant unilamellar vesicles (GUVs) to reconstitute the selective packaging of the HIV-1 genome. The researchers found that the Gag protein is able to selectively package the 5' untranslated region (UTR) of the HIV-1 genome in the presence of excess competitor RNA. This selective packaging is dependent on the ability of Gag to assemble into multimers on the surface of the GUVs.

     The study also showed that mutations in the CA-CTD domain of the Gag protein, which affect its ability to self-assemble on the membrane, abolish the selective packaging of the HIV-1 genome. Furthermore, the researchers found that the association of Gag with transfer RNA (tRNA) inhibits the assembly of virus particles that do not contain the HIV-1 genome.

     The results of this study provide new insights into the mechanism of HIV-1 genome selection and packaging. The researchers propose a model in which the NC domain of the Gag protein is necessary but not sufficient for the selective packaging of the HIV-1 genome. According to this model, a viral RNA with multiple high-affinity binding sites for Gag can recruit several Gag molecules to the membrane, allowing for the selective packaging of the HIV-1 genome.

     These findings have important implications for our understanding of HIV-1 biology and may inform the development of new strategies for preventing the spread of the virus. The study highlights the importance of membrane-localized Gag assembly events in HIV-1 genome selection and packaging, and provides a new framework for understanding the complex interactions between Gag and the HIV-1 genome.
